We all moved in the one\r\nlittle, dull world, but they were not only in their world, they were of\r\nit; I was not. Their daily tasks and their little pleasures provided\r\nsufficient oil for the lamp of their existence--mine demanded more than\r\nPossum Gully could supply. They were totally ignorant of the outside\r\nworld. Patti, Melba, Irving, Terry, Kipling, Caine, Corelli, and even\r\nthe name of Gladstone, were only names to them. Whether they were\r\nislands or racehorses they knew not and cared not. With me it was\r\ndifferent. Where I obtained my information, unless it was born in me, I\r\ndo not know. We took none but the local paper regularly, I saw few\r\nbooks, had the pleasure of conversing with an educated person from the\r\nhigher walks of life about once in a twelvemonth, yet I knew of every\r\ncelebrity in literature, art, music, and drama; their world was my\r\nworld, and in fancy I lived with them. My parents discouraged me in that\r\nspecies of foolishness. They had been fond of literature and the higher\r\narts, but now, having no use for them, had lost interest therein.\r\n\r\nI was discontented and restless, and longed unendurably to be out in the\r\nstream of life. "Action! Action! Give me action!" was my cry. My mother\r\ndid her best with me according to her lights. She energetically preached\r\nat me. All the old saws and homilies were brought into requisition, but\r\nwithout avail. It was like using common nostrums on a disease which\r\ncould be treated by none but a special physician.\r\n\r\nI was treated to a great deal of harping on that tiresome old string,\r\n"Whatsoever your hand findeth to do, do it with all your might." It was\r\ndaily dinned into my cars that the little things of life were the\r\nnoblest, and that all the great people I mooned about said the same. I\r\nusually retorted to the effect that I was well aware that it was noble,\r\nand that I could write as good an essay on it as any philosopher. It was\r\nall very well for great people to point out the greatness of the little,\r\nempty, humdrum life. Why didn\'t they adopt it themselves?\r\n\r\n    "The toad beneath the harrow knows\r\n     Exactly where each tooth-point goes.\r\n     The butterfly upon the road\r\n     Preaches contentment to the toad."\r\n\r\nI wasn\'t anxious to patronize the dull kind of tame nobility of the\r\ntoad; I longed for a few of the triumphs of the butterfly, decried\r\nthough they are as hollow bubbles. I desired life while young enough to\r\nlive, and quoted as my motto:\r\n\r\n    "Though the pitcher that goes to the sparkling rill\r\n     Too oft gets broken at last,\r\n     There are scores of others its place to fill\r\n     When its earth to the earth is cast.\r\n     Keep that pitcher at home, let it never roam,\r\n     But lie like a useless clod;\r\n     Yet sooner or later the hour will come\r\n     When its chips are thrown to the sod.\r\n\r\n    "Is it wise, then, say, in the waning day,\r\n     When the vessel is crack\'d and old,\r\n     To cherish the battered potter\'s clay\r\n     As though it were virgin gold?\r\n     Take care of yourself, dull, boorish elf,\r\n     Though prudent and sage you seem;\r\n     Your pitcher will break on the musty shelf,\r\n     And mine by the dazzling stream."\r\n\r\nI had sense sufficient to see the uselessness of attempting to be other\r\nthan I was. In these days of fierce competition there was no chance for\r\nme--opportunity, not talent, was the main requisite. Fate had thought\r\nfit to deny me even one advantage or opportunity, thus I was helpless. I\r\nset to work to cut my coat according to my cloth. I manfully endeavoured\r\nto squeeze my spirit into "that state of life into which it has pleased\r\nGod to call me". I crushed, compressed, and bruised, but as fast as I\r\nmanaged it on one side it burst out on another, and defied me to cram it\r\ninto the narrow box of Possum Gully.\r\n\r\n    "The restless throbbings and burnings\r\n       That hope unsatisfied brings,\r\n     The weary longings and yearnings\r\n       For the mystical better things,\r\n     Are the sands on which is reflected\r\n       The pitiless moving lake,\r\n     Where the wanderer falls dejected,\r\n       By a thirst he never can slake."\r\n\r\nIn a vain endeavour to slake that cruel thirst my soul groped in strange\r\ndark places. It went out in quest of a God, and finding one not, grew\r\nweary.\r\n\r\nBy the unknown way that the atmosphere of the higher life penetrated to\r\nme, so came a knowledge of the sin and sorrow abroad in the world--the\r\ncry of the millions oppressed, downtrodden, God-forsaken! The wheels of\r\nsocial mechanism needed readjusting--things were awry. Oh, that I might\r\nfind a cure and give it to my fellows! I dizzied my brain with the\r\nproblem; I was too much for myself. A man with these notions is a curse\r\nto himself, but a woman--pity help a woman of that description! She is\r\nnot merely a creature out of her sphere, she is a creature without a\r\nsphere--a lonely being!\r\n\r\nRecognizing this, I turned and cursed God for casting upon me a burden\r\ngreater than I could bear--cursed Him bitterly, and from within came a\r\nwhisper that there was nothing there to curse. There was no God. I was\r\nan unbeliever. It was not that I sought after or desired atheism. I\r\nlonged to be a Christian, and fought against unbelief. I asked the\r\nChristians around me for help. Unsophisticated fool! I might as well\r\nhave announced that I was a harlot. My respectability vanished in one\r\nslap. Some said it was impossible to disbelieve in the existence of a\r\nGod: I was only doing it for notoriety, and they washed their hands of\r\nme at once.\r\n\r\nNot believe in God! I was mad!\r\n\r\nIf there really was a God, would they kindly tell me how to find Him?\r\n\r\nPray! pray!\r\n\r\nI prayed, often and ardently, but ever came that heart-stilling whisper\r\nthat there was nothing to pray to.\r\n\r\nAh, the bitter, hopeless heart-hunger of godlessness none but an atheist\r\ncan understand! Nothing to live for in life--no hope beyond the grave.\r\nIt plunged me into fits of profound melancholy.\r\n\r\nHad my father occupied one of the fat positions of the land, no doubt as\r\nhis daughter my life would have been so full of pleasant occupation and\r\npleasure that I would not have developed the spirit which torments me\r\nnow. Or had I a friend--one who knew, who had suffered and understood,\r\none in whom I could lose myself, one on whom I could lean--I might have\r\ngrown a nicer character. But in all the wide world there was not a soul\r\nto hold out a hand to me, and I said bitterly, "There is no good in the\r\nworld." In softer moods I said, "Ah, the tangle of it! Those who have\r\nthe heart to help have not the power, and those who have the power have\r\nnot the heart."\r\n\r\nBad, like a too-strong opponent in a game of chess, is ever at the elbow\r\nof good to checkmate it like a weakly managed king.\r\n\r\nI am sadly lacking in self-reliance. I needed some one to help me over\r\nthe rough spots in life, and finding them not, at the age of sixteen I\r\nwas as rank a cynic and infidel as could be found in three days\' march.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER EIGHT\r\n\r\nPossum Gully Left Behind. Hurrah! Hurrah!\r\n\r\n\r\nIf a Sydney man has friends residing at Goulburn, he says they are up\r\nthe country. If a Goulburn man has friends at Yass, he says they are up\r\nthe country. If a Yass man has friends at Young, he says they are up the\r\ncountry, and so on. Caddagat is "up the country".\r\n\r\nBound thither on the second Wednesday in August 1896, I bought a ticket\r\nat the Goulburn railway station, and at some time about 1 a.m. took my\r\nseat in a second class carriage of the mail-train on its way to\r\nMelbourne. I had three or four hours to travel in this train when I\r\nwould have to change to a branch line for two hours longer. I was the\r\nonly one from Goulburn in that carriage; all the other passengers had\r\nbeen in some time and were asleep. One or two opened their eyes\r\nstrugglingly, stared glumly at the intruder, and then went to sleep\r\nagain. The motion of the train was a joy to me, and sleep never entered\r\nmy head. I stood up, and pressing my forehead to the cold window-pane,\r\nvainly attempted, through the inky blackness of the foggy night, to\r\ndiscern the objects which flew by.\r\n\r\nI was too full of pleasant anticipation of what was ahead of me to think\r\nof those I had left behind. I did not regret leaving Possum Gully. Quite\r\nthe reverse; I felt inclined to wave my arms and yell for joy at being\r\nfreed from it. Home! God forbid that my experiences at Possum Gully\r\nshould form the only food for my reminiscences of home. I had\r\npractically grown up there, but my heart refused absolutely to regard it\r\nas home. I hated it then, I hate it now, with its narrowing, stagnant\r\nmonotony. It has and had not provided me with one solitary fond\r\nremembrance--only with dreary, wing-clipping, mind-starving\r\nrecollections. No, no; I was not leaving home behind, I was flying\r\nhomeward now. Home, home to Caddagat, home to ferny gullies, to the\r\nsweet sad rush of many mountain waters, to the majesty of rugged\r\nBorgongs; home to dear old grannie, and uncle and aunt, to books, to\r\nmusic; refinement, company, pleasure, and the dear old homestead I love\r\nso well.\r\n\r\nAll in good time I arrived at the end of my train journey, and was taken\r\nin charge by a big red-bearded man, who informed me he was the driver of\r\nthe mail-coach, and had received a letter from Mrs Bossier instructing\r\nhim to take care of me. He informed me also that he was glad to do what\r\nhe termed "that same", and I would be as safe under his care as I would\r\nbe in God\'s pocket.\r\n\r\nMy twenty-six miles\' coach drive was neither pleasant nor eventful. I\r\nwas the only passenger, and so had my choice of seats. The weather being\r\ncold and wet, I preferred being inside the box and curled myself up on\r\nthe seat, to be interrupted every two or three miles by the good-natured\r\ndriver inquiring if I was "all serene".\r\n\r\nAt the Halfway House, where a change of the team of five horses was\r\naffected, I had a meal and a warm, and so tuned myself up for the\r\nremainder of the way. It got colder as we went on, and at 2.30 p.m. I\r\nwas not at all sorry to see the iron roofs of Gool-Gool township\r\ndisclosing to my view. We first went to the post office, where the\r\nmail-bags were delivered, and then returned and pulled rein in front of\r\nthe Woolpack Hotel. A tall young gentleman in a mackintosh and cap, who\r\nhad been standing on the veranda, stepped out on the street as the coach\r\nstopped, and lifting his cap and thrusting his head into the coach,\r\ninquired, "Which is Miss Melvyn?"\r\n\r\nSeeing I was the only occupant, he laughed the pleasantest of laughs,\r\ndisclosing two wide rows of perfect teeth, and turning to the driver,\r\nsaid, "Is that your only passenger? I suppose it is Miss Melvyn?"\r\n\r\n"As I wasn\'t present at her birth, I can\'t swear, but I believe her to\r\nbe that same, as sure as eggs is eggs," he replied.\r\n\r\nMy identity being thus established, the young gentleman with the\r\ngreatest of courtesy assisted me to alight, ordered the hotel groom to\r\nstow my luggage in the Caddagat buggy, and harness the horses with all\r\nexpedition. He then conducted me to the private parlour, where a\r\nfriendly little barmaid had some refreshments on a tray awaiting me, and\r\nwhile warming my feet preparatory to eating I read the letter he had\r\ngiven me, which was addressed in my grandmother\'s handwriting. In it she\r\ntold me that she and my aunt were only just recovering from bad colds,\r\nand on account of the inclemency of the weather thought it unwise to\r\ncome to town to meet me; but Frank Hawden, the jackeroo would take every\r\ncare of me, settle the hotel bill, and tip the coach-driver. Caddagat\r\nwas twenty-four miles distant from Gool-Gool, and the latter part of the\r\nroad was very hilly. It was already past three o\'clock, and, being\r\nrainy, the short winter afternoon would dose in earlier; so I swallowed\r\nmy tea and cake with all expedition, so as not to delay Mr Hawden, who\r\nwas waiting to assist me into the buggy, where the groom was in charge\r\nof the horses in the yard. He struck up a conversation with me\r\nimmediately.\r\n\r\n"Seeing your name on yer bags, an\' knowin\' you was belonging to the\r\nBossiers, I ask if yer might be a daughter of Dick Melvyn, of\r\nBruggabrong, out by Timlinbilly."\r\n\r\n"Yes, I am."\r\n\r\n"Well, miss, please remember me most kindly to yer pa; he was a good\r\nboss was Dick Melvyn. I hope he\'s doin\' well. I\'m Billy Haizelip,\r\nbrother to Mary and Jane. You remember Jane, I s\'pose, miss?"\r\n\r\nI hadn\'t time to say more than promise to send his remembrances to my\r\nfather, for Mr Hawden, saying we would be in the dark, had whipped his\r\nhorses and was bowling off at a great pace, in less than two minutes\r\ncovering a rise which put Gool-Gool out of sight. It was raining a\r\nlittle, so I held over us the big umbrella, which grannie had sent,\r\nwhile we discussed the weather, to the effect that rain was badly needed\r\nand was a great novelty nowadays, and it was to be hoped it would\r\ncontinue. There had been but little, but the soil here away was of that\r\nrich loamy description which little water turns to mud. It clogged the\r\nwheels and loaded the break-blocks; and the near side horse had a nasty\r\nway of throwing his front feet, so that he deposited soft red lumps of\r\nmud in our laps at every step. But, despite these trifling drawbacks, it\r\nwas delightful to be drawn without effort by a pair of fat horses in\r\nsplendid harness. It was a great contrast to our poor skinny old horse\r\nat home, crawling along in much-broken harness, clumsily and much mended\r\nwith string and bits of hide.\r\n\r\nMr Hawden was not at all averse to talking. After emptying our tongues\r\nof the weather, there was silence for some time, which he broke with,\r\n"So you are Mrs Bossier\'s grand-daughter, are you?"\r\n\r\n"Not remembering my birth, I can\'t swear; but I believe myself to be\r\nthat same, as sure as eggs is eggs," I replied.\r\n\r\nHe laughed. "Very good imitation of the coach-driver. But Mrs Bossier\'s\r\ngrand-daughter! Well, I should smile!"\r\n\r\n"What at?"\r\n\r\n"Your being Mrs Bossier\'s grand-daughter."\r\n\r\n"I fear, Mr Hawden, there is a suspicion reverse of complimentary in\r\nyour remark."\r\n\r\n"Well, I should smile! Would you like to have my opinion of you?"\r\n\r\n"Nothing would please me more. I would value your opinion above all\r\nthings, and I\'m sure--I feel certain--that you have formed a true\r\nestimate of me."\r\n\r\nAt any other time his conceit would have brought upon himself a fine\r\nsnubbing, but today I was in high feather, and accordingly very\r\npleasant, and resolved to amuse myself by drawing him out.\r\n\r\n"Well, you are not a bit like Mrs Bossier or Mrs Bell; they are both so\r\ngood-looking," he continued.\r\n\r\n"Indeed!"\r\n\r\n"I was disappointed when I saw you had no pretensions to prettiness, as\r\nthere\'s not a girl up these parts worth wasting a man\'s affections on,\r\nand I was building great hopes on you. But I\'m a great admirer of\r\nbeauty," he twaddled.\r\n\r\n"I am very sorry for you, Mr Hawden. I\'m sure it would take quite a\r\nparagon to be worthy of such affection as I\'m sure yours would be," I\r\nreplied sympathetically.\r\n\r\n"Never mind. Don\'t worry about it. You\'re not a bad sort, and think a\r\nfellow could have great fun with you."\r\n\r\n"I\'m sure, Mr Hawden, you do me too much honour. It quite exhilarates me\r\nto think that I meet with your approval in the smallest degree," I\r\nreplied with the utmost deference. "You are so gentlemanly and nice that\r\nI was alarmed at first lest you might despise me altogether."\r\n\r\n"No fear. You needn\'t be afraid of me; I\'m not a bad sort of fellow," he\r\nreplied with the greatest encouragement.\r\n\r\nBy his accent and innocent style I detected he was not a colonial, so I\r\ngot him to relate his history. He was an Englishman by birth, but had\r\nbeen to America, Spain, New Zealand, Tasmania, etc.; by his own make out\r\nhad ever been a man of note, and had played Old Harry everywhere.\r\n\r\nI allowed him to gabble away full tilt for an hour on this subject,\r\nunconscious that I had taken the measure of him, and was grinning\r\nbroadly to myself. Then I diverted him by inquiring how long since the\r\nwire fence on our right had been put up. It bore evidence of recent\r\nerection, and had replaced an old cockatoo fence which I remembered in\r\nmy childhood.\r\n\r\n"Fine fence, is it not? Eight wires, a top rail, and very stout posts.\r\nHarry Beecham had that put up by contract this year. Twelve miles of it.\r\nIt cost him a lot: couldn\'t get any very low tenders, the ground being\r\nso hard on account of the drought. Those trees are Five-Bob Downs--see,\r\naway over against the range. But I suppose you know the places better\r\nthan I do."\r\n\r\nWe were now within an hour of our destination. How familiar were many\r\nlandmarks to me, although I had not seen them since I was eight years\r\nold.\r\n\r\nA river ran on our right, occasionally a glimmer of its noisy waters\r\nvisible through the shrubbery which profusely lined its banks. The short\r\nevening was drawing to a close. The white mists brought by the rain were\r\ncrawling slowly down the hills, and settling in the hollows of the\r\nranges on our left. A V-shaped rift in them, known as Pheasant Gap, came\r\ninto view. Mr Hawden said it was well named, as it swarmed with\r\nlyrebirds. Night was falling. The skreel of a hundred curlews arose from\r\nthe gullies--how I love their lonely wail!--and it was quite dark when\r\nwe pulled up before the front gate of Caddagat.\r\n\r\nA score of dogs rushed yelping to meet us, the front door was thrown\r\nopen, lights and voices came streaming out.\r\n\r\nI alighted from the buggy feeling rather nervous. I was a pauper with a\r\nbad character. How would my grandmother receive me? Dear old soul, I had\r\nnothing to fear. She folded me in a great warm-hearted hug, saying,\r\n"Dear me, child, your face is cold. I\'m glad you\'ve come. It has been a\r\nterrible day, but we\'re glad to have the rain. You must be frozen. Get\r\nin to the fire, child, as fast as you can. Get in to the fire, get in to\r\nthe fire. I hope you forgive me for not going to meet you." And there\r\nwas my mother\'s only sister, my tall graceful aunt, standing beside her,\r\ngiving me a kiss and cordial hand-clasp, and saying, "Welcome, Sybylla.\r\nWe will be glad to have a young person to brighten up the old home once\r\nmore. I am sorry I was too unwell to meet you. You must be frozen; come\r\nto the fire."\r\n\r\n\r\nMy aunt always spoke very little and very quietly, but there was\r\nsomething in her high-bred style which went right home.\r\n\r\nI could scarcely believe that they were addressing me. Surely they were\r\nmaking a mistake. This reception was meant for some grand relative\r\nhonouring them with a visit, and not for the ugly, useless, bad little\r\npauper come to live upon their bounty.\r\n\r\nTheir welcome did more than all the sermons I had ever heard put\r\ntogether towards thawing a little of the pitiless cynicism which\r\nencrusted my heart.\r\n\r\n"Take the child inside, Helen, as fast as you can," said grannie, "while\r\nI see that the boy attends to the horses. The plaguey fellow can\'t be\r\ntrusted any further than the length of his nose. I told him to tie up\r\nthese dogs, and here they are yelp-yelping fit to deafen a person."\r\n\r\nI left my wet umbrella on the veranda, and aunt Helen led me into the\r\ndining-room, where a spruce maid was making a pleasant clatter in laying\r\nthe table. Caddagat was a very old style of house, and all the front\r\nrooms opened onto the veranda without any such preliminary as a hall,\r\ntherefore it was necessary to pass through the dining-room to my\r\nbedroom, which was a skillion at the back. While auntie paused for a\r\nmoment to give some orders to the maid, I noticed the heavy silver\r\nserviette rings I remembered so well, and the old-fashioned\r\ndinner-plates, and the big fire roaring in the broad white fireplace;\r\nbut more than all, the beautiful pictures on the walls and a table in a\r\ncorner strewn with papers, magazines, and several very new-looking\r\nbooks. On the back of one of these I saw "Corelli", and on\r\nanother--great joy!--was _Trilby_. From the adjoining apartment, which\r\nwas the drawing-room, came the sweet full tones of a beautiful piano.\r\nHere were three things for which I had been starving. An impulse to\r\nrevel in them immediately seized me. I felt like clearing the table at a\r\nbound, seizing and beginning to read both books, and rushing in to the\r\npiano and beginning to play upon it there and then, and examine the\r\npictures--all three things at once. Fortunately for the reputation of my\r\nsanity, however, aunt Helen had by this time conducted me to a pretty\r\nlittle bedroom, and saying it was to be mine, helped me to doff my cape\r\nand hat.\r\n\r\nWhile warming my fingers at the fire my eyes were arrested by a\r\nbeautiful portrait hanging above the mantelpiece. It represented a\r\nlovely girl in the prime of youth and beauty, and attired in floating\r\nwhite dinner draperies.\r\n\r\n"Oh, aunt Helen! isn\'t she lovely? It\'s you, isn\'t it?"\r\n\r\n"No. Do you not recognize it as your mother? It was taken just before\r\nher marriage. I must leave you now, but come out as soon as you arrange\r\nyourself--your grandmother will be anxious to see you."\r\n\r\nWhen aunt Helen left me I plastered my hair down in an instant without\r\neven a glance in the mirror. I took not a particle of interest in my\r\nattire, and would go about dressed anyhow. This was one symptom which\r\ninclined my mother to the belief of my possible insanity, as to most\r\nyoung girls dress is a great delight. I had tried once or twice to make\r\nmyself look nice by dressing prettily, but, by my own judgment,\r\nconsidering I looked as ugly as ever, I had given it up as a bad job.\r\n\r\nThe time which I should have spent in arranging my toilet passed in\r\ngazing at my mother\'s portrait. It was one of the loveliest faces\r\nimaginable. The features may not have been perfect according to rule of\r\nthumb, but the expression was simply angelic--sweet, winning, gentle,\r\nand happy. I turned from the contemplation of it to another\r\nphotograph--one of my father--in a silver frame on the dressing-table.\r\nThis, too, was a fine countenance, possessed of well-cut features and\r\nrefined expression. This was the prince who had won Lucy Bossier from\r\nher home. I looked around my pretty bedroom--it had been my mother\'s in\r\nthe days of her maidenhood. In an exclusive city boarding-school, and\r\namid the pleasant surroundings of this home, her youth had been spent.\r\n\r\nI thought of a man and his wife at Possum Gully. The man was blear-eyed,\r\ndisreputable in appearance, and failed to fulfil his duties as a father\r\nand a citizen. The woman was work-roughened and temper-soured by endless\r\ncare and an unavailing struggle against poverty. Could that pair\r\npossibly be identical with this?\r\n\r\nThis was life as proved by my parents! What right had I to expect any\r\nbetter yield from it? I shut my eyes and shuddered at the possibilities\r\nand probabilities of my future. It was for this that my mother had\r\nyielded up her youth, freedom, strength; for this she had sacrificed the\r\ngreatest possession of woman.\r\n\r\nHere I made my way to the dining-room, where grannie was waiting for me\r\nand gave me another hug.\r\n\r\n"Come here, child, and sit beside me near the fire; but first let me\r\nhave a look at you," and she held me at arm\'s length.\r\n\r\n"Dear, oh, dear, what a little thing you are, and not a bit like any of\r\nyour relations! I am glad your skin is so nice and clear; all my\r\nchildren had beautiful complexions. Goodness me, I never saw such hair!\r\nA plait thicker than my arm and almost to your knees! It is that\r\nbeautiful bright brown like your aunt\'s. Your mother\'s was flaxen. I\r\nmust see your hair loose when you are going to bed. There is nothing I\r\nadmire so much as a beautiful head of hair."\r\n\r\nThe maid announced that dinner was ready, grannie vigorously rang a\r\nlittle bell, aunt Helen, a lady, and a gentleman appeared from the\r\ndrawing-room, and Mr Hawden came in from the back. I discovered that the\r\nlady and gentleman were a neighbouring squatter and a new governess he\r\nwas taking home. Grannie, seeing them pass that afternoon in the rain,\r\nhad gone out and prevailed upon them to spend the night at Caddagat.\r\n\r\nMr Hawden took no notice of me now, but showed off to the others for my\r\nbenefit. After dinner we had music and singing in the drawing-room. I\r\nwas enjoying it immensely, but grannie thought I had better go to bed,\r\nas I had been travelling since about midnight last night. I was neither\r\ntired nor sleepy, but knew it useless to protest, so bade every one good\r\nnight and marched off. Mr Hawden acknowledged my salute with great airs\r\nand stiffness, and aunt Helen whispered that she would come and see me\r\nby and by, if I was awake.\r\n\r\nGrannie escorted me to my room, and examined my hair. I shook it out for\r\nher inspection. It met with her approval in every way. She pronounced it\r\nbeautifully fine, silky, and wavy, and the most wonderful head of hair\r\nshe had seen out of a picture.\r\n\r\nA noise arose somewhere out in the back premises. Grannie went out to\r\nascertain the cause of it and did not return to me, so I extinguished my\r\nlamp and sat thinking in the glow of the firelight.\r\n\r\nFor the first time my thoughts reverted to my leave-taking from home. My\r\nfather had kissed me with no more warmth than if I had been leaving for\r\na day only; my mother had kissed me very coldly, saying shortly, "It is\r\nto be hoped, Sybylla, that your behaviour to your grandmother will be an\r\nimprovement upon what it has ever been to me." Gertie was the only one\r\nwho had felt any sorrow at parting with me, and I knew that she was of\r\nsuch a disposition that I would be forgotten in a day or two. They would\r\nnever miss me, for I had no place in their affections. True, I was an\r\nundutiful child, and deserved none. I possessed no qualities that would\r\nwin either their pride or love, but my heart cried out in love for them.\r\n\r\nWould Gertie miss me tonight, as I would have missed her had our\r\npositions been reversed? Not she. Would my absence from the noisy\r\ntea-table cause a blank? I feared not.\r\n\r\nI thought of poor mother left toiling at home, and my heart grew heavy;\r\nI failed to remember my father\'s faults, but thought of his great\r\npatience with me in the years agone, and all my old-time love for him\r\nrenewed itself. Why, oh, why, would they not love me a little in return!\r\nCertainly I had never striven to be lovable. But see the love some have\r\nlavished upon them without striving for it! Why was I ugly and nasty and\r\nmiserable and useless--without a place in the world?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER NINE\r\n\r\nAunt Helen\'s Recipe\r\n\r\n\r\n"Dear me, Sybylla, not in bed yet, and tears, great big tears! Tell me\r\nwhat is the cause of them."\r\n\r\nIt was aunt Helen\'s voice; she had entered and lit the lamp.\r\n\r\nThere was something beautifully sincere and real about aunt Helen. She\r\nnever fussed over any one or pretended to sympathize just to make out\r\nhow nice she was. She was real, and you felt that no matter what wild or\r\nawful rubbish you talked to her it would never be retailed for any one\'s\r\namusement--and, better than all, she never lectured.\r\n\r\nShe sat down beside me, and I impulsively threw my arms around her neck\r\nand sobbed forth my troubles in a string. How there was no good in the\r\nworld, no use for me there, no one loved me or ever could on account of\r\nmy hideousness.\r\n\r\nShe heard me to the end and then said quietly, "When you are fit to\r\nlisten I will talk to you."\r\n\r\nI controlled myself instantly and waited expectantly. What would she\r\nsay? Surely not that tame old yarn anent this world being merely a place\r\nof probation, wherein we were allowed time to fit ourselves for a\r\nbeautiful world to come. That old tune may be all very well for old\r\ncodgers tottering on the brink of the grave, but to young persons with\r\nyouth and romance and good health surging through their veins, it is\r\nmost boresome. Would she preach that it was flying in the face of\r\nprovidence to moan about my appearance? it being one of the greatest\r\nblessings I had, as it would save me from countless temptations to which\r\npretty girls are born. That was another piece of old croaking of the\r\njob\'s comforter order, of which I was sick unto death, as I am sure\r\nthere is not an ugly person in the world who thinks her lack of beauty a\r\nblessing to her. I need not have feared aunt Helen holding forth in that\r\nstrain. She always said something brave and comforting which made me\r\nashamed of myself and my selfish conceited egotism.\r\n\r\n"I understand you, Sybylla," she said slowly and distinctly, "but you\r\nmust not be a coward. There is any amount of love and good in the world,\r\nbut you must search for it. Being misunderstood is one of the trials we\r\nall must bear. I think that even the most common-minded person in the\r\nland has inner thoughts and feelings which no one can share with him,\r\nand the higher one\'s organization the more one must suffer in that\r\nrespect. I am acquainted with a great number of young girls, some of\r\nthem good and true, but you have a character containing more than any\r\nthree of them put together. With this power, if properly managed, you\r\ncan gain the almost universal love of your fellows. But you are wild and\r\nwayward, you must curb and strain your spirit and bring it into\r\nsubjection, else you will be worse than a person with the emptiest of\r\ncharacters. You will find that plain looks will not prevent you from\r\ngaining the _friendship_ love of your fellows--the only real love there\r\nis. As for the hot fleeting passion of the man for the maid, which is\r\nwrongfully designated love, I will not tell you not to think of it,\r\nknowing that it is human nature to demand it when arriving at a certain\r\nage; but take this comfort: it as frequently passes by on the other side\r\nof those with well-chiselled features as those with faces of plainer\r\nmould."\r\n\r\nShe turned her face away, sighed, and forgetful of my presence lapsed\r\ninto silence. I knew she was thinking of herself.\r\n\r\nLove, not _friendship_ love, for anyone knowing her must give her love\r\nand respect, but the other sort of love had passed her by.\r\n\r\nTwelve years before I went to Caddagat, when Helen Bossier had been\r\neighteen and one of the most beautiful and lovable girls in Australia,\r\nthere had come to Caddagat on a visit a dashing colonel of the name of\r\nBell, in the enjoyment of a most extended furlough for the benefit of\r\nhis health. He married aunt Helen and took her to some part of America\r\nwhere his regiment was stationed. I have heard them say she worshipped\r\nColonel Bell, but in less than a twelvemonth he tired of his lovely\r\nbride, and becoming enamoured of another woman, he tried to obtain a\r\ndivorce. On account of his wife\'s spotless character he was unable to do\r\nthis; he therefore deserted her and openly lived with the other woman as\r\nhis mistress. This forced aunt Helen to return to Caddagat, and her\r\nmother had induced her to sue for a judicial separation, which was\r\neasily obtained.\r\n\r\nWhen a woman is separated from her husband it is the religion of the\r\nworld at large to cast the whole blame on the wife. By reason of her\r\nyouth and purity Mrs Bell had not as much to suffer in this way as some\r\nothers. But, comparatively speaking, her life was wrecked. She had been\r\nhumiliated and outraged in the cruellest way by the man whom she loved\r\nand trusted. He had turned her adrift, neither a wife, widow, nor maid,\r\nand here she was, one of the most estimably lovable and noble women I\r\nhave ever met.\r\n\r\n"Come, Sybylla," she said, starting up brightly, "I have a plan--will\r\nyou agree to it? Come and take one good long look at yourself in the\r\nglass, then I will turn it to the wall, and you must promise me that for\r\nthree or four weeks you will not look in a mirror. I will put as many as\r\nI can out of your way, and you must avoid the remainder. During this\r\ntime I will take you in hand, and you must follow my directions\r\nimplicitly. Will you agree? You will be surprised what a nice-looking\r\nlittle girl I will make of you."\r\n\r\nOf course I agreed. I took a long and critical survey of myself in the\r\nglass. There was reflected a pair of hands, red and coarsened with rough\r\nwork, a round face, shiny and swollen with crying, and a small round\r\nfigure enshrouded in masses of hair falling in thick waves to within an\r\ninch or two of the knees. A very ugly spectacle, I thought. Aunt Helen\r\nturned the face of the large mirror flat against the wall, while I\r\nremarked despondently, "you can make me only middling ugly, you must be\r\na magician."\r\n\r\n"Come now, part of my recipe is that you must not think of yourself at\r\nall. I\'ll take you in hand in the morning. I hope you will like your\r\nroom; I have arranged it on purpose to suit you. And now good night, and\r\nhappy dreams."\r\n\r\nI awoke next morning in very fine spirits, and slithering out of my bed\r\nwith alacrity, revelled--literally wallowed--in the appointments of my\r\nroom. My poor old room at Possum Gully was lacking in barest\r\nnecessaries. We could not afford even a wash-hand basin and jug; Gertie,\r\nthe boys, and myself had to perform our morning ablutions in a leaky tin\r\ndish on a stool outside the kitchen door, which on cold frosty mornings\r\nwas a pretty peppery performance: but this room contained everything\r\ndear to the heart of girlhood. A lovely bed, pretty slippers, dainty\r\nwhite China-matting and many soft skins on the floor, and in one corner\r\na most artistic toilet set, and a wash-stand liberally supplied with a\r\ngreat variety of soap--some of it so exquisitely perfumed that I felt\r\ntempted to taste it. There were pretty pictures on the walls, and on a\r\ncommodious dressing-table a big mirror and large hand-glasses, with\r\ntheir faces to the wall at present. Hairpins, fancy combs, ribbons\r\ngalore, and a pretty work-basket greeted my sight, and with delight I\r\nswooped down upon the most excruciatingly lovely little writing-desk. It\r\nwas stuffed full with all kinds of paper of good quality--fancy, all\r\ncolours, sizes, and shapes, plain, foreign note, pens, ink, and a\r\ngenerous supply of stamps. I felt like writing a dozen letters there and\r\nthen, and was on the point of giving way to my inclination, when my\r\nattention was arrested by what I considered the gem of the whole\r\nturn-out. I refer to a nice little bookcase containing copies of all our\r\nAustralian poets, and two or three dozen novels which I had often longed\r\nto read. I read the first chapters of four of them, and then lost myself\r\nin Gordon, and sat on my dressing-table in my nightgown, regardless of\r\ncold, until brought to my senses by the breakfast-bell. I made great\r\npace, scrambled into my clothes helter-skelter, and appeared at table\r\nwhen the others had been seated and unfolded their serviettes.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen\'s treatment for making me presentable was the wearing of\r\ngloves and a shady hat every time I went outside; and she insisted upon\r\nme spending a proper time over my toilet, and would not allow me to\r\nencroach upon it with the contents of my bookshelf.\r\n\r\n"Rub off some of your gloomy pessimism and cultivate a little more\r\nhealthy girlish vanity, and you will do very well," she would say.\r\n\r\nI observed these rites most religiously for three days. Then I\r\ncontracted a slight attack of influenza, and in poking around the\r\nkitchen, doing one of the things I oughtn\'t at the time I shouldn\'t, a\r\nservant-girl tipped a pot of boiling pot-liquor over my right foot,\r\nscalding it rather severely. Aunt Helen and grannie put me to bed, where\r\nI yelled with pain for hours like a mad Red Indian, despite their\r\napplying every alleviative possible. The combined forces of the burn and\r\ninfluenza made me a trifle dicky, so a decree went forth that I was to\r\nstay in bed until recovered from both complaints. This effectually\r\nprevented me from running in the way of any looking-glasses.\r\n\r\nI was not sufficiently ill to be miserable, and being a pampered invalid\r\nwas therefore fine fun. Aunt Helen was a wonderful nurse. She dressed my\r\nfoot splendidly every morning, and put it in a comfortable position many\r\ntimes throughout the day. Grannie brought me every dainty in the house,\r\nand sent special messengers to Gool-Gool for more. Had I been a\r\nprofessional glutton I would have been in paradise. Even Mr Hawden\r\ncondescended so far as to express his regret concerning the accident,\r\nand favoured me with visits throughout each day; and one Sunday his\r\ngallantry carried him to a gully where he plucked a bouquet of\r\nmaidenhair fern--the first of the season--and put them in a bowl beside\r\nmy bed. My uncle Julius, the only other member of the family besides the\r\nservants, was away "up the country" on some business or another, and was\r\nnot expected home for a month or so.\r\n\r\nThe Bossiers and Beechams were leaders of swelldom among the\r\nsquattocracy up the country, and firm and intimate friends. The Beechams\r\nresided at Five-Bob Downs, twelve miles from Caddagat, and were a family\r\ncomposed of two maiden ladies and their nephew, Harold. One of these\r\nladies was aunt Helen\'s particular friend, and the other had stood in\r\nthe same capacity to my mother in days gone by, but of late years, on\r\naccount of her poverty, mother had been too proud to keep up\r\ncommunication with her. As for Harold Beecham, he was nearly as much at\r\nhome at Caddagat as at Five-Bob Downs. He came and went with that\r\npleasant familiarity practised between congenial spirits among\r\nsquatterdom. The Bossiers and Beechams were congenial spirits in every\r\nway--they lived in the one sphere and held the one set of ideas, the\r\nonly difference between them, and that an unnoticeable one, being that\r\nthe Bossiers, though in comfortable circumstances, were not at all rich,\r\nwhile Harold Beecham was immensely wealthy. When my installation in the\r\nrole of invalid took place, one Miss Beecham was away in Melbourne, and\r\nthe other not well enough to come and see me, but Harold came regularly\r\nto inquire how I was progressing. He always brought me a number of\r\nbeautiful apples. This kindness was because the Caddagat orchard had\r\nbeen too infested with codlin moth for grannie to save any last season.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen used to mischievously tease me about this attention.\r\n\r\n"Here comes Harry Beecham with some more apples," she would say. "No\r\ndoubt he is far more calculating and artful than I thought he was\r\ncapable of being. He is taking time by the forelock and wooing you ere\r\nhe sees you, and so will take the lead. Young ladies are in the minority\r\nup this way, and every one is snapped up as soon as she arrives."\r\n\r\n"You\'d better tell him how ugly I am, auntie, so that he will carry\r\napples twelve miles on his own responsibility, and when he sees me won\'t\r\nbe vexed that all his work has been for nothing. Perhaps, though, it\r\nwould be better not to describe me, or I will get no more apples," I\r\nwould reply.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nAunt Helen was a clever needlewoman. She made all grannie\'s dresses and\r\nher own. Now she was making some for me, which, however, I was not to\r\nsee until I wore them. Aunt Helen had this as a pleasant surprise, and\r\nwent to the trouble of blindfolding me while I was being fitted. While\r\nin bed, grannie and auntie being busy, I was often left hours alone, and\r\nduring that time devoured the contents of my bookshelf.\r\n\r\nThe pleasure, so exquisite as to be almost pain, which I derived from\r\nthe books, and especially the Australian poets, is beyond description.\r\nIn the narrow peasant life of Possum Gully I had been deprived of\r\ncompanionship with people of refinement and education who would talk of\r\nthe things I loved; but, at last here was congeniality, here was\r\ncompanionship.\r\n\r\nThe weird witchery of mighty bush, the breath of wide sunlit plains, the\r\nsound of camp-bells and jingle of hobble chains, floating on the soft\r\ntwilight breezes, had come to these men and had written a tale on their\r\nhearts as had been written on mine. The glory of the starlit heavens,\r\nthe mighty wonder of the sea, and the majesty of thunder had come home\r\nto them, and the breathless fulness of the sunset hour had whispered of\r\nsomething more than the humour of tomorrow\'s weather. The wind and rain\r\nhad a voice which spoke to Kendall, and he too had endured the misery of\r\nlack of companionship. Gordon, with his sad, sad humanism and bitter\r\ndisappointment, held out his hand and took me with him. The regret of it\r\nall was I could never meet them--Byron, Thackeray, Dickens, Longfellow,\r\nGordon, Kendall, the men I loved, all were dead; but, blissful thought!\r\nCaine, Paterson, and Lawson were still living, breathing human\r\nbeings--two of them actually countrymen, fellow Australians!\r\n\r\nI pored with renewed zeal over the terse realism and pathos of Lawson,\r\nand enjoyed Paterson\'s redolence of the rollicking side of the wholesome\r\nlife beneath these sunny skies, which he depicted with grand touches of\r\npower flashing here and there. I learnt them by heart, and in that\r\ngloriously blue receptacle, by and by, where many pleasant youthful\r\ndreams are stowed, I put the hope that one day I would clasp hands with\r\nthem, and feel and know the unspeakable comfort and heart-rest of\r\ncongenial companionship.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER TEN\r\n\r\nEverard Grey\r\n\r\n\r\nUncle Julius had taken a run down to Sydney before returning to\r\nCaddagat, and was to be home during the first week in September,\r\nbringing with him Everard Grey. This young gentleman always spent\r\nChristmas at Caddagat, but as he had just recovered from an illness he\r\nwas coming up for a change now instead. Having heard much of him, I was\r\ncurious to see him. He was grandmamma\'s adopted son, and was the orphan\r\nof very aristocratic English parents who had left him to the\r\nguardianship of distant relatives. They had proved criminally\r\nunscrupulous. By finding a flaw in deeds, or something which none but\r\nlawyers understand, they had deprived him of all his property and left\r\nhim to sink or swim. Grannie had discovered, reared, and educated him.\r\nAmong professions he had chosen the bar, and was now one of Sydney\'s\r\nmost promising young barristers. His foster-mother was no end proud of\r\nhim, and loved him as her own son.\r\n\r\nIn due time a telegram arrived from uncle Julius, containing\r\ninstructions for the buggy to be sent to Gool-Gool to meet him and\r\nEverard Grey.\r\n\r\nBy this time I had quite recovered from influenza and my accident, and\r\nas they would not arrive till near nightfall, for their edification I\r\nwas to be dressed in full-blown dinner costume, also I was to be\r\nfavoured with a look at my reflection in a mirror for the first time\r\nsince my arrival.\r\n\r\nDuring the afternoon I was dispatched by grannie on a message some miles\r\naway, and meeting Mr Hawden some distance from the house, he took it\r\nupon himself to accompany me. Everywhere I went he followed after, much\r\nto my annoyance, because grannie gave me many and serious talkings-to\r\nabout the crime of encouraging young men.\r\n\r\nFrank Hawden had changed his tune, and told me now that it mattered not\r\nthat I was not pretty, as pretty or not I was the greatest brick of a\r\ngirl he had met. His idea for this opinion was that I was able to talk\r\ntheatres with him, and was the only girl there, and because he had\r\narrived at that overflowing age when young men have to be partial to\r\nsome female whether she be ugly or pretty, fat or lean, old or young.\r\nThat I should be the object of these puerile emotions in a fellow like\r\nFrank Hawden, filled me with loathing and disgust.\r\n\r\nIt was late in the afternoon when Hawden and I returned, and the buggy\r\nwas to be seen a long way down the road, approaching at the\r\ngoing-for-the-doctor pace at which uncle Julius always drove.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen hustled me off to dress, but I was only half-rigged when they\r\narrived, and so was unable to go out and meet them. Uncle Julius\r\ninquired for that youngster of Lucy\'s, and aunt Helen replied that she\r\nwould be forthcoming when they were dressed for dinner. The two\r\ngentlemen took a nip, to put a little heart in them uncle Julius said,\r\nand auntie Helen came to finish my toilet while they were making theirs.\r\n\r\n"There now, you have nothing to complain of in the way of looks," she\r\nremarked at the completion of the ceremony. "Come and have a good look\r\nat yourself."\r\n\r\nI was decked in my first evening dress, as it was a great occasion. It\r\nwas only on the rarest occasion that we donned full war-paint at\r\nCaddagat. I think that evening dress is one of the prettiest and most\r\nidiotic customs extant. What can be more foolish than to endanger one\'s\r\nhealth by exposing at night the chest and arms--two of the most vital\r\nspots of the body--which have been covered all day? On the other hand,\r\nwhat can be more beautiful than a soft white bosom rising and falling\r\namid a dainty nest of silk and lace? Every woman looks more soft and\r\nfeminine in a _decollete_ gown. And is there any of the animal lines\r\nknown pleasanter to the eye than the contour of shapely arms? Some there\r\nare who cry down evening dress as being immodest and indecent. These\r\nwill be found among those whose chest and arms will not admit of being\r\ndisplayed, or among those who, not having been reared to the custom,\r\ndislike it with many other things from want of use.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen took me into the wide old drawing-room, now brilliantly\r\nlighted. A heavy lamp was on each of the four brackets in the corners,\r\nand another swung from the centre of the ceiling, and candelabra threw\r\nmany lights from the piano. Never before had I seen this room in such a\r\nblaze of light. During the last week or two aunt Helen and I had\r\noccupied it every night, but we never lighted more than a single candle\r\non the piano. This had been ample light for our purpose. Aunt Helen\r\nwould sing in her sweet sad voice all the beautiful old songs I loved,\r\nwhile I curled myself on a mat at her side and read books--the music\r\noften compelling me to forget the reading, and the reading occasionally\r\nrendering me deaf to the music; but through both ever came the solemn\r\nrush of the stream outside in its weird melancholy, like a wind\r\nceaselessly endeavouring to outstrip a wild vain regret which\r\nrelentlessly pursued.\r\n\r\n"Your uncle Julius always has the drawing-room lighted like this; he\r\ndoes not believe in shadowy half light--calls it sentimental bosh," said\r\naunt Helen in explanation.\r\n\r\n"Is uncle like that?" I remarked, but my question remained unanswered.\r\nLeaving a hand-mirror with me, aunt Helen had slipped away.\r\n\r\nOne wall of the drawing-room was monopolized by a door, a big bookcase,\r\nand a heavy bevelled-edged old-fashioned mirror--the two last-mentioned\r\narticles reaching from floor to ceiling. Since my arrival the face of\r\nthe mirror had been covered, but this evening the blue silken curtains\r\nwere looped up, and it was before this that I stood.\r\n\r\nI looked, and looked again in pleased surprise. I beheld a young girl\r\nwith eyes and skin of the clearest and brightest, and lips of brilliant\r\nscarlet, and a chest and pair of arms which would pass muster with the\r\nbest. If Nature had been in bad humour when moulding my face, she had\r\nused her tools craftily in forming my figure. Aunt Helen had proved a\r\nclever maid and dressmaker. My pale blue cashmere dress fitted my fully\r\ndeveloped yet girlish figure to perfection. Some of my hair fell in\r\ncunning little curls on my forehead; the remainder, tied simply with a\r\npiece of ribbon, hung in thick waves nearly to my knees. My toilet had\r\naltered me almost beyond recognition. It made me look my age--sixteen\r\nyears and ten months--whereas before, when dressed carelessly and with\r\nmy hair plastered in a tight coil, people not knowing me would not\r\nbelieve that I was under twenty. Joy and merriment lit up my face, which\r\nglowed with youth, health, and happiness, which rippled my lips in\r\nsmiles, which displayed a splendid set of teeth, and I really believe\r\nthat on that night I did not look out of the way ugly.\r\n\r\nI was still admiring my reflection when aunt Helen returned to say that\r\nEverard and uncle Julius were smoking on the veranda and asking for me.\r\n\r\n"What do you think of yourself, Sybylla?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, aunt Helen, tell me that there is something about me not completely\r\nhideous!"\r\n\r\nShe took my face between her hands, saying:\r\n\r\n"Silly child, there are some faces with faultless features, which would\r\nreceive nothing more than an indifferent glance while beside other faces\r\nwhich might have few if any pretensions to beauty. Yours is one of those\r\nlast mentioned."\r\n\r\n"But that does not say I am not ugly."\r\n\r\n"No one would dream of calling you plain, let alone ugly; brilliant is\r\nthe word which best describes you."\r\n\r\nUncle Julius had the upper part of his ponderous figure arrayed in a\r\nfrock-coat. He did not take kindly to what he termed "those skittish\r\nsparrow-tailed affairs". Frock-coats suited him, but I am not partial to\r\nthem on every one. They look well enough on a podgy, fat, or broad man,\r\nbut on a skinny one they hang with such a forlorn, dying-duck\r\nexpression, that they invariably make me laugh.\r\n\r\nJulius John Bossier, better known as J. J. Bossier, and better still as\r\nJay-Jay--big, fat, burly, broad, a jovial bachelor of forty, too fond of\r\nall the opposite sex ever to have settled his affections on one in\r\nparticular--was well known, respected, and liked from Wagga Wagga to\r\nAlbury, Forbes to Dandaloo, Bourke to Hay, from Tumut to Monaro, and\r\nback again to Peak Hill, as a generous man, a straight goer in business\r\nmatters, and a jolly good fellow all round.\r\n\r\nI was very proud to call him uncle.\r\n\r\n"So this is yourself, is it!" he exclaimed, giving me a tremendous hug.\r\n\r\n"Oh, uncle," I expostulated, "wipe your old kisses off! Your breath\r\nsmells horribly of whisky and tobacco."\r\n\r\n"Gammon, that\'s what makes my kisses so nice!" he answered; and, after\r\nholding me at arm\'s-length for inspection, "By George, you\'re a\r\nwonderful-looking girl! You\'re surely not done growing yet, though! You\r\nare such a little nipper. I could put you in my pocket with ease. You\r\naren\'t a scrap like your mother. I\'ll give the next shearer who passes a\r\nshilling to cut that hair off. It would kill a dog in the hot weather."\r\n\r\n"Everard, this is my niece, Sybylla" (aunt Helen was introducing us).\r\n"You will have to arrange yourselves--what relation you are, and how to\r\naddress each other."\r\n\r\nThe admiration expressed in his clear sharp eyes gave me a sensation\r\ndifferent to any I had ever experienced previously.\r\n\r\n"I suppose I\'m a kind of uncle and brother in one, and as either\r\nrelationship entitles me to a kiss, I\'m going to take one," he said in a\r\nvery gallant manner.\r\n\r\n"You may take one if you can," I said with mischievous defiance,\r\nspringing off the veranda into the flower-garden. He accepted my\r\nchallenge, and, being lithe as a cat, a tremendous scamper ensued. Round\r\nand round the flower-beds we ran. Uncle Jay-Jay\'s beard opened in a\r\nbroad smile, which ended in a loud laugh. Everard Grey\'s coat-tails flew\r\nin the breeze he made, and his collar was too high for athletic\r\npurposes. I laughed too, and was lost, and we returned to the\r\nveranda--Everard in triumph, and I feeling very red and uncomfortable.\r\n\r\nGrannie had arrived upon the scene, looking the essence of brisk\r\nrespectability in a black silk gown and a white lace cap. She cast on me\r\na glance of severe disapproval, and denounced my conduct as shameful;\r\nbut uncle Jay-Jay\'s eyes twinkled as he dexterously turned the subject.\r\n\r\n"Gammon, mother! I bet you were often kissed when that youngster\'s age.\r\nI bet my boots now that you can\'t count the times you did the same thing\r\nyourself. Now, confess."\r\n\r\nGrannie\'s face melted in a smile as she commenced a little anecdote,\r\nwith that pathetic beginning, "When I was young."\r\n\r\nAunt Helen sent me inside lest I should catch cold, and I stationed\r\nmyself immediately inside the window so that I should not miss the\r\nconversation. "I should think your niece is very excitable," Mr Grey was\r\nsaying to aunt Helen.\r\n\r\n"Oh, very."\r\n\r\n"Yes; I have never seen any but very highly strung temperaments have\r\nthat transparent brilliance of expression."\r\n\r\n"She is very variable--one moment all joy, and the next the reverse."\r\n\r\n"She has a very striking face. I don\'t know what it is that makes it\r\nso."\r\n\r\n"It may be her complexion," said aunt Helen; "her skin is whiter than\r\nthe fairest blonde, and her eyebrows and lashes very dark. Be very\r\ncareful you do not say anything that would let her know you think her\r\nnot nice looking. She broods over her appearance in such a morbid\r\nmanner. It is a weak point with her, so be careful not to sting her\r\nsensitiveness in that respect."\r\n\r\n"Plain-looking! Why, I think she has one of the most fascinating faces\r\nI\'ve seen for some time, and her eyes are simply magnificent. What\r\ncolour are they?"\r\n\r\n"The grass is not bad about Sydney. I think I will send a truck of fat\r\nwethers away next week," said uncle Jay-Jay to grannie.\r\n\r\n"It is getting quite dark. Let\'s get in to dinner at once," said\r\ngrannie.\r\n\r\nDuring the meal I took an opportunity of studying the appearance of\r\nEverard Grey. He had a typically aristocratic English face, even to the\r\ncold rather heartless expression, which is as established a point of an\r\nEnglish blue blood as an arched neck is of a thoroughbred horse.\r\n\r\nA ringer, whose wife had been unexpectedly confined, came for grannie\r\nwhen dinner was over, and the rest of us had a delightful musical\r\nevening. Uncle Jay-Jay bawled "The Vicar of Bray" and "Drink, Puppy,\r\nDrink" in a stentorian bass voice, holding me on his knee, pinching,\r\ntickling, pulling my hair, and shaking me up and down between whiles. Mr\r\nHawden favoured us by rendering "The Holy City". Everard Grey sang\r\nseveral new songs, which was a great treat, as he had a well-trained and\r\nmusical baritone voice. He was a veritable carpet knight, and though not\r\na fop, was exquisitely dressed in full evening costume, and showed his\r\nlong pedigreed blood in every line of his clean-shaven face and tall\r\nslight figure. He was quite a champion on the piano, and played aunt\r\nHelen\'s accompaniments while he made her sing song after song. When she\r\nwas weary uncle Jay-Jay said to me, "Now it\'s your turn, me fine lady.\r\nWe\'ve all done something to keep things rolling but you. Can you sing?"\r\n\r\n"No,"\r\n\r\n"Can this youngster sing, Helen?"\r\n\r\n"She sings very nicely to herself sometimes, but I do not know how she\r\nwould manage before company. Will you try something, Sybylla?"\r\n\r\nUncle Jay-Jay waited to hear no more, but carrying me to the\r\nmusic-stool, and depositing me thereon, warned me not to attempt to\r\nleave it before singing something.\r\n\r\nTo get away to myself, where I was sure no one could bear me, and sing\r\nand sing till I made the echoes ring, was one of the chief joys of my\r\nexistence, but I had never made a success in singing to company. Besides\r\nlosing all nerve, I had a very queer voice, which every one remarked.\r\nHowever, tonight I made an effort in my old favourite, "Three Fishers\r\nWent Sailing". The beauty of the full-toned Ronisch piano, and Everard\'s\r\nclever and sympathetic accompanying, caused me to forget my audience,\r\nand sing as though to myself alone, forgetting that my voice was odd.\r\n\r\nWhen the song ceased Mr Grey wheeled abruptly on the stool and said, "Do\r\nyou know that you have one of the most wonderful natural voices I have\r\nheard. Why, there is a fortune in such a voice if it were, trained! Such\r\nchest-notes, such feeling, such rarity of tone!"\r\n\r\n"Don\'t be sarcastic, Mr Grey," I said shortly.\r\n\r\n"Upon my word as a man, I mean every word I say," he returned\r\nenthusiastically.\r\n\r\nEverard Grey\'s opinion on artistic matters was considered worth having.\r\nHe dabbled in all the arts--writing, music, acting, and sketching, and\r\nwent to every good concert and play in Sydney. Though he was clever at\r\nlaw, it was whispered by some that he would wind up on the stage, as he\r\nhad a great leaning that way.\r\n\r\nI walked away from the piano treading on air. Would I really make a\r\nsinger? I with the voice which had often been ridiculed; I who had often\r\nblasphemously said that I would sell my soul to be able to sing just\r\npassably. Everard Grey\'s opinion gave me an intoxicated sensation of\r\njoy.\r\n\r\n"Can you recite?" he inquired.\r\n\r\n"Yes," I answered firmly.\r\n\r\n"Give us something," said uncle Jay-Jay.\r\n\r\nI recited Longfellow\'s "The Slave\'s Dream". Everard Grey was quite as\r\nenthusiastic over this as he had been about my singing.\r\n\r\n"Such a voice! Such depth and width! Why, she could fill the Centennial\r\nHall without an effort. All she requires is training."\r\n\r\n"By George, she\'s a regular dab! But I wish she would give us something\r\nnot quite so glum," said uncle Jay-Jay.\r\n\r\nI let myself go. Carried away by I don\'t know what sort of a spirit, I\r\nexclaimed, "Very well, I will, if you will wait till I make up, and will\r\nhelp me."\r\n\r\nI disappeared for a few minutes, and returned made up as a fat old Irish\r\nwoman, with a smudge of dirt on my face. There was a general laugh.\r\n\r\nWould Mr Hawden assist me? Of course he was only too delighted, and\r\nflattered that I had called upon him in preference to the others. What\r\nwould he do?\r\n\r\nI sat him on a footstool, so that I might with facility put my hand on\r\nhis sandy hair, and turning to uncle, commenced:\r\n\r\n"Shure, sir, seeing it was a good bhoy yez were afther to run errants,\r\nit\'s meself that has brought this youngsther for yer inspection. It\'s a\r\njool ye\'ll have in him. Shure I rared him meself, and he says his\r\nprayers every morning. Kape sthill, honey! Faith, ye\'re not afraid of\r\nyer poor old mammy pullin\' yer beautiful cur-r-rls?"\r\n\r\nUncle Jay-Jay was laughing like fun; even aunt Helen deigned to smile;\r\nand Everard was looking on with critical interest.\r\n\r\n"Go on," said uncle. But Mr Hawden got huffy at the ridicule which he\r\nsuspected I was calling down upon him, and jumped up looking fit to eat\r\nme.\r\n\r\nI acted several more impromptu scenes with the other occupants of the\r\ndrawing-room. Mr Hawden emitted "Humph!" from the corner where he\r\ngrumpily sat, but Mr Grey was full of praise.\r\n\r\n"Splendid! splendid!" he exclaimed. "You say you have not had an hour\'s\r\ntraining, and never saw a play. Such versatility. Your fortune would be\r\nmade on the stage. It is a sin to have such exceptional talent wasting\r\nin the bush. I must take her to Sydney and put her under a good master."\r\n\r\n"Indeed, you\'ll do no such thing," said uncle. "I\'ll keep her here to\r\nliven up the old barracks. You\'ve got enough puppets on the stage\r\nwithout a niece of mine ever being there."\r\n\r\nI went to bed that night greatly elated. Flattery is sweet to youth. I\r\nfelt pleased with myself, and imagined, as I peeped in the\r\nlooking-glass, that I was not half bad-looking after all.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER ELEVEN\r\n\r\nYah!\r\n\r\n\r\n"Bah, you hideous animal! Ha ha! Your peerless conceit does you credit.\r\nSo you actually imagined that by one or two out of every hundred you\r\nmight be considered passable. You are the most uninteresting person in\r\nthe world. You are small and nasty and bad, and every other thing that\'s\r\nabominable. That\'s what you are."\r\n\r\nThis address I delivered to my reflection in the glass next morning. My\r\nelation of the previous night was as flat as a pancake. Dear, oh dear,\r\nwhat a fool I had been to softly swallow the flattery of Mr Grey without\r\na single snub in return! To make up for my laxity, if he continued to\r\namuse himself by plastering my vanity with the ointment of flattery, I\r\ndetermined to serve up my replies to him red-hot and well seasoned with\r\npepper.\r\n\r\nI finished my toilet, and in a very what\'s-the-good-o\'-anything mood\r\ntook a last glance in the glass to say, "You\'re ugly, you\'re ugly and\r\nuseless; so don\'t forget that and make a fool of yourself again."\r\n\r\nI was in the habit of doing this; it had long ago taken the place of a\r\nmorning prayer. I said this, that by familiarity it might lose a little\r\nof its sting when I heard it from other lips, but somehow it failed in\r\nefficacy.\r\n\r\nI was late for breakfast that morning. All the others were half through\r\nthe meal when I sat down.\r\n\r\nGrannie had not come home till after twelve, but was looking as brisk as\r\nusual.\r\n\r\n"Come, Sybylla, I suppose this comes of sitting up too late, as I was\r\nnot here to hunt you to bed. You are always very lively at night, but\r\nit\'s a different tune in the morning," she said, when giving me the\r\nusual morning hug.\r\n\r\n"When I was a nipper of your age, if I didn\'t turn out like greased\r\nlightning every morning, I was assisted by a little strap oil," remarked\r\nuncle Jay-Jay.\r\n\r\n"Sybylla should be excused this morning," interposed Mr Grey. "She\r\nentertained us for hours last night. Little wonder if she feels languid\r\nthis morning."\r\n\r\n"Entertained you I What did she do?" queried grannie.\r\n\r\n"Many things. Do you know, gran, that you are robbing the world of an\r\nartist by keeping Sybylla hidden away in the bush? I must persuade you\r\nto let me take her to Sydney and have her put under the best masters in\r\nSydney."\r\n\r\n"Under masters for what?"\r\n\r\n"Elocution and singing."\r\n\r\n"I couldn\'t afford it."\r\n\r\n"But I\'d bear the expense myself. It would only be returning a trifle of\r\nall you have done for me."\r\n\r\n"What nonsense! What would you have her do when she was taught?"\r\n\r\n"Go on the stage, of course. With her talent and hair she would cause\r\nquite a sensation."\r\n\r\nNow grannie\'s notions re the stage were very tightly laced. All actors\r\nand actresses, from the lowest circus man up to the most glorious\r\ncantatrice, were people defiled in the sight of God, and utterly outside\r\nthe pale of all respectability, when measured with her code of morals.\r\n\r\nShe turned energetically in her chair, and her keen eyes flashed with\r\nscorn and anger as she spoke.\r\n\r\n"Go on the stage! A grand-daughter of mine! Lucy\'s eldest child! An\r\nactress--a vile, low, brazen hussy! Use the gifts God has given her with\r\nwhich to do good in showing off to a crowd of vile bad men! I would\r\nrather see her struck dead at my feet this instant! I would rather see\r\nher shear off her hair and enter a convent this very hour. Child,\r\npromise you will never be a bold bad actress."\r\n\r\n"I will never be a _bold bad_ actress, grannie," I said, putting great\r\nstress on the adjectives, and bringing out the actress very faintly.\r\n\r\n"Yes," she continued, calming down, "I\'m sure you have not enough bad in\r\nyou. You may he boisterous, and not behave with sufficient propriety\r\nsometimes, but I don\'t think you are wicked enough to ever make an\r\nactress."\r\n\r\nEverard attempted to defend his case.\r\n\r\n"Look here, gran, that\'s a very exploded old notion about the stage\r\nbeing a low profession. It might have been once, but it is quite the\r\nreverse nowadays. There are, of course, low people on the stage, as\r\nthere are in all walks of life. I grant you that; but if people are good\r\nthey can be good on the stage as well as anywhere else. On account of a\r\nlittle prejudice it would be a sin to rob Sybylla of the brilliant\r\ncareer she might have."\r\n\r\n"Career!" exclaimed his foster-mother, catching at the word. "Career!\r\nThat is all girls think of now, instead of being good wives and mothers\r\nand attending to their homes and doing what God intended. All they think\r\nof is gadding about and being fast, and ruining themselves body and\r\nsoul. And the men are as bad to encourage them," looking severely at\r\nEverard.\r\n\r\n"There is a great deal of truth in what you say, gran, I admit. You can\r\napply it to many of our girls, I am sorry to confess, but Sybylla could\r\nnot be brought under that classification. You must look at her in a\r\ndifferent way. If--"\r\n\r\n"I look at her as the child of respectable people, and will not have the\r\nstage mentioned in connection with her." Here Grannie thumped her fist\r\ndown on the table and there was silence, complete, profound. Few dared\r\nargue with Mrs Bossier.\r\n\r\nDear old lady, she was never angry long, and in a minute or two she\r\nproceeded with her breakfast, saying quite pleasantly:\r\n\r\n"Never mention such a subject to me again; but I\'ll tell you what you\r\ncan do. Next autumn, some time in March or April, when the\r\nfruit-preserving and jam-making are done with, Helen can take the child\r\nto Sydney for a month or so, and you can show them round. It will be a\r\ngreat treat for Sybylla as she has never been in Sydney."\r\n\r\n"That\'s right, let\'s strike a bargain on that, gran," said Everard.\r\n\r\n"Yes; it\'s a bargain, if I hear no more about the stage. God intends His\r\ncreatures for a better life than that."\r\n\r\nAfter breakfast I was left to entertain Everard for some while. We had a\r\nfine time. He was a perfect gentleman and a clever conversationalist.\r\n\r\nI was always desirous of enjoying the company of society people who were\r\nwell bred and lived according to etiquette, and possessed of leisure and\r\nculture sufficient to fill their minds with something more than the\r\nprice of farm produce and a hard struggle for existence. Hitherto I had\r\nonly read of such or seen them in pictures, but here was a real live\r\none, and I seized my opportunity with vim. At my questioning and evident\r\ninterest in his talk he told me of all the latest plays, actors, and\r\nactresses with whom he was acquainted, and described the fashionable\r\nballs, dinners, and garden-parties he attended. Having exhausted this\r\nsubject, we fell to discussing books, and I recited snatches of poems\r\ndear to me. Everard placed his hands upon my shoulders and said:\r\n\r\n"Sybylla, do you know you are a most wonderful girl? Your figure is\r\nperfect, your style refreshing, and you have a most interesting face. It\r\nis as ever-changing as a kaleidoscope--sometimes merry, then stern,\r\noften sympathetic, and always sad when at rest. One would think you had\r\nhad some sorrow in your life."\r\n\r\nLifting my skirt at either side, I bowed several times very low in what\r\nI called my stage bow, and called into requisition my stage smile, which\r\ndisplayed two rows of teeth as white and perfect as any twenty-guinea\r\nset turned out on a gold plate by a fashionable dentist.\r\n\r\n"The handsome gentleman is very kind to amuse himself at the expense of\r\na little country bumpkin, but he would do well to ascertain if his\r\nflattery would go down before administering it next time," I said\r\nsarcastically, and I heard him calling to me as I abruptly went off to\r\nshut myself in my room.\r\n\r\n"How dare anyone ridicule me by paying idle brainless compliments! I\r\nknew I was ugly, and did not want any one to perjure his soul pretending\r\nthey thought differently. What right had I to be small? Why wasn\'t I\r\npossessed of a big aquiline nose and a tall commanding figure?" Thus I\r\nsat in burning discontent and ill-humour until soothed by the scent of\r\nroses and the gleam of soft spring sunshine which streamed in through my\r\nopen window. Some of the flower-beds in the garden were completely\r\ncarpeted with pansy blossoms, all colours, and violets-blue and white,\r\nsingle and double. The scent of mignonette, jonquils, and narcissi\r\nfilled the air. I revelled in rich perfumes, and these tempted me forth.\r\nMy ruffled feelings gave way before the delights of the old garden. I\r\ncollected a number of vases, and, filling them with water, set them on a\r\ntable in the veranda near one of the drawing-room windows. I gathered\r\nlapfuls of the lovely blossoms, and commenced arranging them in the\r\nvases.\r\n\r\nPart of the old Caddagat house was built of slabs, and one of the wooden\r\nwalls ran along the veranda side of the drawing-room, so the songs aunt\r\nHelen and Everard Grey were trying to the piano came as a sweet\r\naccompaniment to my congenial task.\r\n\r\nPresently they left off singing and commenced talking. Under the same\r\ncircumstances a heroine of a story would have slipped away; or, if that\r\nwere impossible without discovery, she would have put her fingers in her\r\nears, and would have been in a terrible state of agitation lest she\r\nshould hear something not intended for her. I did not come there with a\r\nview to eavesdropping. It is a degradation to which I never stoop. I\r\nthought they were aware of my presence on the veranda; but it appears\r\nthey were not, as they began to discuss me (wonderfully interesting\r\nsubject to myself), and I stayed there, without one word of disapproval\r\nfrom my conscience, to listen to their conversation.\r\n\r\n"My word, didn\'t gran make a to-do this morning when I proposed to train\r\nSybylla for the stage! Do you know that girl is simply reeking with\r\ntalent; I must have her trained. I will keep bringing the idea before\r\ngran until she gets used to it. I\'ll work the\r\nwe-should-use-the-gifts-God-has-given-us racket for all it is worth, and\r\nyou might use your influence too, Helen."\r\n\r\n"No, Everard; there are very few who succeed on the stage. I would not\r\nuse my influence, as it is a life of which I do not approve."\r\n\r\n"But Sybylla _would_ succeed. I am a personal friend of the leading\r\nmanagers, and my influence would help her greatly."\r\n\r\n"Yes; but what would you do with her? A young gentleman couldn\'t take\r\ncharge of a girl and bring her out without ruining her reputation. There\r\nwould be no end of scandal, as the sister theory would only be\r\nnonsense."\r\n\r\n"There is another way; I could easily stop scandal."\r\n\r\n"Everard, what do you mean!"\r\n\r\n"I mean marriage," he replied deliberately.\r\n\r\n"Surely, boy, you must be dreaming! You have only seen her for an hour\r\nor two. I don\'t believe in these sudden attachments."\r\n\r\nPerhaps she here thought of one (her own) as sudden, which had not ended\r\nhappily.\r\n\r\n"Everard, don\'t do anything rashly. You know you are very fickle and\r\nconsidered a lady-killer--be merciful to my poor little Sybylla, I pray.\r\nIt is just one of your passing fancies. Don\'t wile her passionate young\r\nheart away and then leave her to pine and die."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t think she is that sort," he replied laughingly.\r\n\r\n"No, she would not die, but would grow into a cynic and sceptic, which\r\nis the worst of fates. Let her alone. Flirt as much as you will with\r\nsociety belles who understand the game, but leave my country maiden\r\nalone. I hope to mould her into a splendid character yet."\r\n\r\n"But, Helen, supposing I am in earnest at last, you don\'t think I\'d make\r\nher a bad old hubby, do you?"\r\n\r\n"She is not the girl for you. You are not the man who could ever control\r\nher. What I say may not be complimentary but it is true. Besides, she is\r\nnot seventeen yet, and I do not approve of romantic young girls throwing\r\nthemselves into matrimony. Let them develop their womanhood first."\r\n\r\n"Then I expect I had better hide my attractions under a bushel during\r\nthe remainder of my stay at Caddagat?"\r\n\r\n"Yes. Be as nice to the child as you like, but mind, none of those\r\nlittle ladies\'-man attentions with which it is so easy to steal--"\r\n\r\nI waited to hear no more, but, brimming over with a mixture of emotions,\r\ntore through the garden and into the old orchard. Bees were busy, and\r\ncountless bright-coloured butterflies flitted hither and thither,\r\nsipping from hundreds of trees, white or pink with bloom--their beauty\r\nwas lost upon me. I stood ankle-deep in violets, where they had run wild\r\nunder a gnarled old apple-tree, and gave way to my wounded vanity.\r\n\r\n"Little country maiden, indeed! There\'s no need for him to bag his\r\nattractions up. If he exerted himself to the utmost of his ability, he\r\ncould not make me love him. I\'m not a child. I saw through him in the\r\nfirst hour. There\'s not enough in him to win my love. I\'ll show him I\r\nthink no more of him than of the caterpillars on the old tree there. I\'m\r\nnot a booby that will fall in love with every gussie I see. Bah, there\'s\r\nno fear of that! I hate and detest men!"\r\n\r\n"I suppose you are rehearsing some more airs to show off with tonight,"\r\nsneered a voice behind me.\r\n\r\n"No, I\'m realisticing; and how _dare_ you thrust your obnoxious presence\r\nbefore me when I wish to be alone! Haven\'t I often shown--"\r\n\r\n"While a girl is disengaged, any man who is her equal has the right to\r\npay his addresses to her if he is in earnest," interrupted Mr Hawden. It\r\nwas he who stood before me.\r\n\r\n"I am well aware of that," I replied. "But it is a woman\'s privilege to\r\nrepel those attentions if distasteful to her. You seem disinclined to\r\naccord me that privilege."\r\n\r\nHaving delivered this retort, I returned to the house, leaving him\r\nstanding there looking the fool he was.\r\n\r\nI do not believe in spurning the love of a blackfellow if he behaves in\r\na manly way; but Frank Hawden was such a drivelling mawkish style of\r\nsweetheart that I had no patience with him.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen and Everard had vacated the drawing-room, so I plumped down\r\non the piano-stool and dashed into Kowalski\'s galop, from that into\r\n"Gaite de Coeur" until I made the piano dance and tremble like a thing\r\npossessed. My annoyance faded, and I slowly played that saddest of\r\nwaltzes, "Weber\'s Last". I became aware of a presence in the room, and,\r\nfacing about, confronted Everard Grey.\r\n\r\n"How long have you been here?" I demanded sharply.\r\n\r\n"Since you began to play. Where on earth did you learn to play? Your\r\nexecution is splendid. Do sing \'Three Fishers\', please."\r\n\r\n"Excuse me; I haven\'t time now. Besides I am not competent to sing to\r\nyou," I said brusquely, and made my exit.\r\n\r\n"Mr Hawden wants you, Sybylla," called aunt Helen. "See what he wants\r\nand let him get away to his work, or your grannie will be vexed to see\r\nhim loitering about all the morning."\r\n\r\n"Miss Sybylla," he began, when we were left alone, "I want to apologize\r\nto you. I had no right to plague you, but it all comes of the way I love\r\nyou. A fellow gets jealous at the least little thing, you know."\r\n\r\n"Bore me with no more such trash," I said, turning away in disgust.\r\n\r\n"But, Miss Sybylla, what am I to do with it?"\r\n\r\n"Do with what?"\r\n\r\n"My love."\r\n\r\n"Love!" I retorted scornfully. "There is no such thing."\r\n\r\n"But there is, and I have found it."\r\n\r\n"Well, you stick to it--that\'s my advice to you. It will be a treasure.\r\nIf you send it to my father he will get it bottled up and put it in the\r\nGoulburn museum. He has sent several things there already."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t make such a game of a poor devil. You know I can\'t do that."\r\n\r\n"Bag it up, then; put a big stone to make it sink, and pitch it in the\r\nriver."\r\n\r\n"You\'ll rue this," he said savagely.\r\n\r\n"I may or may not," I sang over my shoulder as I departed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER TWELVE\r\n\r\nOne Grand Passion\r\n\r\n\r\nI had not the opportunity of any more private interviews with Everard\r\nGrey till one morning near his departure, when we happened to be alone\r\non the veranda.\r\n\r\n"Well, Miss Sybylla," he began, "when I arrived I thought you and I\r\nwould have been great friends; but we have not progressed at all. How do\r\nyou account for that?"\r\n\r\nAs he spoke he laid his slender shapely hand kindly upon my head. He was\r\nvery handsome and winning, and moved in literary, musical, and artistic\r\nsociety--a man from my world, a world away.\r\n\r\nOh, what pleasure I might have derived from companionship with him! I\r\nbit my lip to keep back the tears. Why did not social arrangements allow\r\na man and a maid to be chums--chums as two men or two maids may be to\r\neach other, enjoying each other without thought beyond pure platonic\r\nfriendship? But no; it could not be. I understood the conceit of men.\r\nShould I be very affable, I feared Everard Grey would imagine he had\r\nmade a conquest of me. On the other hand, were I glum he would think the\r\nsame, and that I was trying to hide my feelings behind a mask of\r\nbrusquerie. I therefore steered in a bee-line between the two manners,\r\nand remarked with the greatest of indifference:\r\n\r\n"I was not aware that you expected us to be such cronies--in fact, I\r\nhave never given the matter a thought."\r\n\r\nHe turned away in a piqued style. Such a beau of beaux, no doubt he was\r\nannoyed that an insignificant little country bumpkin should not be\r\nflattered by his patronage, or probably he thought me rude or\r\nill-humoured.\r\n\r\nTwo mornings later uncle Jay-Jay took him to Gool-Gool _en route_ for\r\nSydney. When departing he bade me a kindly good-bye, made me promise to\r\nwrite to him, and announced his intention of obtaining the opinion of\r\nsome good masters re my dramatic talent and voice, when I came to Sydney\r\nas promised by my grandmother. I stood on the garden fence waving my\r\nhandkerchief until the buggy passed out of sight among the\r\nmessmate-trees about half a mile from the house.\r\n\r\n"Well I hope, as that dandified ape has gone--and good riddance to\r\nhim--that you will pay more heed to my attentions now," said Mr Hawden\'s\r\nvoice, as I was in the act of descending from the fence.\r\n\r\n"What do you mean by your attentions?"
