I demanded.\r\n\r\n"What do I mean! That is something like coming to business. I\'ll soon\r\nexplain. You know what my intentions are very well. When I am\r\ntwenty-four, I will come into my property in England. It is\r\nconsiderable, and at the end of that time I want to marry you and take\r\nyou home. By Jove! I would just like to take you home. You\'d surprise\r\nsome English girls I know."\r\n\r\n"There would be more than one person surprised if I married you," I\r\nthought to myself, and laughed till I ached with the motion.\r\n\r\n"You infernal little vixen! What are you laughing at? You\'ve got no more\r\nsense than a bat if such a solemn thing only provokes your mirth."\r\n\r\n"Solemn--why, it\'s a screaming farce!" I laughed more and more.\r\n\r\n"What\'s a farce?" he demanded fiercely.\r\n\r\n"The bare idea of you proposing to me."\r\n\r\n"Why? Have I not as much right to propose as any other man?"\r\n\r\n"Man!" I laughed. "That\'s where the absurdity arises. My child, if you\r\nwere a man, certainly you could propose, but do you think I\'d look at a\r\nboy, a child! If ever I perpetrate matrimony the participant in my\r\ndegradation will be a fully developed man--not a hobbledehoy who falls\r\nin love, as he terms it, on an average about twice a week. Love! Ho!"\r\n\r\nI moved in the direction of the house. He barred my path.\r\n\r\n"You are not going to escape me like that, my fine lady. I will make you\r\nlisten to me this time or you will hear more about it," and he seized me\r\nangrily by the wrist.\r\n\r\nI cannot bear the touch of any one--it is one of my idiosyncrasies. With\r\nmy disengaged hand I struck him a vigorous blow on the nose, and\r\nwrenching myself free sprang away, saying, "How dare you lay a finger on\r\nme! If you attempt such a thing again I\'ll make short work of you. Mark\r\nmy words, or you\'ll get something more than a bleeding nose next time, I\r\npromise you."\r\n\r\n"You\'ll hear more of this! You\'ll hear more of this! You fierce, wild,\r\ntouch-me-not thing," he roared.\r\n\r\n"Yes; my motto with men is touch-me-not, and it is your own fault if I\'m\r\nfierce. If children attempt to act the role of a man with adult tools,\r\nthey are sure to cut themselves. Hold hard a bit, honey, till your\r\nwhiskers grow," I retorted as I departed, taking flying leaps over the\r\nblossom-burdened flower-beds.\r\n\r\nAt tea that night, after gazing interestedly at Mr Hawden\'s nose for\r\nsome time, uncle Julius inquired, "in the name of all that\'s mysterious,\r\nwhat the devil have you been doing to your nose? You look as though you\r\nhad been on the spree."\r\n\r\nI was quaking lest he would get me into a fine scrape, but he only\r\nmuttered, "By Jove!" with great energy, and glowered menacingly across\r\nthe table at me.\r\n\r\nAfter tea he requested an interview with grannie, which aroused my\r\ncuriosity greatly. I was destined to hear all about it next morning.\r\nWhen breakfast was over grannie called me into her room and interviewed\r\nme about Mr Hawden\'s interview. She began without any preliminaries:\r\n\r\n"Mr Hawden has complained of your conduct. It grieves me that any young\r\nman should have to speak to me of the behaviour of my own\r\ngrand-daughter. He says you have been flirting with him. Sybylla, I\r\nscarcely thought you would be so immodest and unwomanly."\r\n\r\nOn hearing this my thoughts of Frank Hawden were the reverse of\r\nflattering. He had persecuted me beyond measure, yet I had not deigned\r\nto complain of him to either uncle, grannie, or auntie, as I might\r\nreasonably have done, and have obtained immediate redress. He had been\r\nthe one to blame in the case, yet for the rebuffs he had brought upon\r\nhimself, went tattling to my grandmother.\r\n\r\n"Is that all you have to say, grannie?"\r\n\r\n"No. He wants to marry you, and has asked my consent. I told him it all\r\nrested with yourself and parents. What do you say?"\r\n\r\n"Say," I exclaimed, "grannie, you are only joking, are you not?"\r\n\r\n"No, my child, this is not a matter to joke about."\r\n\r\n"Marry that creature! A boy!" I uttered in consternation.\r\n\r\n"He is no boy. He has attained his majority some months. He is as old as\r\nyour grandfather was when we married. In three years you will be almost\r\ntwenty, and by that time he will be in possession of his property which\r\nis very good--in fact, he will be quite rich. If you care for him there\r\nis nothing against him as I can see. He is healthy, has a good\r\ncharacter, and comes of a high family. Being a bit wild won\'t matter.\r\nVery often, after they sow their wild oats, some of those scampy young\r\nfellows settle down and marry a nice young girl and turn out very good\r\nhusbands."\r\n\r\n"It is disgusting, and you ought to be downright ashamed of yourself,\r\ngrannie! A man can live a life of bestiality and then be considered a\r\nfit husband for the youngest and purest girl! It is shameful! Frank\r\nHawden is not wild, he hasn\'t got enough in him to be so. I hate him.\r\nNo, he hasn\'t enough in him to hate. I loathe and despise him. I would\r\nnot marry him or any one like him though he were King of England. The\r\nidea of marriage even with the best man in the world seems to me a\r\nlowering thing," I raged; "but with him it would be pollution--the\r\nlowest degradation that could be heaped upon me! I will never come down\r\nto marry any one--" here I fell a victim to a flood of excited tears.\r\n\r\nI felt there was no good in the world, especially in men--the hateful\r\ncreatures!--and never would be while it was not expected of them, even\r\nby rigidly pure, true Christians such as my grandmother. Grannie, dear\r\nold grannie, thought I should marry any man who, from a financial point\r\nof view, was a good match for me. That is where the sting came in. No, I\r\nwould never marry. I would procure some occupation in which I could\r\ntread my life out, independent of the degradation of marriage.\r\n\r\n"Dear me, child," said grannie, concernedly, "there is no need to\r\ndistress yourself so. I remember you were always fearfully passionate.\r\nWhen I had you with me as a tiny toddler, you would fret a whole day\r\nabout a thing an ordinary child would forget inside an hour. I will tell\r\nHawden to go about his business. I would not want you to consider\r\nmarriage for an instant with anyone distasteful to you. But tell me\r\ntruly, have you ever flirted with him? I will take your word, for I\r\nthank God you have never yet told me a falsehood!"\r\n\r\n"Grannie," I exclaimed emphatically, "I have discouraged him all I\r\ncould. I would scorn to flirt with any man."\r\n\r\n"Well, well, that is all I want to hear about it. Wash your eyes, and we\r\nwill get our horses and go over to see Mrs Hickey and her baby, and take\r\nher something good to eat."\r\n\r\nI did not encounter Frank Hawden again till the afternoon, when he\r\nleered at me in a very triumphant manner. I stiffened myself and drew\r\nout of his way as though he had been some vile animal. At this treatment\r\nhe whined, so I agreed to talk the matter over with him and have done\r\nwith it once and for all.\r\n\r\nHe was on his way to water some dogs, so I accompanied him out to the\r\nstables near the kennels, to be out of hearing of the household.\r\n\r\nI opened fire without any beating about the bush.\r\n\r\n"I ask you, Mr Hawden, if you have any sense of manliness, from this\r\nhour to cease persecuting me with your idiotic professions of love. I\r\nhave two sentiments regarding it, and in either you disgust me.\r\nSometimes I don\'t believe there is such a thing as love at all--that is,\r\nlove between men and women. While in this frame of mind I would not\r\nlisten to professions of love from an angel. Other times I believe in\r\nlove, and look upon it as a sacred and solemn thing. When in that\r\nhumour, it seems to me a desecration to hear you twaddling about the\r\nholy theme, for you are only a boy, and don\'t know how to feel. I would\r\nnot have spoken thus harshly to you, but by your unmanly conduct you\r\nhave brought it upon yourself. I have told you straight all that I will\r\never deign to tell you on the subject, and take much pleasure in wishing\r\nyou good afternoon."\r\n\r\nI walked away quickly, heedless of his expostulations.\r\n\r\nMy appeal to his manliness had no effect. Did I go for a ride, or a walk\r\nin the afternoon to enjoy the glory of the sunset, or a stroll to drink\r\nin the pleasures of the old garden, there would I find Frank Hawden by\r\nmy side, yah, yah, yahing about the way I treated him, until I wished\r\nhim at the bottom of the Red Sea.\r\n\r\nHowever, in those glorious spring days the sense of life was too\r\npleasant to be much clouded by the trifling annoyance Frank Hawden\r\noccasioned me. The graceful wild clematis festooned the shrubbery along\r\nthe creeks with great wreaths of magnificent white bloom, which loaded\r\nevery breeze with perfume; the pretty bright green senna shrubs along\r\nthe river-banks were decked in blossoms which rivalled the deep blue of\r\nthe sky in brilliance; the magpies built their nests in the tall\r\ngum-trees, and savagely attacked unwary travellers who ventured too near\r\ntheir domain; the horses were rolling fat, and invited one to get on\r\ntheir satin backs and have a gallop; the cry of the leather-heads was\r\nheard in the orchard as the cherry season approached. Oh, it was good to\r\nbe alive!\r\n\r\nAt Caddagat I was as much out of the full flood of life for which I\r\ncraved as at Possum Gully, but here there were sufficient pleasant\r\nlittle ripples on the stream of existence to act as a stop-gap for the\r\npresent.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTEEN\r\n\r\nHe\r\n\r\n\r\nHere goes for a full account of my first, my last, my only _real_\r\nsweetheart, for I considered the professions of that pestiferous\r\njackeroo as merely a grotesque caricature on the genuine article.\r\n\r\nOn making my first appearance before my lover, I looked quite the\r\nreverse of a heroine. My lovely hair was not conveniently escaping from\r\nthe comb at the right moment to catch him hard in the eye, neither was\r\nmy thrillingly low sweet voice floating out on the scented air in a\r\nmanner which went straight to his heart, like the girls I had read of.\r\nOn the contrary, I much resembled a female clown. It was on a day\r\ntowards the end of September, and I had been up the creek making a\r\ncollection of ferns. I had on a pair of men\'s boots with which to walk\r\nin the water, and was garbed in a most dilapidated old dress, which I\r\nhad borrowed from one of the servants for the purpose. A pair of gloves\r\nmade of basil, and a big hat, much torn in struggling through the\r\nundergrowth, completed my make-up. My hair was most unbecomingly screwed\r\nup, the short ends sticking out like a hurrah\'s nest.\r\n\r\nIt was late in the day when, returning from my ramble, I was met on the\r\ndoorstep by aunt Helen.\r\n\r\n"While you are in that trim, I wish you would pluck some lemons for me.\r\nI\'m sure there is no danger of you ruining your turn-out. A sketch of\r\nyou would make a good item for the _Bulletin_," she said.\r\n\r\nI went readily to do her bidding, and fetching a ladder with rungs about\r\ntwo feet six apart, placed it against a lemon-tree at the back of the\r\nhouse, and climbed up.\r\n\r\nHolding a number of lemons in my skirt, I was making a most ungraceful\r\ndescent, when I heard an unknown footstep approaching towards my back.\r\n\r\nPeople came to Caddagat at all hours of the day, so I was not in the\r\nleast disconcerted. Only a tramp, an agent, or a hawker, I bet, I\r\nthought, as I reached my big boot down for another rung of the ladder\r\nwithout turning my head to see whom it might be.\r\n\r\nA pair of strong brown hands encircled my waist, I was tossed up a foot\r\nor so and then deposited lightly on the ground, a masculine voice\r\nsaying, "You\'re a mighty well-shaped young filly--\'a waist rather small,\r\nbut a quarter superb\'."\r\n\r\n"How dare anyone speak to me like that," I thought, as I faced about to\r\nsee who was parodying Gordon. There stood a man I had never before set\r\neyes on, smiling mischievously at me. He was a young man--a very young\r\nman, a bushman tremendously tall and big and sunburnt, with an open\r\npleasant face and chestnut moustache--not at all an awe-inspiring\r\nfellow, in spite of his unusual, though well-proportioned and carried,\r\nheight. I knew it must be Harold Beecham, of Five-Bob Downs, as I had\r\nheard he stood six feet three and a half in his socks.\r\n\r\nI hurriedly let down my dress, the lemons rolling in a dozen directions,\r\nand turned to flee, but that well-formed figure bounded before me with\r\nthe agility of a cat and barred my way.\r\n\r\n"Now, not a step do you go, my fine young blood, until you pick up every\r\njolly lemon and put them away tidily, or I\'ll tell the missus on you as\r\nsure as eggs."\r\n\r\nIt dawned on me that he had mistaken me for one of the servant-girls.\r\nThat wasn\'t bad fun. I determined not to undeceive but to have a lark\r\nwith him. I summed him up as conceited, but not with the disgusting\r\nconceit with which some are afflicted, or perhaps blessed. It was rather\r\nan air of\r\nI-have-always-got-what-I-desire-and-believe,-if-people-fail-it-is-all-their-own-fault,\r\nwhich surrounded him.\r\n\r\n"If you please, sir," I said humbly, "I\'ve gathered them all up, will\r\nyou let me go now."\r\n\r\n"Yes, when you\'ve given me a kiss."\r\n\r\n"Oh, sir, I couldn\'t do that!"\r\n\r\n"Go on, I won\'t poison you. Come now, I\'ll make you."\r\n\r\n"Oh, the missus might catch me."\r\n\r\n"No jolly fear; I\'ll take all the blame if she does."\r\n\r\n"Oh don\'t, sir; let me go, please," I said in such unfeigned distress,\r\nfor I feared he was going to execute his threat, that he laughed and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n"Don\'t be frightened, sissy, I never kiss girls, and I\'m not going to\r\nstart at this time of day, and against their will to boot. You haven\'t\r\nbeen long here, have you? I haven\'t seen you before. Stand out there\r\ntill I see if you\'ve got any grit in you, and then I am done with you."\r\n\r\nI stood in the middle of the yard, the spot he indicated, while he\r\nuncurled his long heavy stock-whip with its big lash and scented myall\r\nhandle. He cracked it round and round my head and arms, but I did not\r\nfeel the least afraid, as I saw at a glance that he was exceedingly\r\ndexterous in the bushman\'s art of handling a stock-whip, and knew, if I\r\nkept perfectly still, I was quite safe. It was thanks to uncle Jay-Jay\r\nthat I was able to bear the operation with unruffled equanimity, as he\r\nwas in the habit of testing my nerves in this way.\r\n\r\n"Well, I never! Not so much as blinked an eyelash! Thoroughbred!" He\r\nsaid after a minute or so, "Where\'s the boss?"\r\n\r\n"In Gool-Gool. He won\'t be home till late."\r\n\r\n"Is Mrs Bossier in?"\r\n\r\n"No, she\'s not, but Mrs Bell is somewhere around in front."\r\n\r\n"Thanks."\r\n\r\nI watched him as he walked away with an easy swinging stride, which\r\nspoke of many long, long days in the saddle. I felt certain as I watched\r\nhim that he had quite forgotten the incident of the little girl with the\r\nlemons.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"Sybylla, hurry up and get dressed. Put on your best bib and tucker, and\r\nI will leave Harry Beecham in your charge, as I want to superintend the\r\nmaking of some of the dishes myself this evening."\r\n\r\n"It\'s too early to put on my evening dress, isn\'t it, auntie?\r\n\r\n"It is rather early; but you can\'t spare time to change twice. Dress\r\nyourself completely; you don\'t know what minute your uncle and his\r\nworship will arrive."\r\n\r\nI had taken a dip in the creek, so had not to bathe, and it took me but\r\na short time to don full war-paint--blue evening dress, satin slippers,\r\nand all. I wore my hair flowing, simply tied with a ribbon. I slipped\r\nout into the passage and called aunt Helen. She came.\r\n\r\n"I\'m ready, auntie. Where is he?"\r\n\r\n"In the dining-room."\r\n\r\n"Come into the drawing-room and call him. I will take charge of him till\r\nyou are at leisure. But, auntie, it will be a long time till dinner--how\r\non earth will I manage him?"\r\n\r\n"Manage him!" she laughed; "he is not at all an obstreperous character."\r\n\r\nWe had reached the drawing-room by this, and I looked at myself in the\r\nlooking-glass while aunt Helen went to summon Harold Augustus Beecham,\r\nbachelor, owner of Five-Bob Downs, Wyambeet, Wallerawang West,\r\nQuat-Quatta, and a couple more stations in New South Wales, besides an\r\nextensive one in Queensland.\r\n\r\nI noticed as he entered the door that since I had seen him he had\r\nwashed, combed his stiff black hair, and divested himself of his hat,\r\nspurs, and whip--his leggings had perforce to remain, as his nether\r\ngarment was a pair of closely fitting grey cloth riding-breeches, which\r\nclearly defined the shapely contour of his lower limbs.\r\n\r\n"Harry, this is Sybylla. I\'m sure you need no further introduction.\r\nExcuse me, I have something on the fire which is likely to burn." And\r\naunt Helen hurried off leaving us facing each other.\r\n\r\nHe stared down at me with undisguised surprise. I looked up at him and\r\nlaughed merrily. The fun was all on my side. He was a great big\r\nman--rich and important. I was a chit--an insignificant nonentity--yet,\r\ndespite his sex, size, and importance, I was complete master of that\r\nsituation, and knew it: thus I laughed.\r\n\r\nI saw that he recognized me again by the dusky red he flushed beneath\r\nhis sun-darkened skin. No doubt he regretted having called me a filly\r\nabove all things. He bowed stiffly, but I held out my hand, saying:\r\n\r\n"Do shake hands. When introduced I always shake hands with anyone I\r\nthink I\'ll like. Besides, I seem to know you well. Just think of all the\r\napples you brought me!"\r\n\r\nHe acceded to my request, holding my hand a deal longer than necessary,\r\nand looking at me helplessly. It amused me greatly, for I saw that it\r\nwas he who did not know how to manage me, and not I that couldn\'t manage\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n"\'Pon my honour, Miss Melvyn, I had no idea it was you, when I said--"\r\nHere he boggled completely, which had the effect of reviving my\r\nlaughter.\r\n\r\n"You had no right to be dressed like that--deceiving a fellow. It wasn\'t\r\nfair."\r\n\r\n"That\'s the best of it. It shows what a larrikin Don Juan sort of\r\ncharacter you are. You can\'t deceive me now if you pretend to be a\r\nvirtuous well-behaved member of society."\r\n\r\n"That is the first time I\'ve ever meddled with any of the kitchen fry,\r\nand, by Jove, it will be the last!" he said energetically. "I\'ve got\r\nmyself into a pretty mess."\r\n\r\n"What nonsense you talk," I replied. "If you say another word about it,\r\nI\'ll write a full account of it and paste it in my scrapbook. But if you\r\ndon\'t worry about it, neither will I. You said nothing very\r\nuncomplimentary; in fact, I was quite flattered."\r\n\r\nI was perched on the high end of a couch, and he was leaning with big\r\ncareless ease on the piano. Had grannie seen me, I would have been\r\nlectured about unladylike behaviour.\r\n\r\n\r\n"What is your uncle at today?" he inquired.\r\n\r\n"He\'s not at anything. He went to Gool-Gool yesterday on the jury. Court\r\nfinishes up today, and he is going to bring the judge home tonight.\r\nThat\'s why I am dressed so carefully," I answered.\r\n\r\n"Good gracious! I never thought of court this time as I wasn\'t called on\r\nthe jury, and for a wonder hadn\'t so much as a case against a Chinaman.\r\nI was going to stay tonight, but can\'t if his worship is going to dine\r\nhere."\r\n\r\n"Why? You\'re surely not afraid of Judge Fossilt? He\'s a very simple old\r\ncustomer."\r\n\r\n"Imagine dining with a judge in this toggery!" and he glanced down his\r\ngreat figure at his riding gear.\r\n\r\n"That doesn\'t matter; he\'s near-sighted. I\'ll get you put at the far end\r\nof the table under my wing. Men don\'t notice dress. If you weren\'t so\r\nbig uncle or Frank Hawden could oblige you."\r\n\r\n"Do you think I could pass muster?"\r\n\r\n"Yes; after I brush you down you\'ll look as spruce as a brass penny.\r\n\r\n"I did brush myself," he answered.\r\n\r\n"You brush yourself!" I retorted. "There\'s a big splash of mud on your\r\nshoulder. You couldn\'t expect to do anything decently, for you\'re only a\r\nman, and men are the uselessest, good-for-nothingest, clumsiest animals\r\nin the world. All they\'re good for is to smoke and swear."\r\n\r\nI fetched a clothes brush.\r\n\r\n"You\'ll have to stand on the table to reach me," he said, looking down\r\nwith amused indulgence.\r\n\r\n"As you are so impertinent you can go dusty," and I tossed the brush\r\naway.\r\n\r\nThe evening was balmy, so I invited him into the garden. He threw his\r\nhandkerchief over my chest, saying I might catch cold, but I scouted the\r\nidea.\r\n\r\nWe wandered into an arbour covered with wistaria, banksia, and Marechal\r\nNiel roses, and I made him a buttonhole.\r\n\r\nA traveller pulled rein in the roadway, and, dismounting, threw his\r\nbridle over a paling of the garden fence while he went inside to try and\r\nbuy a loaf of bread.\r\n\r\nI jumped up, frightening the horse so that it broke away, pulling off\r\nthe paling in the bridle-rein. I ran to bring a hammer to repair the\r\ndamage. Mr Beecham caught the horse while I attempted to drive the nail\r\ninto the fence. It was a futile attempt. I bruised my fingers. He took\r\nthe hammer from me, and fixing the paling in its place with a couple of\r\nwell-aimed blows, said laughingly:\r\n\r\n"You drive a nail! You couldn\'t expect to do anything. You\'re only a\r\ngirl. Girls are the helplessest, uselessest, troublesomest little\r\ncreatures in the world. All they\'re good for is to torment and pester a\r\nfellow."\r\n\r\nI had to laugh.\r\n\r\nAt this juncture we heard uncle Jay-Jay\'s voice, so Mr Beecham went\r\ntowards the back, whence it proceeded, after he left me at the front\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\n"Oh, auntie, we got on splendidly! He\'s not a bit of trouble. We\'re as\r\nchummy as though we had been reared together," I exclaimed.\r\n\r\n"Did you get him to talk?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes."\r\n\r\n"Did you really?" in surprise.\r\n\r\nWhen I came to review the matter I was forced to confess that I had done\r\nall the talking, and young Beecham the listening; moreover I described\r\nhim as the quietest man I had ever seen or heard of.\r\n\r\nThe judge did not come home with uncle Jay-Jay as expected so it was not\r\nnecessary for me to shelter Harold Beecham under my wing. Grannie\r\ngreeted him cordially as "Harold, my boy", he was a great favourite with\r\nher. She and uncle Julius monopolized him for the evening. There was\r\ngreat talk of trucking sheep, the bad outlook as regarded the season,\r\nthe state of the grass in the triangle, the Leigh Spring, the Bimbalong,\r\nand several other paddocks, and of the condition of the London wool\r\nmarket. It did not interest me, so I dived into a book, only\r\noccasionally emerging therefrom to smile at Mr Beecham.\r\n\r\nHe had come to Caddagat for a pair of bullocks which had been fattening\r\nin grannie\'s home paddock. Uncle gave him a start with them next\r\nmorning. When they came out on the road I was standing in a bed of\r\nviolets in a tangled corner of the garden, where roses climbed to kiss\r\nthe lilacs, and spiraea stooped to rest upon the wallflowers, and where\r\ntwo tall kurrajongs stood like sentries over all. Harold Beecham\r\ndismounted, and, leaning over the fence, lingered with me, leaving the\r\nbullocks to uncle Jay-Jay. Uncle raved vigorously. Women, he asserted,\r\nwere the bane of society and the ruination of all men; but he had always\r\nconsidered Harold as too sensible to neglect his business to stand\r\ngrinning at a pesky youngster in short skirts and a pigtail. Which was\r\nthe greatest idiot of the two he didn\'t know.\r\n\r\nHis grumbling did not affect Harold in the least.\r\n\r\n"Complimentary to both of us," he remarked as he leisurely threw himself\r\nacross his great horse, and smiled his pleasant quiet smile, disclosing\r\ntwo rows of magnificent teeth, untainted by contamination with beer or\r\ntobacco. Raising his panama hat with the green fly-veil around it, he\r\ncantered off. I wondered as I watched him if anything ever disturbed his\r\nserenity, and desired to try. He looked too big and quiet to be ruffled\r\nby such emotions as rage, worry, jealousy, or even love. Returning to\r\nthe house, I put aunt Helen through an exhaustive catechism concerning\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n_Question._ Auntie, what age is Harold Beecham?\r\n\r\n_Answer._ Twenty-five last December.\r\n\r\n_Q._ Did he ever have any brothers or sisters?\r\n\r\n_A._ No. His birth caused his mother\'s death. Q. How long has his father\r\nbeen dead?\r\n\r\n_A._ Since Harold could crawl.\r\n\r\n_Q._ Who reared him?\r\n\r\nA. His aunts.\r\n\r\n_Q._ Does he ever talk any more than that? A. Often a great deal less.\r\n\r\n_Q._ Is he really very rich?\r\n\r\n_A._ If he manages to pull through these seasons he will be second to\r\nnone but Tyson in point of wealth.\r\n\r\n_Q._ Is Five-Bob a very pretty place?\r\n\r\n_A._ Yes; one of the show places of the district. Q. Does he often come\r\nto Caddagat?\r\n\r\n_A._ Yes, he often drops in.\r\n\r\n_Q._ What makes his hair so black and his moustache that light colour?\r\n\r\nA. You\'ll have to study science to find that out. I\'m sure I can\'t tell\r\nyou.\r\n\r\n_Q._ Does he--?\r\n\r\n"Now, Sybylla," said auntie, laughing, "you are taking a suspicious\r\ninterest in my sunburnt young giant. Did I not tell you he was taking\r\ntime by the forelock when he brought the apples?"\r\n\r\n"Oh, auntie, I am only asking questions because--"\r\n\r\n"Yes, because, because, I understand perfectly. Because you are a girl,\r\nand all the girls fall a victim to Harry\'s charms at once. If you don\'t\r\nwant to succumb meekly to your fate, \'Heed the spark or you may dread\r\nthe fire.\' That is the only advice I can tender you."\r\n\r\nThis was a Thursday, and on the following Sunday Harold Beecham\r\nreappeared at Caddagat and remained from three in the afternoon until\r\nnine at night. Uncle Julius and Frank Hawden were absent. The weather\r\nhad taken a sudden backward lurch into winter again, so we had a fire.\r\nHarold sat beside it all the time, and interposed yes and no at the\r\nproper intervals in grannie\'s brisk business conversation, but he never\r\naddressed one word to me beyond "Good afternoon, Miss Melvyn," on his\r\narrival, and "Good night, Miss Melvyn," when leaving.\r\n\r\nI studied him attentively all the while. What were his ideas and\r\nsentiments it were hard to tell: he never expressed any. He was\r\nfearfully and wonderfully quiet. Yet his was an intelligent silence, not\r\nof that wooden brainless description which casts a damper on company,\r\nneither was it of the morose or dreaming order.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER FOURTEEN\r\n\r\nPrincipally Letters\r\n\r\n\r\nCaddagat, 29th Sept., 1896\r\n\r\nMy dearest Gertie,\r\n\r\nI have started to write no less than seven letters to you, but something\r\nalways interrupted me and I did not finish them. However, I\'ll finish\r\nthis one in the teeth of Father Peter himself. I will parenthesize all\r\nthe interruptions. (A traveller just asked me for a rose. I had to get\r\nup and give him one.) Living here is lovely. (Another man inquired the\r\nway to Somingley Gap, and I\'ve just finished directing him.) Grannie is\r\nterribly nice. You could not believe. She is always giving me something,\r\nand takes me wherever she goes. Auntie is an angel. I wish you could\r\nhear the piano. It is a beauty. There are dozens of papers and books to\r\nread. Uncle is a dear old fellow. You should hear him rave and swear\r\nsometimes when he gets in a rage. It is great fun. He brings me lollies,\r\ngloves, ribbons, or something every time he comes from town. (Two Indian\r\nhawkers have arrived, and I am going out to see their goods. There were\r\nnineteen hawkers here last week. I am sitting on a squatter\'s chair and\r\nwriting on a table in the veranda, and the road goes right by the\r\nflower-garden. That is how I see everyone.) Have you had rain down there\r\nthis week? They have great squawking about the drought up here. I wish\r\nthey could see Goulburn, and then they\'d know what drought means. I\r\ndon\'t know what sort of a bobberie they would kick up. It\'s pretty dry\r\nout on the run, but everyone calls the paddocks about the house an\r\noasis. You see there are such splendid facilities for irrigation here.\r\nUncle has put on a lot of men. They have cut races between the two\r\ncreeks between which the house is situated. Every now and again they let\r\nthe water from these over the orchard gardens and about a hundred acres\r\nof paddock land around the house. The grass therein is up to the horses\'\r\nfetlocks. There is any amount of rhubarb and early vegetables in the\r\ngarden. Grannie says there is a splendid promise of fruit in the\r\norchard, and the flower-garden is a perfect dream. This is the dearest\r\nold place in the world. Dozens of people plague grannie to be let put\r\ntheir horses in the grass--especially shearers, there are droves of them\r\ngoing home now--but she won\'t let them; wants all the grass for her own\r\nstock. Uncle has had to put another man on to mind it, or at night all\r\nthe wires are cut and the horses put in. (An agent, I think by the cut\r\nof him, is asking for grannie. I\'ll have to run and find her.) It is\r\nvery lively here. Never a night but we have the house full of agents or\r\ntravellers of one sort or another, and there are often a dozen swaggies\r\nin the one day.\r\n\r\nHarold Beecham is my favourite of all the men hereaway. He is\r\ndelightfully big and quiet. He isn\'t good-looking, but I like his face.\r\n(Been attending to the demands of a couple of impudent swaggies. Being\r\noff the road at Possum Gully, you escape them.) For the love of life,\r\nnext time you write, fire into the news at once and don\'t half-fill your\r\nletter telling me about the pen and your bad writing. I am scribbling at\r\nthe rate of 365 miles an hour, and don\'t care a jot whether it is good\r\nwriting or not.\r\n\r\nAuntie, uncle, Frank Hawden and I, are going to ride to Yabtree church\r\nnext Sunday. It is four miles beyond Five-Bob Downs, so that is sixteen\r\nmiles. It is the nearest church. I expect it will be rare fun. There\r\nwill be such a crowd coming home, and that always makes the horses\r\ndelightfully frisky. (A man wants to put his horses in the paddock for\r\nthe night, so I will have to find uncle.) I never saw such a place for\r\nmen. It is all men, men, men. You cannot go anywhere outside the house\r\nbut you see men coming and going in all directions. It wouldn\'t do to\r\nundress without bothering to drop the window-blind like we used at\r\nPossum Gully. Grannie and uncle say it is a curse to be living beside\r\nthe road, as it costs them a tremendous lot a year. There are seven\r\nlemon-trees here, loaded (another hawker). I hope you think of me\r\nsometimes. I am just as ugly as ever. (A traveller wants to buy a loaf\r\nof bread.)\r\n\r\nWith stacks of love to all at home, and a whole dray-load for yourself,\r\nfrom your loving sister,\r\n\r\nSybylla.\r\n\r\n\r\nRemember me to Goulburn, drowsing lazily in its dreamy graceful hollow\r\nin the blue distance.\r\n\r\n\r\nCaddagat, 29th Sept., 1896\r\n\r\nDear Everard,\r\n\r\nThank you very much for the magazines and "An Australian Bush Track". I\r\nsuppose you have quite forgotten us and Caddagat by this time. The sun\r\nhas sunk behind the gum-trees, and the blue evening mists are hanging\r\nlazily in the hollows of the hills. I expect you are donning your\r\n"swallow-tail" preparatory to leading some be-satined "faire ladye" in\r\nto a gorgeous dinner, thence to the play, then to a dance probably. No\r\ndoubt all around you is bustle, glare of lights, noise, and fun. It is\r\nsuch a different scene here. From down the road comes the tinkle of\r\ncamp-bells and jingle of hobble-chains. From down in that sheltered\r\nangle where the creek meets the river comes the gleam of camp-fires\r\nthrough the gathering twilight, and I can see several tents rigged for\r\nthe night, looking like white specks in the distance.\r\n\r\nI long for the time to come when I shall get to Sydney. I\'m going to\r\nlead you and aunt Helen a pretty dance. You\'ll have to keep going night\r\nand day. It will be great. I must get up and dance a jig on the veranda\r\nwhen I think of it. You\'ll have to show me everything--slums and all. I\r\nwant to find out the truth of heaps of things for myself.\r\n\r\nSave for the weird rush of the stream and the kookaburras\' good-night,\r\nall is still, with a mighty far-reaching stillness which can be felt.\r\nNow the curlews are beginning their wild moaning cry. From the rifts in\r\nthe dark lone ranges, far down the river, it comes like a hunted spirit\r\nuntil it makes me feel--\r\n\r\n\r\nAt this point I said, "Bah! I\'m mad to write to Everard Grey like this.\r\nHe would laugh and call me a poor little fool." I tore the half-finished\r\nletter to shreds, and consigned it to the kitchen fire. I substituted a\r\nprim formal note, merely thanking him for the books and magazine he had\r\nsent me. To this I never received an answer. I heard through his letters\r\nto grannie that he was much occupied. Had been to Brisbane and Melbourne\r\non important cases, so very likely had not time to be bothered with me;\r\nor, he might have been like the majority of his fellows who make a great\r\nparade of friendship while with one, then go away and forget one\'s\r\nexistence in an hour.\r\n\r\nWhile at Caddagat there were a few duties allotted to me. One of these\r\nwas to attend to the drawing-room; another was to find uncle Jay-Jay\'s\r\nhat when he mislaid it--often ten times per day. I assisted my\r\ngrandmother to make up her accounts and write business letters, and I\r\nattended to tramps. A man was never refused a bit to eat at Caddagat.\r\nThis necessitated the purchase of an extra ton of flour per year, also\r\nnearly a ton of sugar, to say nothing of tea, potatoes, beef, and all\r\nbroken meats which went thus. This was not reckoning the consumption of\r\nvictuals by the other class of travellers with which the house was\r\ngenerally full year in and year out. Had there been any charge for their\r\nboard and lodging, the Bossiers would surely have made a fortune. I\r\ninterviewed on an average fifty tramps a week, and seldom saw the same\r\nman twice. What a great army they were! Hopeless, homeless, aimless,\r\nshameless souls, tramping on from north to south, and east to west,\r\nnever relinquishing their heart-sickening, futile quest for work--some\r\nof them so long on the tramp that the ambitions of manhood had been\r\nground out of them, and they wished for nothing more than this.\r\n\r\nThere were all shapes, sizes, ages, kinds, and conditions of men--the\r\nshamefaced boy in the bud of his youth, showing by the way he begged\r\nthat the humiliation of the situation had not yet worn off, and poor old\r\ncreatures tottering on the brink of the grave, with nothing left in life\r\nbut the enjoyment of beer and tobacco. There were strong men in their\r\nprime who really desired work when they asked for it, and skulking\r\ncowards who hoped they would not get it. There were the diseased, the\r\neducated, the ignorant, the deformed, the blind, the evil, the honest,\r\nthe mad, and the sane. Some in real professional beggars\' style called\r\ndown blessings on me; others were morose and glum, while some were\r\nimpudent and thankless, and said to supply them with food was just what\r\nI should do, for the swagmen kept the squatters--as, had the squatters\r\nnot monopolized the land, the swagmen would have had plenty. A moiety of\r\nthe last-mentioned--dirty, besotted, ragged creatures--had a glare in\r\ntheir eyes which made one shudder to look at them, and, while\r\nspasmodically twirling their billies or clenching their fists, talked\r\nwildly of making one to "bust up the damn banks", or to drive all the\r\npresent squatters out of the country and put the people on the\r\nland--clearly showing that, because they had failed for one reason or\r\nanother, it had maddened them to see others succeed.\r\n\r\nIn a wide young country of boundless resources, why is this thing? This\r\nquestion worried me. Our legislators are unable or unwilling to cope\r\nwith it. They trouble not to be patriots and statesmen. Australia can\r\nbring forth writers, orators, financiers, singers, musicians, actors,\r\nand athletes which are second to none of any nation under the sun. Why\r\ncan she not bear sons, men of soul, mind, truth, godliness, and\r\npatriotism sufficient to rise and cast off the grim shackles which widen\r\nround us day by day?\r\n\r\nI was the only one at Caddagat who held these silly ideas. Harold\r\nBeecham, uncle Julius, grannie, and Frank Hawden did not worry about the\r\ncause of tramps. They simply termed them a lazy lot of sneaking\r\ncreatures, fed them, and thought no more of the matter.\r\n\r\nI broached the subject to uncle Jay-Jay once, simply to discover his\r\nideas thereon.\r\n\r\nI was sitting on a chair in the veranda sewing; he, with his head on a\r\ncushion, was comfortably stretched on a rug on the floor.\r\n\r\n"Uncle Boss, why can\'t something be done for tramps?"\r\n\r\n"How done for \'em?"\r\n\r\n"Couldn\'t some means of employing them be arrived at?"\r\n\r\n"Work!" he ejaculated. "That\'s the very thing the crawling divils are\r\nterrified they might get."\r\n\r\n"Yes; but couldn\'t some law be made to help them?"\r\n\r\n"A law to make me cut up Caddagat and give ten of \'em each a piece, and\r\ngo on the wallaby myself, I suppose?"\r\n\r\n"No, uncle; but there was a poor young fellow here this morning who, I\r\nfeel sure, was in earnest when he asked for work."\r\n\r\n"Helen!" bawled uncle Jay-Jay.\r\n\r\n"Well, what is it?" she inquired, appearing in the doorway.\r\n\r\n"Next time Sybylla is giving a tramp some tucker, you keep a sharp eye\r\non her or she will be sloping one of these days. There was a young\r\nfellow here today with a scarlet moustache and green eyes, and she\'s\r\ndean gone on him, and has been bullying me to give him half Caddagat."\r\n\r\n"What a disgusting thing to say! Uncle, you ought to be ashamed of\r\nyourself," I exclaimed.\r\n\r\n"Very well, I\'ll be careful," said aunt Helen, departing.\r\n\r\n"What with the damned flies, and the tramps, and a pesky thing called\r\nSybylla, a man\'s life ain\'t worth a penny to him," said uncle.\r\n\r\nWe fell into silence, which was broken presently by a dirty red-bearded\r\nface appearing over the garden gate, and a man\'s voice:\r\n\r\n"Good day, boss! Give us a chew of tobaccer?"\r\n\r\n"I\'m not the boss," said uncle with assumed fierceness.\r\n\r\n"Then who is?" inquired the man.\r\n\r\nUncle pointed his thumb at me, and, rolling out on the floor again as\r\nthough very sleepy, began to snore. The tramp grinned, and made his\r\nrequest of me. I took him round to the back, served him with flour,\r\nbeef, and an inch or two of rank tobacco out of a keg which had been\r\nbought for the purpose. Refusing a drink of milk which I offered, he\r\nresumed his endless tramp with a "So long, little missy. God bless your\r\npleasant face."\r\n\r\nI watched him out of sight. One of my brothers--one of God\'s children\r\nunder the Southern Cross. Did these old fellows really believe in the\r\nGod whose name they mentioned so glibly? I wondered. But I am thankful\r\nthat while at Caddagat it was only rarely that my old top-heavy thoughts\r\ntroubled me. Life was so pleasant that I was content merely to be\r\nyoung--a chit in the first flush of teens, health, hope, happiness,\r\nyouth--a heedless creature recking not for the morrow.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER FIFTEEN\r\n\r\nWhen the Heart is Young\r\n\r\n\r\nAbout a week or so after I first met Harold Beecham, aunt Helen allowed\r\nme to read a letter she had received from the elder of the two Misses\r\nBeecham. It ran as follows:\r\n\r\n\r\n"My dearest Helen,\r\n\r\n"This is a begging letter, and I am writing another to your mother at\r\nthe same time. I am asking her to allow her grand-daughter to spend a\r\nfew weeks with me, and I want you to use your influence in the matter.\r\nSarah has not been well lately, and is going to Melbourne for a change,\r\nand as I will be lonely while she is away Harold insists upon me having\r\nsomeone to keep me company--you know how considerate the dear boy is. I\r\nhardly like to ask you to spare your little girl to me. It must be a\r\ngreat comfort to have her. I could have got Miss Benson to stay with me,\r\nbut Harold will not hear of her. He says she is too slow, and would give\r\nus both the mopes. But he says your little niece will keep us all alive.\r\nJulius was telling me the other day that he could not part with her, as\r\nshe makes \'the old barracks\', as he always calls Caddagat, echo with fun\r\nand noise. I am so looking forward to seeing her, as she is dear Lucy\'s\r\nchild. Give her my love," etc., etc., and as a postscript the letter\r\nhad--"Harold will go up for Sybylla on Wednesday afternoon. I do hope\r\nyou will be able to spare her to me for a while."\r\n\r\n\r\n"Oh, auntie, how lovely!" I exclaimed. "What are you laughing at?"\r\n\r\n"For whom do you think Harry wants the companion? It is nice to have an\r\nold auntie, as a blind, is it not? Well, all is fair in love and war.\r\nYou have permission to use me in any way you like."\r\n\r\nI pretended to miss her meaning.\r\n\r\nGrannie consented to Miss Beecham\'s proposal, and ere the day arrived I\r\nhad a trunk packed with some lovely new dresses, and was looking forward\r\nwith great glee to my visit to Five-Bob Downs.\r\n\r\nOne o\'clock on Wednesday afternoon arrived; two o\'clock struck, and I\r\nwas beginning to fear no one was coming for me, when, turning to look\r\nout the window for the eighteenth time, I saw the straight blunt nose of\r\nHarold Beecham passing. Grannie was serving afternoon tea on the\r\nveranda. I did not want any, so got ready while my escort was having\r\nhis.\r\n\r\nIt was rather late when we bowled away at a tremendous pace in a red\r\nsulky, my portmanteau strapped on at the back, and a thoroughbred\r\nAmerican trotter, which had taken prizes at Sydney shows, harnessed to\r\nthe front. We just whizzed! It was splendid! The stones and dust rose in\r\na thick cloud from the whirling wheels and flying hoofs, and the posts\r\nof the wire fence on our left passed like magic as we went. Mr Beecham\r\nallowed me to drive after a time while he sat ready to take the reins\r\nshould an emergency arise.\r\n\r\nIt was sunset--most majestic hour of the twenty-four--when we drove up\r\nto the great white gates which opened into the avenue leading to the\r\nmain homestead of Five-Bob Downs station--beautiful far-reaching\r\nFive-Bob Downs! Dreamy blue hills rose behind, and wide rich flats\r\nstretched before, through which the Yarrangung river, glazed with\r\nsunset, could be seen like a silver snake winding between shrubberied\r\nbanks. The odour from the six-acred flower-garden was overpowering and\r\ndelightful. A breeze gently swayed the crowd of trees amid the houses,\r\nand swept over the great orchard which sloped down from the south side\r\nof the houses. In the fading sunlight thirty iron roofs gleamed and\r\nglared, and seemed like a little town; and the yelp of many dogs went up\r\nat the sound of our wheels. Ah! beautiful, beautiful Five-Bob Downs!\r\n\r\nIt seemed as though a hundred dogs leapt forth to greet us when that\r\ngate flew open, but I subsequently discovered there were but\r\ntwenty-three.\r\n\r\nTwo female figures came out to meet us--one nearly six feet high, the\r\nother, a tiny creature, seemed about eighteen inches, though, of course,\r\nwas more than that.\r\n\r\n"I\'ve brought her, aunt Gussie," said Harold, jumping out of the sulky,\r\nthough not relinquishing the reins, while he kissed the taller figure,\r\nand the small one attached itself to his leg saying, "Dimme wide."\r\n\r\n"Hullo! Possum, why wasn\'t old Spanker let go? I see he\'s not among the\r\ndogs," and my host picked the tiny individual up in his arms and got\r\ninto the sulky to give her the desired ride, while after being embraced\r\nby Miss Beecham and lifted to the ground by her nephew, I went with the\r\nformer over an asphalted tennis-court, through the wide garden, then\r\nacross a broad veranda into the great, spreading, one-storeyed house\r\nfrom which gleamed many lights.\r\n\r\n"I am so glad you have come, my dear. I must have a good look at you\r\nwhen we get into the light. I hope you are like your mother."\r\n\r\nThis prospect discomfited me. I knew she would find a very ugly girl\r\nwith not the least resemblance to her pretty mother, and I cursed my\r\nappearance under my breath.\r\n\r\n"Your name is Sybylla," Miss Beecham continued, "Sybylla Penelope. Your\r\nmother used to be very dear to me, but I don\'t know why she doesn\'t\r\nwrite to me now. I have never seen her since her marriage. It seems\r\nstrange to think of her as the mother of eight--five boys and three\r\ngirls, is it not?"\r\n\r\nMiss Beecham had piloted me through a wide hall and along an extended\r\npassage out of which a row of bedrooms opened, into one of which we\r\nwent.\r\n\r\n"I hope you will be comfortable here, child. You need not dress for\r\ndinner while you are here; we never do, only on very special occasions."\r\n\r\n"Neither do we at Caddagat," I replied.\r\n\r\n"Now, child, let me have a good look at you without your hat."\r\n\r\n"Oh, please don\'t!" I exclaimed, covering my face with my hands. "I am\r\nso dreadfully ugly that I cannot bear to have anyone look at me."\r\n\r\n"What a silly little girl! You are not like your mother, but you are not\r\nat all plain-looking. Harold says you are the best style of girl he has\r\nseen yet, and sing beautifully. He got a tuner up from Sydney last week,\r\nso we will expect you to entertain us every night."\r\n\r\nI learnt that what Harold pronounced good no one dared gainsay at\r\nFive-Bob Downs.\r\n\r\nWe proceeded direct to the dining-room, and had not been there long when\r\nMr Beecham entered with the little girl on his shoulder. Miss Beecham\r\nhad told me she was Minnie Benson, daughter of Harold\'s married overseer\r\non Wyambeet, his adjoining station. Miss Beecham considered it would\r\nhave been more seemly for her nephew to have selected a little boy as a\r\nplay-thing, but his sentiments regarding boys were that they were\r\nmachines invented for the torment of adults.\r\n\r\n"Well, O\'Doolan, what sort of a day has it been?" Harold inquired,\r\nsetting his human toy upon the floor.\r\n\r\n"Fine wezzer for yim duts," she promptly replied.\r\n\r\n"Harold, it is shameful to teach a little innocent child such abominable\r\nslang; and you might give her a decent nickname," said Miss Beecham.\r\n\r\n"O\'Doolan, this is Miss Melvyn, and you have to do the same to her as\r\nyou do to me."\r\n\r\nThe little thing held out her arms to me. I took her up, and she hugged\r\nand kissed me, saying:\r\n\r\n"I luz oo, I luz oo," and turning to Mr Beecham, "zat anuff?\r\n\r\n"Yes, that will do," he said; and she struggled to be put down.\r\n\r\nThree jackeroos, an overseer, and two other young men came in, were\r\nintroduced to me, and then we began dinner.\r\n\r\nO\'Doolan sat on a high chair beside Mr Beecham, and he attended to all\r\nher wants. She did everything he did, even taking mustard, and was very\r\nbrave at quelling the tears that rose to the doll-like blue eyes. When\r\nMr Beecham wiped his moustache, it was amusing to see her also wipe an\r\nimaginary one.\r\n\r\nAfter dinner the jackeroos and the three other men repaired to a\r\nsitting-room in the backyard, which was specially set apart for them,\r\nand where they amused themselves as they liked. My host and hostess,\r\nmyself, and the child, spent the evening in a tiny sitting-room\r\nadjoining the dining-room. Miss Beecham entertained me with conversation\r\nand the family albums, and Harold amused himself entirely with the\r\nchild.\r\n\r\nOnce when they were absent for a few minutes, Miss Beecham told me it\r\nwas ridiculous the way he fussed with the child, and that he had her\r\nwith him more than half his time. She also asked me what I thought of\r\nher nephew. I evaded the question by querying if he was always so quiet\r\nand good-tempered.\r\n\r\n"Oh dear, no. He is considered a particularly bad-tempered man. Not one\r\nof the snarling nasty tempers, but--"\r\n\r\nHere the re-entry of the owner of the temper put a stop to this\r\nconversation.\r\n\r\nHarold gave O\'Doolan rides on his back, going on all-fours. She shouted\r\nin childish glee, and wound up by curling her small proportions on his\r\nbroad chest, and going to sleep there.\r\n\r\nMrs Benson had sent for little O\'Doolan, and Harold took her home next\r\nday. He invited me to accompany him, so we set out in the sulky with\r\nO\'Doolan on my lap. It was a pleasant drive of twelve miles to and from\r\nWyambeet. O\'Doolan was much distressed at parting from Mr Beecham, but\r\nhe promised to come for her again shortly.\r\n\r\n"One little girl at a time is enough for me to care for properly," he\r\nsaid to me in the winning manner with which, and his wealth,\r\nunintentionally and unconsciously made slaughter among the hearts of the\r\nfair sex.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER SIXTEEN\r\n\r\nWhen Fortune Smiles\r\n\r\n\r\n"Now, Harold, you have compelled Sybylla to come here, you must not let\r\nthe time drag with her," said Miss Beecham.\r\n\r\nIt was the second day after my arrival at Five-Bob. Lunch was over, and\r\nwe had adjourned to the veranda. Miss Beecham was busy at her\r\nwork-table; I was ensconced on a mat on the floor reading a book; Harold\r\nwas stretched in a squatter\'s chair some distance away. His big brown\r\nhands were clasped behind his head, his chin rested on his broad chest,\r\nhis eyes were closed, he occasionally thrust his lower lip forward and\r\nsent a puff of breath upwards to scatter the flies from his face; he\r\nlooked a big monument of comfort, and answered his aunt\'s remarks\r\nlazily:\r\n\r\n"Yes, aunt, I\'ll do my best;" and to me, "Miss Melvyn, while here,\r\nplease bear in mind that it will be no end of pleasure to me to do\r\nanything for your enjoyment. Don\'t fail to command me in any way."\r\n\r\n"Thank you, Mr Beecham. I will not fail to avail myself of your offer."\r\n\r\n"The absurdity of you two children addressing each other so formally,"\r\nsaid Miss Beecham. "Why, you are a sort of cousins almost, by right of\r\nold friendship between the families. You must call me aunt."\r\n\r\nAfter this Mr Beecham and I called each other nothing when in Miss\r\nBeecham\'s hearing, but adhered to formality on other occasions.\r\n\r\nHarold looked so comfortable and lazy that I longed to test how far he\r\nmeant the offer he had made me.\r\n\r\n"I\'m just dying for a row on the river. Would you oblige me?" I said.\r\n\r\n"Just look at the thermometer!" exclaimed Miss Augusta. "Wait till it\r\ngets cooler, child."\r\n\r\n"Oh, I love the heat!" I replied. "And I am sure it won\'t hurt his\r\nlordship. He\'s used to the sun, to judge from all appearances."\r\n\r\n"Yes, I don\'t think it can destroy my complexion," he said\r\ngood-humouredly, rubbing his finger and thumb along his stubble-covered\r\nchin. The bushmen up-country shaved regularly every Sunday morning, but\r\nnever during the week for anything less than a ball. They did this to\r\nobviate the blue--what they termed "scraped pig"--appearance of the\r\nfaces of city men in the habit of using the razor daily, and to which\r\nthey preferred the stubble of a seven-days\' beard. "I\'ll take you to the\r\nriver in half an hour," he said, rising from his seat. "First I must\r\nstick on one of Warrigal\'s shoes that he\'s flung. I want him tomorrow,\r\nand must do it at once, as he always goes lame if ridden immediately\r\nafter shoeing."\r\n\r\n"Shall I blow the bellows?" I volunteered.\r\n\r\n"Oh no, thanks. I can manage myself. It would be better though if I had\r\nsome one. But I can get one of the girls."\r\n\r\n"Can\'t you get one of the boys?" said his aunt.\r\n\r\n"There\'s not one in. I sent every one off to the Triangle paddock today\r\nto do some drafting. They all took their quart pots and a snack in their\r\nsaddle-bags, and won\'t be home till dark."\r\n\r\n"Let me go," I persisted; "I often blow the bellows for uncle Jay-Jay,\r\nand think it great fun."\r\n\r\nThe offer of my services being accepted, we set out.\r\n\r\nHarold took his favourite horse, Warrigal, from the stable, and led him\r\nto the blacksmith\'s forge under an open, stringybark-roofed shed, nearly\r\ncovered with creepers. He lit a fire and put a shoe in it. Doffing his\r\ncoat and hat, rolling up his shirt-sleeves, and donning a leather apron,\r\nhe began preparing the horse\'s hoof.\r\n\r\nWhen an emergency arose that necessitated uncle Jay-Jay shoeing his\r\nhorses himself. I always manipulated the bellows, and did so with great\r\ndecorum, as he was very exacting and I feared his displeasure. In this\r\ncase it was different. I worked the pole with such energy that it almost\r\nblew the whole fire out of the pan, and sent the ashes and sparks in a\r\nwhirlwind around Harold. The horse--a touchy beast--snorted and dragged\r\nhis foot from his master\'s grasp.\r\n\r\n"That the way to blow?" I inquired demurely.\r\n\r\n"Take things a little easier," he replied.\r\n\r\nI took them so very easily that the fire was on the last gasp and the\r\nshoe nearly cold when it was required.\r\n\r\n"This won\'t do," said Beecham.\r\n\r\nI recommenced blowing with such force that he had to retreat.\r\n\r\n"Steady I steady!" he shouted.\r\n\r\n"Sure O\'i can\'t plaze yez anyhows," I replied.\r\n\r\n"If you don\'t try to plaze me directly I\'ll punish you in a way you\r\nwon\'t relish," he said laughingly. But I knew he was thinking of a\r\npunishment which I would have secretly enjoyed.\r\n\r\n"If you don\'t let me finish this work I\'ll make one of the men do it\r\ntonight by candle-light when they come home tired. I know you wouldn\'t\r\nlike them to do that," he continued.\r\n\r\n"Arrah, go on, ye\'re only tazin\'!" I retorted. "Don\'t you remember\r\ntelling me that Warrigal was such a nasty-tempered brute that he allowed\r\nno one but yourself to touch him?"\r\n\r\n"Oh well, then, I\'m floored, and will have to put up with the\r\nconsequences," he good-humouredly made answer.\r\n\r\nSeeing that my efforts to annoy him failed, I gave in, and we were soon\r\ndone, and then started for the river--Mr Beecham clad in a khaki suit\r\nand I in a dainty white wrapper and flyaway sort of hat. In one hand my\r\nhost held a big white umbrella, with which he shaded me from the hot\r\nrays of the October sun, and in the other was a small basket containing\r\ncake and lollies for our delectation.\r\n\r\nHaving traversed the half-mile between the house and river, we pushed\r\noff from the bank in a tiny boat just big enough for two. In the teeth\r\nof Harold\'s remonstrance I persisted in dangling over the boat-side to\r\ndabble in the clear, deep, running water. In a few minutes we were in\r\nit. Being unable to swim, but for my companion it would have been all up\r\nwith me. When I rose to the surface he promptly seized me, and without\r\nmuch effort, clothes and all, swam with me to the bank, where we\r\nlanded--a pair of sorry figures. Harold had mud all over his nose, and\r\nin general looked very ludicrous. As soon as I could stand I laughed.\r\n\r\n"Oh, for a snapshot of you!" I said.\r\n\r\n"We might have both been drowned," he said sternly.\r\n\r\n"Mights don\'t fly," I returned. "And it was worth the dip to see you\r\nlooking such a comical article." We were both minus our hats.\r\n\r\nHis expression relaxed.\r\n\r\n"I believe you would laugh at your own funeral. If I look queer, you\r\nlook forty times worse. Run for your life and get a hot bath and a drop\r\nof spirits or you\'ll catch your death of cold. Aunt Augusta will take a\r\nfit and tie you up for the rest of the time in case something more will\r\nhappen to you."\r\n\r\n"Catch a death of cold!" I ejaculated. "It is only good, pretty little\r\ngirls, who are a blessing to everyone, who die for such trifles; girls\r\nlike I am always live till nearly ninety, to plague themselves and\r\neverybody else. I\'ll sneak home so that your aunt won\'t see me, and no\r\none need be a bit the wiser."\r\n\r\n"You\'ll be sun-struck!" he said in dismay.\r\n\r\n"Take care you don\'t get daughter-struck," I said perkily, turning to\r\nflee, for it had suddenly dawned upon me that my thin wet clothing was\r\noutlining my figure rather too clearly for propriety.\r\n\r\nBy a circuitous way I managed to reach my bedroom unseen. It did not\r\ntake me long to change my clothes, hang them to dry, and appear on the\r\nmain veranda where Miss Augusta was still sewing. I picked up the book I\r\nhad left on the mat, and, taking up a position in a hammock near her, I\r\ncommenced to read.\r\n\r\n"You did not stay long at the river," she remarked. "Have you been\r\nwashing your head? I never saw the like of it. Such a mass of it. It\r\nwill take all day to dry."\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later Harold appeared dressed in a warm suit of tweed. He\r\nwas looking pale and languid, as though he had caught a chill, and\r\nshivered as he threw himself on a lounge. I was feeling none the worse\r\nfor my immersion.\r\n\r\n"Why did you change your clothes, Harold? You surely weren\'t cold on a\r\nday like this. Sybylla has changed hers too, when I come to notice it,\r\nand her hair is wet. Have you had an accident?" said Miss Augusta,\r\nrising from her chair in a startled manner.\r\n\r\n"Rubbish!" ejaculated Harold in a tone which forbade further\r\nquestioning, and the matter dropped.\r\n\r\nShe presently left the veranda, and I took the opportunity to say, "It\r\nis yourself that requires the hot bath and a drop of spirits, Mr\r\nBeecham."\r\n\r\n"Yes; I think I\'ll take a good stiff nobbler. I feel a trifle squeamish.\r\nIt gave me a bit of a turn when I rose to the top and could not see you.\r\nI was afraid the boat might have stunned you in capsizing, and you would\r\nbe drowned before I could find you."\r\n\r\n"Yes; I would have been such a loss to the world in general if I had\r\nbeen drowned," I said satirically.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nSeveral jackeroos, a neighbouring squatter, and a couple of bicycle\r\ntourists turned up at Five-Bob that evening, and we had a jovial night.\r\nThe great, richly furnished drawing-room was brilliantly lighted, and\r\nthe magnificent Erard grand piano sang and rang again with music, now\r\nmartial and loud, now soft and solemn, now gay and sparkling. I made the\r\nvery pleasant discovery that Harold Beecham was an excellent pianist, a\r\ngifted player on the violin, and sang with a strong, clear, well-trained\r\ntenor, which penetrated far into the night. How many, many times I have\r\nlived those nights over again! The great room with its rich\r\nappointments, the superb piano, the lights, the merriment, the breeze\r\nfrom the east, rich with the heavy intoxicating perfume of countless\r\nflowers; the tall perfect figure, holding the violin with a master hand,\r\nmaking it speak the same language as I read in the dark eyes of the\r\nmusician, while above and around was the soft warmth of an Australian\r\nsummer night.\r\n\r\nAh, health and wealth, happiness and youth, joy and light, life and\r\nlove! What a warm-hearted place is the world, how full of pleasure,\r\ngood, and beauty, when fortune smiles! _When fortune smiles!_\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nFortune did smile, and broadly, in those days. We played tricks on one\r\nanother, and had a deal of innocent fun and frolic. I was a little\r\nstartled one night on retiring to find a huge goanna near the head of my\r\nbed. I called Harold to dislodge the creature, when it came to light\r\nthat it was roped to the bedpost. Great was the laughter at my expense.\r\nWho tethered the goanna I never discovered, but I suspected Harold. In\r\nreturn for this joke, I collected all the portable clocks in the\r\nhouse--about twenty--and arrayed them on his bedroom table. The majority\r\nof them were Waterburys for common use, so I set each alarm for a\r\ndifferent hour. Inscribing a placard "Hospital for Insane", I erected it\r\nabove his door. Next morning I was awakened at three o\'clock by fifteen\r\nalarms in concert outside my door. When an hour or two later I emerged I\r\nfound a notice on my door, "This way to the Zoo".\r\n\r\nIt was a very busy time for the men at Five-Bob. Waggons were arriving\r\nwith shearing supplies, for it was drawing nigh unto the great event of\r\nthe year. In another week\'s time the bleat of thousands of sheep, and\r\nthe incense of much tar and wool, would be ascending to the heavens from\r\nthe vicinity of Five-Bob Downs. I was looking forward to the shearing.\r\nThere never was any at Caddagat. Uncle did not keep many sheep, and\r\nalways sold them long-woolled and rebought after shearing.\r\n\r\nI had not much opportunity of persecuting Harold during the daytime. He\r\nand all his subordinates were away all day, busy drafting, sorting, and\r\notherwise pottering with sheep. But I always, and Miss Augusta\r\nsometimes, went to meet them coming home in the evening. It was great\r\nfun. The dogs yelped and jumped about. The men were dirty with much\r\ndust, and smelt powerfully of sheep, and had worked hard all day in the\r\nblazing sun, but they were never too tired for fun, or at night to\r\ndance, after they had bathed and dressed. We all had splendid horses.\r\nThey reared and pranced; we galloped and jumped every log which came in\r\nour path. Jokes, repartee, and nonsense rattled off our tongues. We did\r\nnot worry about thousands of our fellows--starving and reeking with\r\ndisease in city slums. We were selfish. We were heedless. We were happy.\r\nWe were young.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nHarold Beecham was a splendid host. Anyone possessed of the least talent\r\nfor enjoyment had a pleasant time as his guest. He was hospitable in a\r\nquiet unostentatious manner. His overseer, jackeroos, and other\r\nemployees were all allowed the freedom of home, and could invite whom\r\nthey pleased to Five-Bob Downs. It is all very well to talk of good\r\nhosts. Bah, I could be a good hostess myself if I had Harold Beecham\'s\r\nsuperior implements of the art! With an immense station, plenty of\r\nhouse-room, tennis courts, musical instruments; a river wherein to fish,\r\nswim, and boat; any number of horses, vehicles, orchards, gardens, guns,\r\nand ammunition no object, it is easy to be a good host.\r\n\r\nI had been just a week at Five-Bob when uncle Julius came to take me\r\nhome, so I missed the shearing. Caddagat had been a dull hole without\r\nme, he averred, and I must return with him that very day. Mr and Miss\r\nBeecham remonstrated. Could I not be spared at least a fortnight longer?\r\nIt would be lonely without me. Thereupon uncle Jay-Jay volunteered to\r\nprocure Miss Benson from Wyambeet as a substitute. Harold declined the\r\noffer with thanks.\r\n\r\n"The schemes of youngsters are very transparent," said uncle Jay-Jay and\r\nMiss Augusta, smiling significantly at us. I feigned to be dense, but\r\nHarold smiled as though the insinuation was not only known, but also\r\nagreeable to him.\r\n\r\nUncle was inexorable, so home I had to go. It was sweet to me to hear\r\nfrom the lips of my grandmother and aunt that my absence had been felt.\r\n\r\nAs a confidante aunt Helen was the pink of perfection--tactful and\r\nsympathetic. My feather-brained chatter must often have bored her, but\r\nshe apparently was ever interested in it.\r\n\r\nI told her long yarns of how I had spent my time at the Beechams; of the\r\ndeafening duets Harold and I had played on the piano; and how he would\r\npersist in dancing with me, and he being so tall and broad, and I so\r\nsmall, it was like being stretched on a hay-rack, and very fatiguing. I\r\ngave a graphic account of the arguments--tough ones they were too--that\r\nMiss Augusta had with the overseer on religion, and many other subjects;\r\nof one jackeroo who gabbed never-endingly about his great relations at\r\nhome; another who incessantly clattered about spurs, whips, horses, and\r\nsport; and the third one--Joe Archer--who talked literature and trash\r\nwith me.\r\n\r\n"What was Harry doing all this time?" asked auntie. "What did he say?"\r\n\r\nHarold had been present all the while, yet I could not call to mind one\r\nthing he had said. I cannot remember him ever holding forth on a subject\r\nor cause, as most people do at one time or another.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER SEVENTEEN\r\n\r\nIdylls of Youth\r\n\r\n\r\nIn pursuance of his duty a government mail-contractor passed Caddagat\r\nevery Monday, dropping the Bossier mail as he went. On Thursday we also\r\ngot the post, but had to depend partly on our own exertions.\r\n\r\nA selector at Dogtrap, on the Wyambeet run, at a point of the compass\r\nten miles down the road from Caddagat, kept a hooded van. Every Thursday\r\nhe ran this to and from Gool-Gool for the purpose of taking to market\r\nvegetables and other farm produce. He also took parcels and passengers,\r\nboth ways, if called upon to do so. Caddagat and Five-Bob gave him a\r\ngreat deal of carrying, and he brought the mail for these and two or\r\nthree other places. It was one of my duties, or rather privileges, to\r\nride thither on Thursday afternoon for the post, a leather bag slung\r\nround my shoulders for the purpose. I always had a splendid mount, and\r\nthe weather being beautifully hot, it was a jaunt which I never failed\r\nto enjoy. Frank Hawden went with me once or twice--not because grannie\r\nor I thought his escort necessary. The idea was his own; but I gave him\r\nsuch a time that he was forced to relinquish accompanying me as a bad\r\njob.\r\n\r\nHarold Beecham kept a snivelling little Queensland black boy as a sort\r\nof black-your-boots, odd-jobs slavey or factotum, and he came to Dogtrap\r\nfor the mail, but after I started to ride for it Harold came regularly\r\nfor his mail himself. Our homeward way lay together for two miles, but\r\nhe always came with me till nearly in sight of home. Some days we raced\r\ntill our horses were white with lather; and once or twice mine was in\r\nsuch a state that we dismounted, and Harold unsaddled him and wiped the\r\nsweat off with his towel saddle-cloth, to remove the evidence of hard\r\nriding, so that I would not get into a scrape with uncle Jay-Jay. Other\r\ntimes we dawdled, so that when we parted the last rays of sunset would\r\nbe laughing at us between the white trunks of the tall gum-trees, the\r\nkookaburras would be making the echoes ring with their mocking\r\ngood-night, and scores of wild duck would be flying quickly roostward.\r\nAs I passed through the angle formed by the creek and the river, about\r\nhalf a mile from home, there came to my ears the cheery clink-clink of\r\nhobble-chains, the jangle of horse-bells, and the gleam of a dozen\r\ncamp-fires. The shearing was done out in Riverina now, and the men were\r\nall going home. Day after day dozens of them passed along the long white\r\nroad, bound for Monaro and the cool country beyond the blue peaks to the\r\nsoutheast, where the shearing was about to begin. When I had come to\r\nCaddagat the last of them had gone "down" with horses poor; now they\r\nwere travelling "up" with their horses--some of them\r\nthoroughbreds--rolling fat, and a cheque for their weeks of back-bending\r\nlabour in their pockets. But whether coming or going they always made to\r\nCaddagat to camp. That camping-ground was renowned as the best from\r\nMonaro to Riverina. It was a well-watered and sheltered nook, and the\r\nground was so rich that there was always a mouthful of grass to be had\r\nthere. It was a rare thing to see it without a fire; and the empty\r\njam-tins, bottles, bits of bag, paper, tent-pegs, and fish-tins to be\r\nfound there would have loaded a dozen waggons.\r\n\r\nThursday evening was always spent in going to Dogtrap, and all the other\r\ndays had their pleasant tasks and were full of wholesome enjoyment. The\r\nblue senna flowers along the river gave place to the white bloom of the\r\ntea-tree. Grannie, uncle, and aunt Helen filled the house with girl\r\nvisitors for my pleasure. In the late afternoon, as the weather got hot,\r\nwe went for bogeys in a part of the river two miles distant. Some of the\r\ngirls from neighbouring runs brought their saddles, others from town had\r\nto be provided therewith, which produced a dearth in sidesaddles, and it\r\nwas necessary for me to take a man\'s. With a rollicking gallop and a\r\nbogey ahead, that did not trouble me. Aunt Helen always accompanied us\r\non our bathing expeditions to keep us in check. She was the only one who\r\nbothered with a bathing-dress. The rest of us reefed off our clothing,\r\nin our hurry sending buttons in all directions, and plunged into the\r\npleasant water. Then--such water-fights, frolic, laughter, shouting and\r\nroaring fun as a dozen strong healthy girls can make when enjoying\r\nthemselves. Aunt Helen generally called time before we were half\r\ninclined to leave. We would linger too long, then there would be a great\r\nscramble for clothes, next for horses, and with wet hair streaming on\r\nour towels, we would go home full belt, twelve sets of galloping hoofs\r\nmaking a royal clatter on the hard dusty road. Grannie made a rule that\r\nwhen we arrived late we had to unsaddle our horses ourselves, and not\r\ndisturb the working men from their meal for our pleasure. We mostly were\r\nlate, and so there would be a tight race to see who would arrive at\r\ntable first. A dozen heated horses were turned out unceremoniously, a\r\ndozen saddles and bridles dumped down anywhere anyhow, and their\r\noccupants, with wet dishevelled hair and clothing in glorious disarray,\r\nwould appear at table averring that they were starving.\r\n\r\nThe Caddagat folk were enthusiastic anglers. Fishing was a favourite and\r\noften enjoyed amusement of the household. In the afternoon a tinful of\r\nworms would be dug out of one of the water-races, tackle collected,\r\nhorses saddled, and grannie, uncle, aunt, Frank Hawden, myself, and any\r\none else who had happened to drop in, would repair to the fish-holes\r\nthree miles distant. I hate fishing. Ugh! The hideous barbarity of\r\nshoving a hook through a living worm, and the cruelty of taking the fish\r\noff the hook! Uncle allowed no idlers at the river--all had to\r\nmanipulate a rod and line. Indulging in pleasant air-castles, I\r\ngenerally forgot my cork till the rod would be jerked in my hand, when I\r\nwould pull--too late! the fish would be gone. Uncle would lecture me for\r\nbeing a jackdaw, so next time I would glare at the cork unwinkingly, and\r\npull at the first signs of it bobbing--too soon! the fish would escape\r\nagain, and I would again be in disgrace. After a little experience I\r\nfound it was a good plan to be civil to Frank Hawden when the prospect\r\nof fishing hung around, and then he would attend to my line as well as\r\nhis own, while I read a book which I smuggled with me. The fish-hole was\r\nsuch a shrub-hidden nook that, though the main road passed within two\r\nhundred yards, neither we nor our horses could be seen by the travellers\r\nthereon. I lay on the soft moss and leaves and drank deeply of the\r\nbeauties of nature. The soft rush of the river, the scent of the shrubs,\r\nthe golden sunset, occasionally the musical clatter of hoofs on the\r\nroad, the gentle noises of the fishers fishing, the plop, plop of a\r\nplatypus disporting itself mid stream, came to me as sweetest elixir in\r\nmy ideal, dream-of-a-poet nook among the pink-based, grey-topped,\r\nmoss-carpeted rocks.\r\n\r\nI was a creature of joy in those days. Life is made up of little things.\r\nIt was a small thing to have a little pocket-money to spend on anything\r\nthat took my fancy--a very small thing, and yet how much pleasure it\r\ngave me. Though eating is not one of the great aims of my life, yet it\r\nwas nice to have enough of any delicacy one fancied. Not that we ever\r\nwent hungry at home, but when one has nothing to eat in the hot weather\r\nbut bread and beef it gives them tendency to dream of fruit and cool\r\ndainties. When one thinks of the countless army of one\'s fellows who are\r\ndaily selling their very souls for the barest necessaries of life, I\r\nsuppose we--irresponsible beings--should be thankful to God for allowing\r\nus, by scratching and scraping all our lives, to keep a crust in our\r\nmouth and a rag on our back. I am not thankful, I have been guilty of\r\nwhat Pat would term a "digresshion"--I started about going for the mail\r\nat Dogtrap. Harold Beecham never once missed taking me home on\r\nThursdays, even when his shearing was in full swing and he must have\r\nbeen very busy. He never once uttered a word of love to me--not so much\r\nas one of the soft nothings in which young people of opposite sexes\r\noften deal without any particular significance. Whether he went to all\r\nthe bother and waste of time accruing from escorting me home out of\r\ngentlemanliness alone, was a mystery to me. I desired to find out, and\r\nresolved to drive instead of ride to Dogtrap one day to see what he\r\nwould say.\r\n\r\nGrannie assented to the project. Of course I could drive for once if I\r\ndidn\'t feel able to ride, but the horses had been spelling for a long\r\ntime and were very frisky. I must take Frank with me or I might get my\r\nneck broken.\r\n\r\nI flatly opposed the idea of Frank Hawden going with me. He would make a\r\nmull of the whole thing. It was no use arguing with grannie and\r\nimpressing upon her the fact that I was not the least nervous concerning\r\nthe horses. I could take Frank with me in the buggy, ride, or stay at\r\nhome. I preferred driving. Accordingly the fat horses were harnessed to\r\nthe buggy, and with many injunctions to be careful and not forget the\r\nparcels, we set out. Frank Hawden\'s presence spoilt it all, but I\r\ndetermined to soon make short work of him.\r\n\r\nThere was one gate to go through, about four miles from the house. Frank\r\nHawden got out to open it. I drove through, and while he was pushing it\r\nto, laid the whip on the horses and went off full tilt. He ran after me\r\nshouting all manner of things that I could not hear on account of the\r\nrattle of the buggy. One horse began kicking up, so, to give him no time\r\nfor further pranks, I drove at a good round gallop, which quickly left\r\nthe lovable jackeroo a speck in the distance. The dust rose in thick\r\nclouds, the stones rattled from the whirling wheels, the chirr! chirr!\r\nof a myriad cicadas filled the air, and the white road glistened in the\r\ndazzling sunlight. I was enjoying myself tip-top, and chuckled to think\r\nof the way I had euchred Frank Hawden. It was such a good joke that I\r\nconsidered it worth two of the blowings-up I was sure of getting from\r\ngrannie for my conduct.\r\n\r\nIt was not long before I fetched up at Dogtrap homestead, where,\r\ntethered to the "six-foot" paling fence which surrounded the\r\nflower-garden, was Harold Beecham\'s favourite, great, black,\r\nsaddle-horse Warrigal. The vicious brute turned his beautiful head,\r\ndisplaying a white star on the forehead, and snorted as I approached.\r\nHis master appeared on the veranda raising his soft panama hat, and\r\nremarking, "Well I never! You\'re not by yourself, are you?"\r\n\r\n"I am. Would you please tell Mrs Butler to bring out grannie\'s parcels\r\nand post at once. I\'m afraid to dawdle, it\'s getting late."\r\n\r\nHe disappeared to execute my request and reappeared in less than a\r\nminute.\r\n\r\n"Mr Beecham, please would you examine Barney\'s harness. Something must\r\nbe hurting him. He has been kicking up all the way."\r\n\r\nExamining the harness and noticing the sweat that was dripping from the\r\nanimals, panting from their run, he said:\r\n\r\n"It looks as though you\'ve been making the pace a cracker. There is\r\nnothing that is irritating Barney in the least. If he\'s putting on any\r\nairs it is because he is frisky and not safe for you to drive. How did\r\nJulius happen to let you away by yourself?"\r\n\r\n"I\'m not frightened," I replied.\r\n\r\n"I see you\'re not. You\'d be game to tackle a pair of wild elephants, I\r\nknow, but you must remember you\'re not much bigger than a sparrow\r\nsitting up there, and I won\'t let you go back by yourself."\r\n\r\n"You cannot stop me."\r\n\r\n"I can."\r\n\r\n"You can\'t."\r\n\r\n"I can."\r\n\r\n"You can\'t."\r\n\r\n"I can."\r\n\r\n"How?"\r\n\r\n"I\'m going with you," he said.\r\n\r\n"You\'re not."\r\n\r\n"I am."\r\n\r\n"You\'re not."\r\n\r\nI am".\r\n\r\n"You ar-r-re not."\r\n\r\n"I am".\r\n\r\n"You are, ar-r-re not."\r\n\r\n"We\'ll see whether I will or not in a minute or two," he said with\r\namusement.\r\n\r\n"But, Mr Betcham, I object to your company. I am quite capable of taking\r\ncare of myself; besides, if you come home with me I will not be allowed\r\nout alone again--it will be altogether unpleasant for me."\r\n\r\nMrs Butler now appeared with the mail and some parcels, and Harold\r\nstowed them in the buggy.\r\n\r\n"You\'d better come in an\' \'ave a drop of tay-warter, miss, the kittle\'s\r\nbilin\'; and I have the table laid out for both of yez."\r\n\r\n"No, thank you, Mrs Butler. I can\'t possibly stay today, it\'s getting\r\nlate. I must hurry off. Good-bye! Good afternoon, Mr Beecham."\r\n\r\nI turned my buggy and pair smartly round and was swooping off. Without a\r\nword Harold was at their heads and seized the reins. He seized his\r\nhorse\'s bridle, where it was over the paling, and in a moment had him\r\ntied on the off-side of Barney, then stepping quietly into the buggy he\r\nput me away from the driver\'s seat as though I were a baby, quietly took\r\nthe reins and whip, raised his hat to Mrs Butler, who was smiling\r\nknowingly, and drove off.\r\n\r\nI was highly delighted with his action, as I would have despised him as\r\na booby had he given in to me, but I did not let my satisfaction appear.\r\nI sat as far away from him as possible, and pretended to be in a great\r\nhuff. For a while he was too fully occupied in making Barney "sit up" to\r\nnotice me, but after a few minutes he looked round, smiling a most\r\nannoying and pleasant smile.\r\n\r\n"I\'d advise you to straighten out your chin. It is too round and soft to\r\nlook well screwed up that way," he said provokingly.\r\n\r\nI tried to extinguish him with a look, but it had not the desired\r\neffect.\r\n\r\n"Now you had better be civil, for I have got the big end of the whip,"\r\nhe said.\r\n\r\n"I reserve to myself the right of behaving as I think fit in my own\r\nuncle\'s buggy.
