You are an intruder; it is yourself that should be\r\ncivil."\r\n\r\nI erected my parasol and held it so as to tease Harold. I put it down so\r\nthat he could not see the horses. He quietly seized my wrist and held it\r\nout of his way for a time, and then loosing me said, "Now, behave."\r\n\r\nI flouted it now, so that his ears and eyes were endangered, and he was\r\nforced to hold his hat on.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll give you three minutes to behave, or I\'ll put you out," he said\r\nwith mock severity.\r\n\r\n"Shure it\'s me wot\'s behavin\' beautiful," I replied, continuing my\r\nnonsense.\r\n\r\nHe pulled rein, seized me in one arm, and lifted me lightly to the\r\nground.\r\n\r\n"Now, you can walk till you promise to conduct yourself like a\r\nChristian!" he said, driving at a walk.\r\n\r\n"If you wait till I promise anything, you\'ll wait till the end of the\r\ncentury. I\'m quite capable of walking home."\r\n\r\n"You\'ll soon get tired of walking in this heat, and your feet will he\r\nblistered in a mile with those bits of paper."\r\n\r\nThe bits of paper to which he alluded were a pair of thin-soled white\r\ncanvas slippers--not at all fitted for walking the eight miles on the\r\nhard hot road ahead of me. I walked resolutely on, without deigning a\r\nglance at Harold, who had slowed down to a crawling walk.\r\n\r\n"Aren\'t you ready to get up now?" he inquired presently.\r\n\r\nI did not reply. At the end of a quarter of a mile he jumped out of the\r\nbuggy, seized upon me, lifted me in, and laughed, saying, "You\'re a very\r\nslashing little concern, but you are not big enough to do much damage."\r\n\r\nWe were about half-way home when Barney gave a tremendous lurch,\r\nbreaking a trace and some other straps. Mr Beecham was at the head of\r\nthe plunging horse in a twinkling. The harness seemed to be scattered\r\neverywhere.\r\n\r\n"I expect I had better walk on now," I remarked.\r\n\r\n"Walk, be grannied! With two fat lazy horses to draw you?" returned Mr\r\nBeecham.\r\n\r\nMen are clumsy, stupid creatures regarding little things, but in their\r\nright place they are wonderful animals. If a buggy was smashed to\r\nsmithereens, from one of their many mysterious pockets they would\r\nproduce a knife and some string, and put the wreck into working order in\r\nno time.\r\n\r\nHarold was as clever in this way as any other man with as much bushman\r\nability as he had, so it was not long ere we were bowling along as\r\nmerrily as ever.\r\n\r\nJust before we came in sight of Caddagat he came to a standstill, jumped\r\nto the ground, untied Warrigal, and put the reins in my hand, saying:\r\n\r\n"I think you can get home safely from here. Don\'t be in such a huff--I\r\nwas afraid something might happen you if alone. You needn\'t mention that\r\nI came with you unless you like. Good-bye."\r\n\r\n"Good-bye, Mr Beecham. Thank you for being so officious," I said by way\r\nof a parting shot.\r\n\r\n"Old Nick will run away with you for being so ungrateful," he returned.\r\n\r\n"Old Nick will have me anyhow," I thought to myself as I drove home amid\r\nthe shadows. The hum of the cicadas was still, and dozens of rabbits,\r\ntempted out by the cool of the twilight, scuttled across my path and hid\r\nin the ferns.\r\n\r\nI wished the harness had not broken, as I feared it would put a clincher\r\non my being allowed out driving alone in future.\r\n\r\nJoe Slocombe, the man who acted as groom and rouseabout, was waiting for\r\nme at the entrance gate.\r\n\r\n"I\'m glad you come at last, Miss Sybyller. The missus has been in a\r\ndreadful stoo for fear something had happened yuz. She\'s been runnin\' in\r\nan\' out like a gurrl on the look-out fer her lover, and was torkin\' of\r\nsendin\' me after yuz, but she went to her tea soon as she see the buggy\r\ncome in sight. I\'ll put all the parcels on the back veranda, and yuz can\r\ngo in at woncest or yuz\'ll be late fer yer tea."\r\n\r\n"Joe, the harness broke and had to be tied up. That is what kept me so\r\nlate," I explained.\r\n\r\n"The harness broke!" he exclaimed. "How the doose is that! Broke here in\r\nthe trace, and that strap! Well, I\'ll be hanged! I thought them straps\r\ncouldn\'t break only onder a tremenjous strain. The boss is so dashed\r\npartickler too. I believe he\'ll sool me off the place; and I looked at\r\nthat harness only yesterday. I can\'t make out how it come to break so\r\nsimple. The boss will rise the devil of a shine, and say you might have\r\nbeen killed."\r\n\r\nThis put a different complexion on things. I knew Joe Slocombe could\r\nmend the harness with little trouble, as it was because he was what\r\nuncle Jay-Jay termed a "handy divil" at saddlery that he was retained at\r\nCaddagat. I said carelessly:\r\n\r\n"If you mend the harness at once, Joe, uncle Julius need not be bothered\r\nabout it. As it happened, there is no harm done, and I won\'t mention the\r\nmatter."\r\n\r\n"Thank you, miss," he said eagerly. "I\'ll mend it at once."\r\n\r\nNow that I had that piece of business so luckily disposed of, I did not\r\nfeel the least nervous about meeting grannie. I took the mail in my arms\r\nand entered the dining-room, chirping pleasantly:\r\n\r\n"Grannie, I\'m such a good mail-boy. I have heaps of letters, and did not\r\nforget one of your commissions."\r\n\r\n"I don\'t want to hear that now," she said, drawing her dear old mouth\r\ninto a straight line, which told me I was not going to palm things off\r\nas easily as I thought. "I want a reason for your conduct this\r\nafternoon."\r\n\r\n"Explain what, grannie?" I inquired.\r\n\r\n"None of that pretence! Not only have you been most outrageously\r\ninsulting to Mr Hawden when I sent him with you, but you also\r\ndeliberately and wilfully disobeyed me."\r\n\r\nUncle Julius listened attentively, and Hawden looked at me with such a\r\nleer of triumph that my fingers tingled to smack his cars. Turning to my\r\ngrandmother, I said distinctly and cuttingly:\r\n\r\n"Grannie, I did not intentionally disobey you. Disobedience never\r\nentered my head. I hate that thing. His presence was detestable to me.\r\nWhen he got out at the gate I could not resist the impulse to drive off\r\nand leave him there. He looked such a complete jackdaw that you would\r\nhave laughed yourself to see him."\r\n\r\n"Dear, oh dear! You wicked hussy, what will become of you!" And grannie\r\nshook her head, trying to look stern, and hiding a smile in her\r\nserviette.\r\n\r\n"Your manners are not improving, Sybylla. I fear you must be\r\nincorrigible," said aunt Helen.\r\n\r\nWhen uncle Jay-Jay heard the whole particulars of the affair, he lay\r\nback in his chair and laughed fit to kill himself.\r\n\r\n"You ought to be ashamed to always encourage her in her tomboyish ways,\r\nJulius. It grieves me to see she makes no effort to acquire a ladylike\r\ndemeanour," said grannie.\r\n\r\nMr Hawden had come off second-best, so he arose from his half-finished\r\nmeal and stamped out, banging the door after him, and muttering\r\nsomething about "a disgustingly spoilt and petted tomboy", "a hideous\r\nbarbarian", and so forth.\r\n\r\nUncle Jay-Jay related that story to everyone, dwelling with great\r\ndelight upon the fact that Frank Hawden was forced to walk four miles in\r\nthe heat and dust.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER EIGHTEEN\r\n\r\nAs Short as I Wish had been the Majority of Sermons to which I have been\r\nForced to give Ear\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen alone I confessed to aunt Helen that Harold had accompanied me to\r\nwithin a short distance of home. She did not smile as usual, but looked\r\nvery grave, and, drawing me in front of her, said:\r\n\r\n"Sybylla, do you know what you are doing? Do you love Harry Beecham? Do\r\nyou mean to marry him?"\r\n\r\n"Aunt Helen, what a question to ask! I never dreamt of such a thing. He\r\nhas never spoken a word of love to me. Marriage! I am sure he does not\r\nfor an instant think of me in that light. I\'m not seventeen."\r\n\r\n"Yes, you are young, but some people\'s age cannot be reckoned by years.\r\nI am glad to see you have developed a certain amount of half-real and\r\nhalf-assumed youthfulness lately, but when the novelty of your present\r\nlife wears away, your old mature nature will be there, so it is of no\r\nuse feigning childishness. Harold Beecham is not given to speech--action\r\nwith him is the same thing. Can you look at me straight, Sybylla, and\r\nsay that Harold has not extended you something more than common\r\npoliteness?"\r\n\r\nHad aunt Helen put that question to me a day before, I would have\r\nblushed and felt guilty. But today not so. The words of the jackeroo the\r\nnight before had struck home. "A hideous barbarian", he had called me,\r\nand it seemed to me he had spoken the truth. My life had been so\r\npleasant lately that I had overlooked this fact, but now it returned to\r\nsting with redoubled bitterness. I had no lovable qualities to win for\r\nme the love of my fellows, which I so much desired.\r\n\r\nI returned aunt Helen a gaze as steady as her own, and said bitterly:\r\n\r\n"Aunt Helen, I can truly say he has never, and will never extend to me\r\nmore than common politeness. Neither will any other man. Surely you know\r\nenough of masculine human nature to see there is no danger of a man\r\nlosing his heart to a plain woman like me. Love in fancy and song is a\r\npretty myth, embracing unity of souls, congeniality of tastes, and such\r\nlike commodities. In workaday reality it is the lowest of passions,\r\nwhich is set alight by the most artistic nose and mouth, and it matters\r\nnot if its object is vile, low, or brainless to idiocy, so long as it\r\nhas these attributes."\r\n\r\n"Sybylla, Sybylla," said auntie sadly, as if to herself. "In the first\r\nflush of girlhood, and so bitter. Why is this?"\r\n\r\n"Because I have been cursed with the power of seeing, thinking, and,\r\nworse than all, feeling, and branded with the stinging affliction of\r\nugliness," I replied.\r\n\r\n"Now, Sybylla, you are going to think of yourself again. Something has\r\nput you out. Be sensible for once in a way. What you have said of men\'s\r\nlove may be true in a sense, but it is not always so, and Harry is not\r\nthat kind of man. I have known him all his life, and understand him, and\r\nfeel sure he loves you truly. Tell me plainly, do you intend to accept\r\nhim?"\r\n\r\n"Intend to accept him!" I echoed. "I haven\'t once thought of such a\r\npossibility. I never mean to marry anyone."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t you care for Harold? Just a little? Think."\r\n\r\n"How could I care for him?"\r\n\r\n"For many, many reasons. He is young, and very kind and gentle. He is\r\none of the biggest and finest-looking men you could find. He is a man\r\nwhom no one could despise, for he has nothing despicable about him. But,\r\nbest of all, he is true, and that, I think, is the bedrock of all\r\nvirtues."\r\n\r\n"But he is so conceited," I remarked.\r\n\r\n"That does not make him any the less lovable. I know another young\r\nperson very conceited, and it does not prevent me from loving her\r\ndearly," here aunt Helen smiled affectionately at me. "What you complain\r\nof in Harold will wear off presently--life has been very easy for him so\r\nfar, you see."\r\n\r\n"But, auntie, I\'m sure he thinks he could have any girl for the asking."\r\n\r\n"Well, he has a great number to choose from, for they all like him."\r\n\r\n"Yes, just for his money," I said scornfully. "But I\'ll surprise him if\r\nhe thinks he can get me for the asking."\r\n\r\n"Sybylla, never flirt. To play with a man\'s heart, I think, is one of\r\nthe most horribly unwomanly actions our sex can be guilty of."\r\n\r\n"I would scorn to flirt with any man," I returned with vigour. "Play\r\nwith a man\'s heart! You\'d really think they had such a thing, aunt\r\nHelen, to hear you talk. Hurt their vanity for a few days is the most a\r\nwoman could do with any of them. I am sick of this preach, preach about\r\nplaying with men\'s hearts. It is an old fable which should have been\r\nabolished long ago. It does not matter how a woman is played with."\r\n\r\n"Sybylla, you talk at random. The shortcomings of men are no excuse for\r\nyou to be unwomanly," said aunt Helen.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER NINETEEN\r\n\r\nThe 9th of November 1896\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Prince of Wales\'s birthday up the country was celebrated as usual\r\nthereaway by the annual horse-races on the Wyambeet course, about\r\nfourteen miles from Caddagat.\r\n\r\nThe holding of these races was an elderly institution, and was followed\r\nat night by a servants\' ball given by one of the squatters. Last year it\r\nhad been Beecham\'s ball, the year before Bossier\'s, and this year it was\r\nto take place in the woolshed of James Grant of Yabtree. Our two girls,\r\nthe gardener, and Joe Slocombe the groom, were to be present, as also\r\nwere all the other employees about. Nearly every one in the\r\ndistrict--masters and men--attended the races. We were going, Frank\r\nHawden volunteering to stay and mind the house.\r\n\r\nWe started at nine o\'clock. Grannie and uncle Boss sat in the front seat\r\nof the buggy, and aunt Helen and I occupied the back. Uncle always drove\r\nat a good round gallop. His idea was to have good horses, not donkeys,\r\nand not to spare them, as there were plenty more to be had any day. On\r\nthis morning he went off at his usual pace. Grannie urged as\r\nremonstrance that the dust was fearful when going at that rate. I\r\nclapped my hands and exclaimed, "Go it, Mr Bossier! Well done, uncle\r\nJay-Jay! Hurrah for Clancy!"\r\n\r\nUncle first said he was glad to see I had the spirit of an Australian,\r\nand then threatened to put my nose above my chin if I failed to behave\r\nproperly. Grannie remarked that I might have the spirit of an\r\nAustralian, but I had by no means the manners of a lady; while aunt\r\nHelen ventured a wish that I might expend all my superfluous spirits on\r\nthe way, so that I would be enabled to deport myself with a little\r\ndecorum when arrived at the racecourse.\r\n\r\nWe went at a great pace; lizards and goannas scampered out of the way in\r\ndozens, and, clambering trees, eyed us unblinkingly as we passed. Did we\r\nsee a person or vehicle a tiny speck ahead of us--in a short time they\r\nwere as far away in the background.\r\n\r\n"Please, uncle, let me drive," I requested.\r\n\r\n"Couldn\'t now. Your grannie can\'t sit in the back-seat--neither could\r\nI--and look like a tame cockatoo while you sat in front. You ask Harry\r\nto let you drive him. I bet he\'ll consent; he\'s sure to be in a sulky\r\nwith a spare seat on spec. We\'re sure to overtake him in a few minutes."\r\n\r\nThere was a vehicle in the distance which proved to be from Five-Bob\r\nDowns, but as we overhauled it, it was the drag, and not a sulky. Harold\r\noccupied the driver\'s seat, and the other occupants were all ladies. I\r\nnoticed the one beside him was wearing a very big hat, all ruffles,\r\nflowers, and plumes.\r\n\r\n"Shall I pull up and get you a seat?" inquired uncle Jay-Jay.\r\n\r\n"No, no, no."\r\n\r\nThe boss of Five-Bob drew to his side of the road, and when we had\r\npassed uncle began to tease:\r\n\r\n"Got faint-hearted, did you? The flower-garden on that woman\'s hat\r\ncorked your chances altogether. Never mind, don\'t you funk; I\'ll see\r\nthat you have a fair show. I\'ll get you a regular cart-wheel next time I\r\ngo to town, and we\'ll trim it up with some of old Barney\'s tail. If that\r\nwon\'t fetch him, I\'m sure nothing will."\r\n\r\nBefore we got to the racecourse Barney went lame through getting a stone\r\nin his hoof; this caused a delay which enabled the Five-Bob trap to\r\ncatch us, and we pulled rein a little distance apart at the same time,\r\nto alight.\r\n\r\nMr Beecham\'s groom went to his horses\' heads while Harold himself\r\nassisted his carriageful of ladies to set foot on the ground. Aunt Helen\r\nand grannie went to talk to them, but I stayed with uncle Jay-Jay while\r\nhe took the horses out. Somehow I was feeling very disappointed. I had\r\nexpected Harold Beecham to be alone. He had attended on me so absolutely\r\neverywhere I had met him lately, that I had unconsciously grown to look\r\nupon him as mine exclusively; and now, seeing he would belong to his own\r\nparty of ladies for the day, things promised to be somewhat flat without\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n"I told that devil of a Joe to be sure and turn up as soon as I arrived.\r\nI wanted him to water the horses, but I can\'t see him anywhere--the\r\ninfernal, crawling, doosed idiot!" ejaculated uncle Julius.\r\n\r\n"Never mind, uncle, let him have his holiday. I suppose he\'d like to\r\nhave time to spoon with his girl. I can easily water the horses."\r\n\r\n"That would suit Joe, I have no doubt; but I don\'t pay him to let you\r\nwater the horses. I\'ll water \'em myself."\r\n\r\nHe led one animal, I took the other, and we went in the direction of\r\nwater a few hundred yards away.\r\n\r\n"You run along to your grannie and the rest of them, and I\'ll go by\r\nmyself," said uncle, but I kept on with the horse.\r\n\r\n"You mustn\'t let a five-guinea hat destroy your hopes altogether," he\r\ncontinued, with a mischievous twinkle in his eyes. "If you stick to your\r\nguns you have a better show than anyone to bag the boss of Five-Bob."\r\n\r\n"I am at a loss to interpret your innuendo, Mr Bossier," I said stiffly.\r\n\r\n"Now, little woman, you think you are very smart, but you can\'t deceive\r\nme. I\'ve seen the game you and Harry have been up to this last month. If\r\nit had been any other man, I would have restricted your capers long\r\nago."\r\n\r\n"Uncle--" I began.\r\n\r\n"Now, Sybylla, none of your crammers. There is no harm in being a bit\r\ngone on Harry. It\'s only natural, and just what I\'d expect. I\'ve known\r\nhim since he was born, and he\'s a good all-round fellow. His head is\r\nscrewed on the right way, his heart is in the right place, and his\r\nprinciples are tip-top. He could give you fal-de-rals and rubbish to no\r\nend, and wouldn\'t be stingy either. You\'ll never get a better man. Don\'t\r\nyou be put out of the running so cheaply: hold your own and win, that\'s\r\nmy advice to you. There is nothing against him, only temper--old Nick\r\nhimself isn\'t a patch on him for temper."\r\n\r\n"Temper!" I exclaimed. "He is always so quiet and pleasant."\r\n\r\n"Yes, he controls it well. He\'s a fellow with a will like iron, and that\r\nis what you want, as I find you have none of your own. But be careful of\r\nHarry Beecham in a temper. He is like a raging lion, and when his temper\r\ndies away is a sulking brute, which is the vilest of all tempers. But he\r\nis not vindictive, and is easy managed, if you don\'t mind giving in and\r\ncoaxing a little."\r\n\r\n"Now, uncle, you have had your say, I will have mine. You seem to think\r\nI have more than a friendly regard for Mr Beecham, but I have not. I\r\nwould not marry him even if I could. I am so sick of every one thinking\r\nI would marry any man for his possessions. I would not stoop to marry a\r\nking if I did not love him. As for trying to win a man, I would scorn\r\nany action that way; I never intend to marry. Instead of wasting so much\r\nmoney on me in presents and other ways, I wish you would get me\r\nsomething to do, a profession that will last me all my life, so that I\r\nmay be independent."\r\n\r\n"No mistake, you\'re a rum youngster. You can be my companion till\r\nfurther orders. That\'s a profession that will last you a goodish while."\r\n\r\nWith this I had to be contented, as I saw he considered what I had said\r\nas a joke.\r\n\r\nI left uncle and went in quest of grannie, who, by this, was beyond the\r\nother side of the course, fully a quarter of a mile away. Going in her\r\ndirection I met Joe Archer, one of the Five-Bob jackeroos, and a great\r\nchum of mine. He had a taste for literature, and we got on together like\r\none o\'clock. We sat on a log under a stringybark-tree and discussed the\r\nbooks we had read since last we met, and enjoyed ourselves so much that\r\nwe quite forgot about the races or the flight of time until recalled\r\nfrom book-land by Harold Beecham\'s voice.\r\n\r\n"Excuse me, Miss Melvyn, but your grannie has commissioned me to find\r\nyou as we want to have lunch, and it appears you are the only one who\r\nknows the run of some of the tucker bags."\r\n\r\n"How do you do, Mr Beecham? Where are they going to have lunch?"\r\n\r\n"Over in that clump of box-trees," he replied, pointing in the direction\r\nof a little rise at a good distance.\r\n\r\n"How are you enjoying yourself?" he asked, looking straight at me.\r\n\r\n"Treminjous intoirely, sor," I replied.\r\n\r\n"I suppose you know the winner of every race," he remarked, quizzically\r\nwatching Joe Archer, who was blushing and as uneasy as a schoolgirl when\r\nnabbed in the enjoyment of an illicit love-letter.\r\n\r\n"Really, Mr Beecham, Mr Archer and I have been so interested in\r\nourselves that we quite forgot there was such a thing as a race at all,"\r\nI returned.\r\n\r\n"You\'d better see where old Boxer is. He might kick some of the other\r\nhorses if you don\'t keep a sharp look-out," he said, turning to his\r\njackeroo.\r\n\r\n"Ladies before gentlemen," I interposed. "I want Mr Archer to take me to\r\ngrannie, then he can go and look after old Boxer."\r\n\r\n"I\'ll escort you," said Beecham.\r\n\r\n"Thank you, but I have requested Mr Archer to do so."\r\n\r\n"In that case, I beg your pardon, and will attend to Boxer while Joe\r\ndoes as you request."\r\n\r\nRaising his hat he walked swiftly away with a curious expression on his\r\nusually pleasant face.\r\n\r\n"By Jove, I\'m in for it!" ejaculated my escort. "The boss doesn\'t get\r\nthat expression on his face for nothing. You take my tip for it, he felt\r\ninclined to seize me by the scruff of the neck and kick me from here to\r\nYabtree."\r\n\r\n"Go on!"\r\n\r\n"It\'s a fact. He did not believe in me not going to do his bidding\r\nimmediately. He has a roaring derry on disobedience. Everyone has to\r\nobey him like winkie or they can take their beds up and trot off quick\r\nand lively."\r\n\r\n"Mr Beecham has sufficient sense to see I was the cause of your\r\ndisobedience," I replied.\r\n\r\n"That\'s where it is. He would not have cared had it been some other\r\nlady, but he gets mad if any one dares to monopolize you. I don\'t know\r\nhow you are going to manage him. He is a pretty hot member sometimes."\r\n\r\n"Mr Archer, you presume! But throwing such empty banter aside, is Mr\r\nBeecham really bad-tempered?"\r\n\r\n"Bad-tempered is a tame name for it. You should have seen the dust he\r\nraised the other day with old Benson. He just did perform."\r\n\r\nI was always hearing of Harold Beecham\'s temper, and wished I could see\r\na little of it. He was always so imperturbably calm, and unfailingly\r\ngood-tempered under the most trying circumstances, that I feared he had\r\nno emotions in him, and longed to stir him up.\r\n\r\nGrannie greeted me with, "Sybylla, you are such a tiresome girl. I don\'t\r\nknow how you have packed these hampers, and we want to have lunch. Where\r\non earth have you been?"\r\n\r\nMiss Augusta Beecham saluted me warmly with a kiss, and presented me to\r\nher sister Sarah, who also embraced me. I went through an introduction\r\nto several ladies and gentlemen, greeted my acquaintances, and then set\r\nto work in dead earnest to get our provisions laid out--the Five-Bob\r\nDowns party had theirs in readiness. Needless to say, we were combining\r\nforces. I had my work completed when Mr Beecham appeared upon the scene\r\nwith two young ladies. One was a bright-faced little brunette, and the\r\nother a tall light blonde, whom, on account of her much trimmed hat, I\r\nrecognized as the lady who had been sitting on the box-seat of the\r\nBeecham drag that morning.\r\n\r\nJoe Archer informed me in a whisper that she was Miss Blanche Derrick\r\nfrom Melbourne, and was considered one of the greatest beauties of that\r\ncity.\r\n\r\nThis made me anxious to examine her carefully, but I did not get an\r\nopportunity of doing so. In the hurry to attend on the party, I missed\r\nthe honour of an introduction, and when I was at leisure she was sitting\r\nat some distance on a log, Harold Beecham shading her in a most\r\nreligious manner with a dainty parasol. In the afternoon she strolled\r\naway with him, and after I had attended to the remains of the feast, I\r\ntook Joe Archer in tow. He informed me that Miss Derrick had arrived at\r\nFive-Bob three days before, and was setting her cap determinedly at his\r\nboss.\r\n\r\n"Was she really very handsome?" I inquired.\r\n\r\n"By Jove, yes!" he replied. "But one of your disdainful haughty\r\nbeauties, who wouldn\'t deign to say good-day to a chap with less than\r\nsix or seven thousand a year."\r\n\r\nI don\'t know why I took no interest in the races. I knew nearly all the\r\nhorses running. Some of them were uncle\'s; though he never raced horses\r\nhimself, he kept some swift stock which he lent to his men for the\r\noccasion.\r\n\r\nOf more interest to me than the races was the pair strolling at a\r\ndistance. They were fit for an artist\'s models. The tall, broad,\r\nindependent figure of the bushman with his easy gentlemanliness, his\r\njockey costume enhancing his size. The equally tall majestic form of the\r\ncity belle, whose self-confident fashionable style spoke of nothing\r\nappertaining to girlhood, but of the full-blown rose--indeed, a splendid\r\npair physically!\r\n\r\nThen I thought of my lack of beauty, my miserable five-feet-one-inch\r\nstature, and I looked at the man beside me, small and round-shouldered,\r\nand we were both dependent children of indigence. The contrast we\r\npresented to the other pair struck me hard, and I laughed a short bitter\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\nI excused myself to my companion, and acceded to the request of several\r\nchildren to go on a flower- and gum-hunting expedition. We were a long\r\ntime absent, and returning, the little ones scampered ahead and left me\r\nalone. Harold Beecham came to meet me, looking as pleasant as ever.\r\n\r\n"Am I keeping grannie and uncle waiting?" I inquired.\r\n\r\n"No. They have gone over an hour," he replied.\r\n\r\n"Gone! How am I to get home? She must have been very angry to go and\r\nleave me. What did she say?"\r\n\r\n"On the contrary, she was in great fiddle. She said to tell you not to\r\nkill yourself with fun, and as you are not going home, she left me to\r\nsay good night. I suppose she kisses you when performing that ceremony,"\r\nhe said mischievously.\r\n\r\n"Where am I going tonight?"\r\n\r\n"To Five-Bob Downs, the camp of yours truly," he replied.\r\n\r\n"I haven\'t got a dinner dress, and am not prepared. I will go home."\r\n\r\n"We have plenty dinner dresses at Five-Bob without any more. It is Miss\r\nMelvyn we want," he said.\r\n\r\n"Oh, bother you!" I retorted. "Men are such stupid creatures, and never\r\nunderstand about dress or anything. They think you could go to a ball in\r\na wrapper."\r\n\r\n"At all events, they are cute enough to know when they want a young lady\r\nat their place, no matter how she\'s dressed," he said good-humouredly.\r\n\r\nOn reaching the racecourse I was surprised to see aunt Helen there. From\r\nher I learnt that grannie and uncle Jay-Jay had really gone home, but Mr\r\nBeecham had persuaded them to allow aunt Helen and me to spend the night\r\nat Five-Bob Downs, our host promising to send or take us home on the\r\nmorrow. Now that I was to have aunt Helen with me I was delighted at the\r\nprospect, otherwise I would have felt a little out of it. With aunt\r\nHelen, however, I was content anywhere, and built a castle in the air,\r\nwherein one day she and I were always to live together--for ever! Till\r\ndeath!\r\n\r\nGoing home aunt Helen occupied a front seat with Harold and Miss\r\nDerrick, and I was crammed in at the back beside Miss Augusta, who\r\npatted my hand and said she was delighted to see me.\r\n\r\nA great concourse of young men and women in vehicles and on horseback,\r\nand in expectation of great fun, were wending their way to\r\nYabtree--nearly every trap containing a fiddle, concertina, flute, or\r\naccordion in readiness for the fray.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER TWENTY\r\n\r\nSame Yarn--continued\r\n\r\n\r\nEvery station hand from Five-Bob, male and female, had gone to the ball\r\nat Yabtree. Harold and his overseer had to attend to the horses, while\r\nthe jackeroos started a fire in the kitchen, opened windows and doors\r\nwhich had been locked all day, and saw to the comfort of the gentlemen\r\nguests.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen and I shared the one bedroom. As we had not fresh dresses to\r\nput on we had to make the best of our present toilet.\r\n\r\nI unplaited my hair (shook the dust out of it) and wore it flowing. We\r\nwashed and dusted ourselves, and wore as adornment--roses. Crimson and\r\ncream roses paid the penalty of peeping in the window. Aunt Helen\r\nplucked some of them, which she put in my hair and belt, and pinned\r\ncarefully at my throat, and then we were ready. Miss Beecham assured us\r\nthere was nothing to be done, as the maids had set the table and\r\nprepared the viands for a cold meal before leaving in the morning, so we\r\nproceeded to the drawing-room to await the arrival of the other\r\nvisitors. They soon made their appearance. First, two stout old\r\nsquatters with big laughs and bigger corporations, then Miss Augusta\r\nBeecham, next Joe Archer the overseer, and the two other jackeroos.\r\nAfter these appeared a couple of governesses, Mr, Mrs, and Miss Benson,\r\na clergyman, an auctioneer, a young friend of Harold\'s from Cootamundra,\r\na horse-buyer, a wool-classer, Miss Sarah Beecham, and then Miss Derrick\r\nbrought herself and her dress in with great style and airs. She was\r\ngarbed in a sea-green silk, and had jewellery on her neck, arms, and\r\nhair. Her self-confident mien was suggestive of the conquest of many\r\nmasculine hearts. She was a big handsome woman. Beside her, I in my\r\ncrushed white muslin dress was as overshadowed as a little white\r\nhandkerchief would be in comparison to a gorgeous shawl heavily wrought\r\nin silks and velvet. She was given the best seat as though she were a\r\nprincess. She sat down with great indifference, twirled a bracelet round\r\nher wrist, languidly opened her fan, and closed her eyes as she wafted\r\nit slowly to and fro.\r\n\r\n"By Jove, isn\'t she a splendid creature?" enthusiastically whispered a\r\ngentleman sitting beside me.\r\n\r\nI looked at her critically. She was very big, and in a bony stiff way\r\nwas much developed in figure. She had a nice big nose, and a long\r\nwell-shaped face, a thin straight mouth, and empty light eyes. If my\r\nattention had not been called to her I would not have noticed her one\r\nway or the other, but being pointed to as a beauty, I weighed her\r\naccording to my idea of facial charm, and pronounced her one of the most\r\ninsipid-looking people I had set eyes upon.\r\n\r\nShe was the kind of woman with whom men become much infatuated. She\r\nwould never make a fool of herself by letting her emotions run away with\r\nher, because she had no emotions, but lived in a sea of unruffled\r\nself-consciousness and self-confidence. Any man would be proud to\r\nintroduce her as his wife to his friends whom he had brought home to\r\ndinner. She would adorn the head of his table. She would never worry him\r\nwith silly ideas. She would never act with impropriety. She would never\r\nbecome a companion to her husband. Bah, a man does not want his wife to\r\nbe a companion! There were myths and fables in the old day; so there are\r\nnow. The story that men like a companion as well as a wife is an\r\nup-to-date one.\r\n\r\nThis train of thought was interrupted by our host, who appeared in the\r\ndoorway, clad from sole to neck in white. We steered for the\r\ndining-room--twenty-two all told--thirteen men and nine representatives\r\nof the other sex.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen got one seat of honour near the head of the table and Miss\r\nDerrick another. I drifted to the foot among the unimportant younger\r\nfry, where we had no end of fun and idle chatter. We had to wait on\r\nourselves, and as all formality was dispensed with, it was something\r\nlike a picnic.\r\n\r\nThe heat was excessive. Every window and door were open, and the balmy,\r\nalmost imperceptible, zephyrs which faintly rustled the curtains and\r\nkissed our perspiration-beaded brows were rich with many scents from the\r\nwide old flower-garden, which, despite the drought, brought forth a\r\nwealth of blossom.\r\n\r\nWhen done eating we had to wash the dishes. Such a scamper ensued back\r\nand forwards to the kitchen, which rang with noise, and merriment.\r\nEveryone was helping, hindering, laughing, joking, teasing, and brimming\r\nover with fun and enjoyment. When we had completed this task, dancing\r\nwas proposed. Some of the elderly and more sensible people said it was\r\ntoo hot, but all the young folks did not care a rap for the temperature.\r\nHarold had no objections, Miss Derrick was agreeable, Miss Benson\r\nannounced herself ready and willing, and Joe Archer said he was\r\n"leppin\'" to begin, so we adjourned to the dancing-room and commenced\r\noperations.\r\n\r\nI played the piano for the first quadrille, and aunt Helen for the\r\nsecond dance. It was most enjoyable. There was a table at one end of the\r\nroom on which was any amount of cherries, lollies, cake, dainties,\r\nbeers, syrups, and glasses, where all could regale themselves without\r\nceremony or bother every time the inclination seized them. Several doors\r\nand windows of the long room opened into the garden, and, provided one\r\nhad no fear of snakes, it was delightful to walk amid the flowers and\r\ncool oneself between dances.\r\n\r\nA little exertion on such a night made us very hot. After the third\r\ndance the two old squatters, the horse-buyer, the clergyman, and Mr\r\nBenson disappeared. Judging from the hilarity of their demeanour and the\r\nkilling odour of their breaths when they returned an hour or so later,\r\nduring their absence they must have conscientiously sampled the contents\r\nof every whisky decanter on the dining-room sideboard.\r\n\r\nI could not dance, but had no lack of partners, as, ladies being in the\r\nminority, the gentlemen had to occasionally put up with their own sex in\r\na dance.\r\n\r\n"Let\'s take a breeze now and have a song or two, but no more dancing for\r\na while," said some of them; but Harold Beecham said, "One more turn,\r\nand then we will have a long spell and a change of programme."\r\n\r\nHe ordered Joe Archer to play a waltz, and the floor soon held several\r\nwhirling couples. Harold "requested the pleasure" of me--the first time\r\nthat night. I demurred. He would not take a refusal.\r\n\r\n"Believe me, if I felt competent, Mr Beecham, I would not refuse. I\r\ncannot dance. It will be no pleasure to you."\r\n\r\n"Allow me to be the best judge of what is a pleasure to me," he said,\r\nquietly placing me in position.\r\n\r\nHe swung me once round the room, and then through an open window into\r\nthe garden.\r\n\r\n"I am sorry that I haven\'t had more time to look after you today. Come\r\nround into my room. I want to strike a bargain with you," were his\r\nwords.\r\n\r\nI followed him in the direction of a detached building in the garden.\r\nThis was Harold\'s particular domain. It contained three rooms--one a\r\nlibrary and office, another an arsenal and deed-room, and the third,\r\ninto which he led me, was a sort of sitting-room, containing a piano,\r\nfacilities for washing, a table, easy-chairs, and other things. As we\r\nentered I noticed the lamp, burning brightly on the table, gleamed on\r\nthe face of a clock on the wall, which pointed to half past ten.\r\n\r\nWe stood beside the table, some distance apart, and, facing me, he said:\r\n\r\n"It is no use of me making a long yarn about nothing. I\'m sure you know\r\nwhat I want to say better than I do myself. You always are wonderfully\r\nsmart at seeing through a fellow. Tell me, will it be yes or no?"\r\n\r\nThis was an experience in love. He did not turn red or white, or yellow\r\nor green, nor did he tremble or stammer, or cry or laugh, or become\r\nfierce or passionate, or tender or anything but just himself, as I had\r\nalways known him. He displayed no more emotion than had he been inviting\r\nme to a picnic. This was not as I had pictured a man would tell his\r\nlove, or as I had read of it, heard of it, or wished it should be. A\r\ncurious feeling--disappointment, perhaps--stole over me. His\r\nmatter-of-fact coolness flabbergasted me.\r\n\r\n"Is this not rather sudden? You have given me no intimation of your\r\nintentions," I stammered.\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t think it wise to dawdle any longer," he replied. "Surely you\r\nhave known what I\'ve been driving at ever since I first clapped eyes on\r\nyou. There\'s plenty of time. I don\'t want to hurry you, only I want you\r\nto be engaged to me for safety."\r\n\r\nHe spoke as usual in his slow twangy drawl, which would have proclaimed\r\nhis Colonial nationality anywhere. No word of love was uttered to me and\r\nnone requested from me.\r\n\r\nI put it down to his conceit. I thought that he fancied he could win any\r\nwoman, and me without the least palaver or trouble. I felt annoyed. I\r\nsaid aloud, "I will become engaged to you;" to myself I added, "Just for\r\na little while, the more to surprise and take the conceit out of you\r\nwhen the time comes."\r\n\r\nNow that I understand his character I know that it was not conceit, but\r\njust his quiet unpretending way. He had meant all his actions towards\r\nme, and had taken mine in return.\r\n\r\n"Thank you, Sybylla, that is all I want. We will talk about the matter\r\nmore some other time. I will go up to Caddagat next Sunday. You have\r\nsurprised me nearly out of my wits," here he laughed. "I never dreamt\r\nyou would say yes so easily, just like any other girl. I thought I would\r\nhave a lot of trouble with you."\r\n\r\nHe approached me and was stooping to kiss me. I cannot account for my\r\naction or condemn it sufficiently. It was hysterical--the outcome of an\r\noverstrung, highly excitable, and nervous temperament. Perhaps my vanity\r\nwas wounded, and my tendency to strike when touched was up in arms. The\r\ncalm air of ownership with which Harold drew near annoyed me, or, as\r\nSunday-school teachers would explain it, Satan got hold of me. He\r\ncertainly placed a long strong riding-whip on the table beneath my hand!\r\nAs Harold stooped with the intention of pressing his lips to mine, I\r\nquickly raised the whip and brought it with all my strength right across\r\nhis face. The instant the whip had descended I would have smashed my arm\r\non the door-post to recall that blow. But that was impossible. It had\r\nleft a great weal on the healthy sun-tanned skin. His moustache had\r\nsaved his lips, but it had caught his nose, the left cheek, had blinded\r\nthe left eye, and had left a cut on the temple from which drops of blood\r\nwere rolling down his cheek and staining his white coat. A momentary\r\ngleam of anger shot into his eyes and he gave a gasp, whether of\r\nsurprise, pain, or annoyance, I know not. He made a gesture towards me.\r\nI half expected and fervently wished he would strike. The enormity of\r\nwhat I had done paralysed me. The whip fell from my fingers and I\r\ndropped on to a low lounge behind me, and placing my elbows on my knees\r\ncrouchingly buried my face in my hands; my hair tumbled softly over my\r\nshoulders and reached the floor, as though to sympathetically curtain my\r\nhumiliation. Oh, that Harold would thrash me severely! It would have\r\ninfinitely relieved me. I had done a mean unwomanly thing in thus\r\nstriking a man, who by his great strength and sex was debarred\r\nretaliation. I had committed a violation of self-respect and common\r\ndecency; I had given a man an ignominious blow in the face with a\r\nriding-whip. And that man was Harold Beecham, who with all his strength\r\nand great stature was so wondrously gentle--who had always treated my\r\nwhims and nonsense with something like the amused tolerance held by a\r\ngreat Newfoundland for the pranks of a kitten.\r\n\r\nThe clock struck eleven.\r\n\r\n"A less stinging rebuke would have served your purpose. I had no idea\r\nthat a simple caress from the man whose proposal of marriage you had\r\njust accepted would be considered such an unpardonable familiarity."\r\n\r\nHarold\'s voice fell clearly, calmly, cuttingly on the silence. He moved\r\naway to the other end of the room and I heard the sound of water.\r\n\r\nA desire filled me to tell him that I did not think he had attempted a\r\nfamiliarity, but that I had been mad. I wished to say I could not\r\naccount for my action, but I was dumb. My tongue refused to work, and I\r\nfelt as though I would choke. The splash of the water came from the\r\nother end of the room. I knew he must be suffering acute pain in his\r\neye. A far lighter blow had kept me sleepless a whole night. A fear\r\npossessed me that I might have permanently injured his sight. The splash\r\nof water ceased. His footfall stopped beside me. I could feel he was\r\nwithin touching distance, but I did not move.\r\n\r\nOh, the horrible stillness! Why did he not speak? He placed his hand\r\nlightly on my head.\r\n\r\n"It doesn\'t matter, Syb. I know you didn\'t mean to hurt me. I suppose\r\nyou thought you couldn\'t affect my dark, old, saddle-flap-looking phiz.\r\nThat is one of the disadvantages of being a big lumbering concern like I\r\nam. Jump up. That\'s the girl."\r\n\r\nI arose. I was giddy, and would have fallen but for Harold steadying me\r\nby the shoulder. I looked up at him nervously and tried to ask his\r\nforgiveness, but I failed.\r\n\r\n"Good heavens, child, you are as white as a sheet! I was a beast to\r\nspeak harshly to you." He held a glass of water to my lips and I drank.\r\n\r\n"Great Jupiter, there\'s nothing to worry about! I know you hadn\'t the\r\nslightest intention of hurting me. It\'s nothing--I\'ll be right in a few\r\nmoments. I\'ve often been amused at and have admired your touch-me-not\r\nstyle. You only forgot you had something in your hand."\r\n\r\nHe had taken it quite as a matter of fact, and was excusing me in the\r\nkindest possible terms.\r\n\r\n"Good gracious, you mustn\'t stew over such a trifling accident! It\'s\r\nnothing. Just tie this handkerchief on for me, please, and then we\'ll go\r\nback to the others or there will be a search-party after us."\r\n\r\nHe could have tied the handkerchief just as well himself--it was only\r\nout of kindly tact he requested my services. I accepted his kindness\r\ngratefully. He sank on his knee so that I could reach him, and I tied a\r\nlarge white handkerchief across the injured part. He could not open his\r\neye, and hot water poured from it, but he made light of the idea of it\r\npaining. I was feeling better now, so we returned to the ballroom. The\r\nclock struck the half-hour after eleven as we left the room. Harold\r\nentered by one door and, I by another, and I slipped into a seat as\r\nthough I had been there some time.\r\n\r\nThere were only a few people in the room. The majority were absent--some\r\nlove-making, others playing cards. Miss Beecham was one who was not thus\r\nengaged. She exclaimed at once:\r\n\r\n"Good gracious, boy, what have you done to yourself?"\r\n\r\n"Looks as if he had been interviewing a belligerent tramp," said aunt\r\nHelen, smilingly.\r\n\r\n"He\'s run into the clothes-line, that\'s what he\'s done," said Miss\r\nAugusta confidently, after she had peeped beneath the bandage.\r\n\r\n"You ought to get a bun for guessing, aunt Gus," said Harold laughing.\r\n\r\n"I told them to put the clothes-lines up when they had done with them. I\r\nknew there would be an accident."\r\n\r\n"Perhaps they were put up high enough for ordinary purposes," remarked\r\nher nephew.\r\n\r\n"Let me do something for you, dear."\r\n\r\n"No, thank you, aunt Gus. It is nothing," he said carelessly, and the\r\nmatter dropped.\r\n\r\nHarold Beecham was not a man to invite inquiry concerning himself.\r\n\r\nSeeing I was unobserved by the company, I slipped away to indulge in my\r\nfoolish habit of asking the why and the wherefore of things. Why had\r\nHarold Beecham (who was a sort of young sultan who could throw the\r\nhandkerchief where he liked) chosen me of all women? I had no charms to\r\nrecommend me--none of the virtues which men demand of the woman they\r\nwish to make their wife. To begin with, I was small, I was erratic and\r\nunorthodox, I was nothing but a tomboy--and, cardinal disqualification,\r\nI was ugly. Why, then, had he proposed matrimony to me? Was it merely a\r\nwhim? Was he really in earnest?\r\n\r\nThe night was soft and dark; after being out in it for a time I could\r\ndiscern the shrubs dimly silhouetted against the light. The music struck\r\nup inside again. A step approached me on the gravelled walk among the\r\nflowers, and Harold called me softly by name. I answered him.\r\n\r\n"Come," he said, "we are going to dance; will you be my partner?"\r\n\r\nWe danced, and then followed songs and parlour games, and it was in the\r\nsmall hours when the merry goodnights were all said and we had retired\r\nto rest. Aunt Helen dropped to sleep in a short time; but I lay awake\r\nlistening to the soft distant call of the mopokes in the scrub beyond\r\nthe stables.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER TWENTY-ONE\r\n\r\nMy Unladylike Behaviour Again\r\n\r\n\r\nJoe Archer was appointed to take us home on the morrow. When our host\r\nwas seeing us off--still with his eye covered--he took opportunity of\r\nwhispering to me his intention of coming to Caddagat on the following\r\nSunday.\r\n\r\nEarly in the afternoon of that day I took a book, and, going down the\r\nroad some distance, climbed up a broad-branched willow-tree to wait for\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nIt was not long before he appeared at a smart canter. He did not see me\r\nin the tree, but his horse did, and propping, snorted wildly, and gave a\r\nbackward run. Harold spurred him, he bucked spiritedly. Harold now saw\r\nme and sang out:\r\n\r\n"I say, don\'t frighten him any more or he\'ll fling me, saddle and all. I\r\nhaven\'t got a crupper or a breastplate."\r\n\r\n"Why haven\'t you, then? Hang on to him. I do like the look of you while\r\nthe horse is going on like that."\r\n\r\nHe had dismounted, and had thrown the bridle rein over a post of the\r\nfence.\r\n\r\n"I came with nothing but a girth, and that loose, as it was so hot; and\r\nI was as near as twopence to being off, saddle and all. You might have\r\nbeen the death of me," he said good-humouredly.\r\n\r\n"Had I been, my fortune would have been made," I replied.\r\n\r\n"How do you make that out? You\'re as complimentary as ever."\r\n\r\n"Everyone would be wanting to engage me as the great noxious weed-killer\r\nand poisonous insect exterminator if I made away with you," I answered.\r\nI gave him an invitation to take a seat with me, and accepting, he swung\r\nup with easy grace. There was any amount of accommodation for the two of\r\nus on the good-natured branches of the old willow-tree.\r\n\r\nWhen he had settled himself, my companion said, "Now, Syb, I\'m ready for\r\nyou. Fire away. But wait a minute, I\'ve got something here for you which\r\nI hope you\'ll like."\r\n\r\nAs he searched in his pockets, I noticed that his eye had quite\r\nrecovered, though there was still a slight mark on his cheek. He handed\r\nme a tiny morocco case, which on being opened disclosed a costly ring. I\r\nhave about as much idea of the prices of things as a turkey would have.\r\nPerhaps that ring cost thirty pounds or possibly fifty guineas, for all\r\nI know. It was very heavy, and had a big diamond supported on either\r\nside by a large sapphire, and had many small gems surrounding it.\r\n\r\n"Let me see if it fits," he said, taking my hand; but I drew it away.\r\n\r\n"No; don\'t you put it on. That would make us irrevocably engaged."\r\n\r\n"Isn\'t that what we intend to be?" he said in a tone of surprise.\r\n\r\n"Not just yet; that is what I want to say to you. We will have three\r\nmonths\' probation to see how we get on. At the end of that time, if we\r\nmanage to sail along smoothly, we\'ll have the real thing; until then we\r\nwill not be any more than we have been to each other."\r\n\r\n"But what am I to do in the meantime?" he asked, with amusement curving\r\nthe corners of his mouth.\r\n\r\n"Do! Do the usual thing, of course; but don\'t pay me any special\r\nattentions, or I\'ll be done with you at once."\r\n\r\n"What\'s your idea for this?"\r\n\r\n"It is no use making fools of ourselves; we might change our minds."\r\n\r\n"Very well; so be it," he said laughing. "I might have known you would\r\nhave things arranged different from any other girl. But you\'ll take the\r\nring and wear it, won\'t you? Let me put it on."\r\n\r\n"No; I won\'t let you put a finger on me till the three months are up.\r\nThen, if we definitely make up our minds, you can put it on; but till\r\nthen, don\'t for the life of you hint by word or sign that we have any\r\nsort of an arrangement between us. Give me the ring and I\'ll wear it\r\nsometimes."\r\n\r\nHe handed it to me again, and I tried it on. It was a little large.\r\nHarold took it, and tried to put it on one of his fingers. It would fit\r\non none but the very top of his little finger. We laughed heartily at\r\nthe disparity in the size of our hands.\r\n\r\n"I\'ll agree to your bargain," he said. "But you\'ll be really engaged to\r\nme all the same.`\r\n\r\n"Yes; under those conditions. Then it will not matter if we have a tiff.\r\nWe can part, and no one will be the wiser."\r\n\r\nOn my suggesting that it was now time to go to the house, he swung\r\nhimself down by a branch and turned to assist me. Descending from that\r\ntree was a feat which presented no difficulties to me when no one was\r\nby, but now it seemed an awkward performance.\r\n\r\n"Just lead your horse underneath, so that I can get on to his back,\r\nthence to the ground quite easily," I said.\r\n\r\n"No fear! Warrigal wouldn\'t stand that kind of dodge. Won\'t I do? I\r\ndon\'t think your weight will quite squash me," he returned, placing\r\nhimself in leap-frog position, and I stepped on to his back and slid\r\nfrom there to the ground quite easily.\r\n\r\nThat afternoon, when leaving the house, I had been followed by one of\r\nthe dogs, which, when I went up the willow-tree, amused himself chasing\r\nwater lizards along the bank of the creek. He treed one, and kept up a\r\nfurious barking at the base of its refuge. The yelping had disturbed\r\ngrannie where she was reading on the veranda, and coming down the road\r\nunder a big umbrella to see what the noise was about, as luck would have\r\nit she was in the nick of time to catch me standing on Harold Beecham\'s\r\nback. Grannie frequently showed marked displeasure regarding what she\r\ntermed my larrikinism, but never before had I seen her so thoroughly\r\nangry. Shutting her umbrella, she thrust at me with it, saying, "shame!\r\nshame! You\'ll come to some harm yet, you immodest, bold, bad hussy! I\r\nwill write to your mother about you. Go home at once, miss, and confine\r\nyourself in your room for the remainder of the day, and don\'t dare eat\r\nanything until tomorrow. Spend the time in fasting, and pray to God to\r\nmake you better. I don\'t know what makes you so forward with men. Your\r\nmother and aunt never gave me the slightest trouble in that way."\r\n\r\nShe pushed me from her in anger, and I turned and strode housewards\r\nwithout a word or glancing behind. I could hear grannie deprecating my\r\nconduct as I departed, and Harold quietly and decidedly differing from\r\nher.\r\n\r\nFrom the time of my infancy punishment of any description never had a\r\nbeneficial effect upon me. But dear old grannie was acting according to\r\nher principles in putting me through a term of penance, so I shut myself\r\nin my room as directed, with goodwill towards her at my heart. I was\r\nburning with shame. Was I bold and immodest with men, as accused of\r\nbeing? It was the last indiscretion I would intentionally have been\r\nguilty of. In associating with men I never realize that the trifling\r\ndifference of sex is sufficient to be a great wall between us. The fact\r\nof sex never for an instant enters my head, and I find it as easy to be\r\nchummy with men as with girls: men in return have always been very good,\r\nand have treated me in the same way.\r\n\r\nOn returning from her walk grannie came to my room, brought me some\r\npreachy books to read, and held out to me the privilege of saying I was\r\nsorry, and being restored to my usual place in the society of the\r\nhousehold.\r\n\r\n"Grannie, I cannot say I am sorry and promise to reform, for my\r\nconscience does not reproach me in the least. I had no evil--not even a\r\nviolation of manners--in my intentions; but I am sorry that I vexed\r\nyou," I said.\r\n\r\n"Vexing me is not the sinful part of it. It is your unrepentant heart\r\nthat fills me with fears for your future. I will leave you here to think\r\nby yourself. The only redeeming point about you is, you do not pretend\r\nto be sorry when you are not."\r\n\r\nThe dear old lady shook her head sorrowfully as she departed.\r\n\r\nThe afternoon soon ran away, as I turned to my bookcase for\r\nentertainment and had that beautiful ring to admire.\r\n\r\nI heard them come in to tea, and I thought Harold had gone till I heard\r\nuncle Jay-Jay address him:\r\n\r\n"Joe Archer told me you ran into a clothes-line on race-night, and ever\r\nsince then mother has kept up a daddy of a fuss about ours. We\'ve got\r\nprops about a hundred feet long, and if you weren\'t in the know you\'d\r\nthink we had a telegraph wire to old St Peter up above."\r\n\r\nI wondered what Harold thought of the woman he had selected as his\r\nfuture wife being shut up for being a "naughty girl". The situation\r\namused me exceedingly.\r\n\r\nAbout nine o\'clock he knocked at my window and said:\r\n\r\n"Never mind, Syb. I tried to get you off, but it was no go. Old people\r\noften have troublesome straitlaced ideas. It will blow over by\r\ntomorrow."\r\n\r\nI did not answer; so he passed on with firm regular footfall, and\r\npresently I heard his horse\'s hoof-beats dying away in the darkness, and\r\nthe closing and locking of doors around me as the household retired for\r\nthe night.\r\n\r\nDuring the following fortnight I saw Harold a good many times at\r\ncricket-matches, hare-drives, and so forth, but he did not take any\r\nparticular notice of me. I flirted and frolicked with my other young men\r\nfriends, but he did not care. I did not find him an ardent or a jealous\r\nlover. He was so irritatingly cool and matter-of-fact that I wished for\r\nthe three months to pass so that I might be done with him, as I had come\r\nto the conclusion that he was barren of emotion or passion of any kind.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER TWENTY-TWO\r\n\r\nSWEET SEVENTEEN\r\n\r\n\r\nMonday arrived--last day of November and seventeenth anniversary of my\r\nbirth--and I celebrated it in a manner which I capitally enjoyed.\r\n\r\nIt was the time of the annual muster at Cummabella--a cattle-station\r\nseventeen miles eastward from Caddagat--and all our men were there\r\nassisting. Word had been sent that a considerable number of beasts among\r\nthose yarded bore the impress of the Bossier brand on their hides; so on\r\nSunday afternoon uncle Jay-Jay had also proceeded thither to be in\r\nreadiness for the final drafting early on Monday morning. This left us\r\nmanless, as Frank Hawden, being incapacitated with a dislocated wrist,\r\nwas spending a few weeks in Gool-Gool until he should be fit for work\r\nagain.\r\n\r\nUncle had not been gone an hour when a drover appeared to report that\r\ntwenty thousand sheep would pass through on the morrow. Grass was\r\nprecious. It would not do to let the sheep spread and dawdle at their\r\ndrovers\' pleasure. There was not a man on the place; grannie was in a\r\ngreat stew; so I volunteered my services. At first she would not hear of\r\nsuch a thing, but eventually consented. With many injunctions to conduct\r\nmyself with proper stiffness, I started early on Monday morning. I was\r\nclad in a cool blouse, a holland riding-skirt, and a big straw hat; was\r\nseated on a big bay horse, was accompanied by a wonderful sheep-dog, and\r\ncarried a long heavy stock-whip. I sang and cracked my stock-whip as I\r\ncantered along, quite forgetting to be reserved and proper. Presently I\r\ncame upon the sheep just setting out for their day\'s tramp, with a black\r\nboy ahead of them, of whom I inquired which was the boss. He pointed\r\ntowards a man at the rear wearing a donkey-supper hat. I made my way\r\nthrough the sheep in his direction, and asked if he were in charge of\r\nthem. On being answered in the affirmative, I informed him that I was Mr\r\nBossier\'s niece, and, as the men were otherwise engaged, I would see the\r\nsheep through.\r\n\r\n"That\'s all right, miss. I will look out that you don\'t have much\r\ntrouble," he replied, politely raising his hat, while a look of\r\namusement played on his face.\r\n\r\nHe rode away, and shouted to his men to keep the flock strictly within\r\nbounds and make good travelling.\r\n\r\n"Right you are, boss," they answered; and returning to my side he told\r\nme his name was George Ledwood, and made some remarks about the great\r\ndrought and so on, while we rode in the best places to keep out of the\r\ndust and in the shade. I asked questions such as whence came the sheep?\r\nwhither were they bound? and how long had they been on the road? And\r\nhaving exhausted these orthodox remarks, we fell a-talking in dead\r\nearnest without the least restraint. I listened with interest to stories\r\nof weeks and weeks spent beneath the sun and stars while crossing widths\r\nof saltbush country, mulga and myall scrubs, of encounters with blacks\r\nin Queensland, and was favoured with a graphic description of a big\r\nstrike among the shearers when the narrator had been boss-of-the-board\r\nout beyond Bourke. He spoke as though well educated, and a gentleman--as\r\ndrovers often are. Why, then, was he on the road? I put him down as a\r\nscapegrace, for he had all the winning pleasant manner of a\r\nne\'er-do-well.\r\n\r\nAt noon--a nice, blazing, dusty noon--we halted within a mile of\r\nCaddagat for lunch. I could have easily ridden home for mine, but\r\npreferred to have it with the drovers for fun. The men boiled the billy\r\nand made the tea, which we drank out of tin pots, with tinned fish and\r\ndamper off tin plates as the completion of the menu, Mr Ledwood and I at\r\na little distance from the men. Tea boiled in a billy at a bush fire has\r\na deliciously aromatic flavour, and I enjoyed my birthday lunch\r\nimmensely. Leaving the cook to collect the things and put them in the\r\nspring-cart, we continued on our way, lazily lolling on our horses and\r\nchewing gum-leaves as we went.\r\n\r\nWhen the last of the sheep got off the Caddagat run it was nearing two\r\no\'clock.\r\n\r\nMr Ledwood and I shook hands at parting, each expressing a wish that we\r\nmight meet again some day.\r\n\r\nI turned and rode homewards. I looked back and saw the drover gazing\r\nafter me. I waved my hand; he raised his hat and smiled, displaying his\r\nteeth, a gleam of white in his sun-browned face. I kissed my hand to\r\nhim; he bowed low; I whistled to my dog; he resumed his way behind the\r\ncrawling sheep; I cantered home quickly and dismounted at the front gate\r\nat 2.30 p.m., a dusty, heated, tired girl.\r\n\r\nGrannie came out to question me regarding the sex, age, condition, and\r\nspecies of the sheep, what was their destination, whether they were in\r\nsearch of grass or were for sale, had they spread or eaten much grass,\r\nand had the men been civil?\r\n\r\nWhen I had satisfactorily informed her on all these points, she bade me\r\nhave something to eat, to bathe and dress, and gave me a holiday for the\r\nremainder of the day.\r\n\r\nMy hair was grey with dust, so I washed all over, arrayed myself in a\r\ncool white dress, and throwing myself in a squatter\'s chair in the\r\nveranda, spread my hair over the back of it to dry. Copies of Gordon,\r\nKendall, and Lawson were on my lap, but I was too physically content and\r\ncomfortable to indulge in even these, my sworn friends and companions. I\r\nsurrendered myself to the mere joy of being alive. How the sunlight\r\nblazed and danced in the roadway--the leaves of the gum-trees gleaming\r\nin it like a myriad gems! A cloud of white, which I knew to be\r\ncockatoos, circled over the distant hilltop. Nearer they wheeled until I\r\ncould hear their discordant screech. The thermometer on the wall rested\r\nat 104 degrees despite the dense shade thrown on the broad old veranda\r\nby the foliage of creepers, shrubs, and trees. The gurgling rush of the\r\ncreek, the scent of the flower-laden garden, and the stamp, stamp of a\r\nhorse in the orchard as he attempted to rid himself of tormenting flies,\r\nfilled my senses. The warmth was delightful. Summer is heavenly, I\r\nsaid--life is a joy.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen\'s slender fingers looked artistic among some pretty\r\nfancy-work upon which she was engaged. Bright butterflies flitted round\r\nthe garden, and thousands of bees droned lazily among the flowers. I\r\nclosed my eyes--my being filled with the beauty of it all.\r\n\r\nI could hear grannie\'s pen fly over the paper as she made out a list of\r\nChristmas supplies on a table near me.\r\n\r\n"Helen, I suppose a hundredweight of currants will be sufficient?"\r\n\r\n"Yes; I should think so."\r\n\r\n"Seven dozen yards of unbleached calico be enough?"\r\n\r\n"Yes; plenty."\r\n\r\n"Which tea-service did you order?"\r\n\r\n"Number two."\r\n\r\n"Do you or Sybylla want anything extra?"\r\n\r\n"Yes; parasols, gloves, and some books."\r\n\r\n"Books! Can I get them at Hordern\'s?"\r\n\r\n"Yes."\r\n\r\nGrannie\'s voice faded on my ears, my thoughts ran on uncle Jay-Jay. He\r\nhad promised to be home in time for my birthday spread, and I was sure\r\nhe had a present for me. What would it be?--something nice. He would be\r\nnearly sure to bring someone home with him from Cummabella, and we would\r\nhave games and fun to no end. I was just seventeen, only seventeen, and\r\nhad a long, long life before me wherein to enjoy myself. Oh, it was good\r\nto be alive! What a delightful place the world was!--so accommodating, I\r\nfelt complete mistress of it. It was like an orange--I merely had to\r\nsqueeze it and it gave forth sweets plenteously. The stream sounded far\r\naway, the sunlight blazed and danced, grannie\'s voice was a pleasant\r\nmurmur in my ear, the cockatoos screamed over the house and passed away\r\nto the west. Summer is heavenly and life is a joy, I reiterated. Joy!\r\nJoy! There was joy in the quit! quit! of the green-and-crimson parrots,\r\nwhich swung for a moment in the rose-bush over the gate, and then\r\nwhizzed on into the summer day. There was joy in the gleam of the sun\r\nand in the hum of the bees, and it throbbed in my heart. Joy! Joy! A\r\njackass laughed his joy as he perched on the telegraph wire out in the\r\nroad. Joy! joy! Summer is a dream of delight and life is a joy, I said\r\nin my heart. I was repeating the one thing over and over--but ah! it was\r\na measure of happiness which allowed of much repetition. The cool murmur\r\nof the creek grew far away, I felt my poetry books slip off my knees and\r\nfall to the floor, but I was too content to bother about them--too happy\r\nto need their consolation, which I had previously so often and so\r\nhungrily sought. Youth! Joy! Warmth!\r\n\r\nThe clack of the garden gate, as it swung to, awoke me from a pleasant\r\nsleep. Grannie had left the veranda, and on the table where she had been\r\nwriting aunt Helen was filling many vases with maidenhair fern and La\r\nFrance roses. A pleasant clatter from the dining-room announced that my\r\nbirthday tea was in active preparation. The position of the yellow\r\nsunbeams at the far end of the wide veranda told that the dense shadows\r\nwere lengthening, and that the last of the afternoon was wheeling\r\nwestward. Taking this in, in an instant I straightened the piece of\r\nmosquito-netting, which, to protect me from the flies, someone--auntie\r\nprobably--had spread across my face, and feigned to be yet asleep. By\r\nthe footsteps which sounded on the stoned garden walk, I knew that\r\nHarold Beecham was one of the individuals approaching.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"How do you do, Mrs Bell? Allow me to introduce my friend, Archie\r\nGoodchum. Mrs Bell, Mr Goodchum. Hasn\'t it been a roaster today?\r\nConsiderably over 100 degrees in the shade. Terribly hot!"\r\n\r\nAunt Helen acknowledged the introduction, and seated her guests, saying:\r\n\r\n"Harry, have you got an artistic eye? If so, you can assist me with\r\nthese flowers. So might Mr Goodchum, if he feels disposed."\r\n\r\nHarold accepted the proposal, and remarked:\r\n\r\n"What is the matter with your niece? It is the first time I ever saw her\r\nquiet."\r\n\r\n"Yes; she is a noisy little article--a perfect whirlwind in the\r\nhouse--but she is a little tired this afternoon; she has been seeing\r\nthose sheep through today."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t you think it would be a good lark if I get something and tickle\r\nher?" said Goodchum.\r\n\r\n"Yes, do," said Harold; "but look out for squalls. She is a great little\r\nfizzer."\r\n\r\n"Then she might be insulted."\r\n\r\n"Not she," interposed auntie. "No one will enjoy the fun more than\r\nherself."\r\n\r\nI had my eyes half open beneath the net, so saw him cautiously approach\r\nwith a rose-stem between his fingers. Being extremely sensitive to\r\ntickling, so soon as touched under the ear I took a flying leap from the\r\nchair somewhat disconcerting my tormentor.\r\n\r\nHe was a pleasant-looking young fellow somewhere about twenty, whose\r\nface was quite familiar to me.\r\n\r\nHe smiled so good-humouredly at me that I widely did the same in return,\r\nand he came forward with extended hand, exclaiming, "At last!"\r\n\r\nThe others looked on in surprise, Harold remarking suspiciously, "You\r\nsaid you were unacquainted with Miss Melvyn, but an introduction does\r\nnot seem necessary."\r\n\r\n"Oh, yes it is," chirped Mr Goodchum. "I haven\'t the slightest idea of\r\nthe young lady\'s name."\r\n\r\n"Don\'t know each other!" ejaculated Harold; and grannie, who had\r\nappeared upon the scene, inquired stiffly what we meant by such capers\r\nif unacquainted.\r\n\r\nMr Goodchum hastened to explain.\r\n\r\n"I have seen the young lady on several occasions in the bank where I am\r\nemployed, and I had the good fortune to be of a little service to her\r\none day when I was out biking. Her harness, or at least the harness on\r\nthe horse she was driving, broke, and I came to the rescue with my\r\npocket-knife and some string, thereby proving, if not ornamental, I was\r\nuseful. After that I tried hard to find out who she was, but my\r\ninquiries always came to nothing. I little dreamt who Miss Melvyn was\r\nwhen Harry, telling me she was a Goulburn girl, asked if I knew her."\r\n\r\n"Quite romantic," said aunt Helen, smiling; and a great thankfulness\r\novercame me that Mr Goodchum had been unable to discover my identity\r\nuntil now. It was right enough to be unearthed as Miss Melvyn,\r\ngrand-daughter of Mrs Bossier of Caddagat, and great friend and intimate\r\nof the swell Beechams of Five-Bob Downs station. At Goulburn I was only\r\nthe daughter of old Dick Melvyn, broken-down farmer-cockatoo, well known\r\nby reason of his sprees about the commonest pubs in town.\r\n\r\nMr Goodchum told us it was his first experience of the country, and\r\ntherefore he was enjoying himself immensely. He also mentioned that he\r\nwas anxious to see some of the gullies around Caddagat, which, he had\r\nheard, were renowned for the beauty of their ferns. Aunt Helen,\r\naccordingly, proposed a walk in the direction of one of them, and\r\nhurried off to attend to a little matter before starting. While waiting\r\nfor her, Harold happened to say it was my birthday, and Mr Goodchum\r\ntendered me the orthodox wishes, remarking, "It is surely pardonable at\r\nyour time of life to ask what age you have attained today?"\r\n\r\n"Seventeen."\r\n\r\n"Oh! oh! \'sweet seventeen, and never been kissed\'; but I suppose you\r\ncannot truthfully say that, Miss Melvyn?"\r\n\r\n"Oh yes, I can."\r\n\r\n"Well, you won\'t be able to say it much longer," he said, making a\r\nsuggestive move in my direction. I ran, and he followed, grannie\r\nreappearing from the dining-room just in time to see me bang the garden\r\ngate with great force on my pursuer.\r\n\r\n"What on earth is the girl doing now?" I heard her inquire.\r\n\r\nHowever, Mr Goodchum did not execute his threat; instead we walked along\r\ndecorously in the direction of the nearest ferns, while Harold and aunt\r\nHelen followed, the latter carrying a sun-bonnet for me.\r\n\r\nAfter we had climbed some distance up a gully aunt Helen called out that\r\nshe and Harold would rest while I did the honours of the fern grots to\r\nmy companion.\r\n\r\nWe went on and on, soon getting out of sight of the others.\r\n\r\n"What do you say to my carving our names on a gum-tree, the bark is so\r\nnice and soft?" said the bank clerk; and I seconded the proposal.\r\n\r\n"I will make it allegorical," he remarked, setting to work.\r\n\r\nHe was very deft with his penknife, and in a few minutes had carved S.\r\nP. M. and A. S. G., encircling the initials by a ring and two hearts\r\ninterlaced.\r\n\r\n"That\'ll do nicely," he remarked, and turning round, "Why, you\'ll get a\r\nsunstroke; do take my hat."\r\n\r\nI demurred, he pressed the matter, and I agreed on condition he allowed\r\nme to tie his handkerchief over his head. I was wearing his hat and\r\ntying the ends of a big silk handkerchief beneath his chin when the\r\ncracking of a twig caused me to look up and see Harold Beecham with an\r\nexpression on his face that startled me.\r\n\r\n"Your aunt sent me on with your hood," he said jerkily.\r\n\r\n"You can wear it--I\'ve been promoted," I said flippantly, raising my\r\nhead-gear to him and bowing. He did not laugh as he usually did at my\r\ntricks, but frowned darkly instead.\r\n\r\n"We\'ve been carving our names--at least, I have," remarked Goodchum.\r\n\r\nHarold tossed my sun-bonnet on the ground, and said shortly, "Come on,\r\nGoodchum, we must be going."\r\n\r\n"Oh, don\'t go, Mr Beecham. I thought you came on purpose for my birthday\r\ntea. Auntie has made me a tremendous cake. You must stay. We never\r\ndreamt of you doing anything else."\r\n\r\n"I\'ve changed my mind," he replied, striding on at such a pace that we\r\nhad difficulty in keeping near him. As we resumed our own head-wear,\r\nGood churn whispered, "A bulldog ant must have stung the boss. Let\'s ask\r\nhim."\r\n\r\nOn reaching the house we found other company had arrived in the persons\r\nof young Mr Goodjay from Cummabella, his sister, her governess, and a\r\ncouple of jackeroos. They were seated on the veranda, and uncle Jay-Jay,\r\nattired in his shirt-sleeves, was appearing through the dining-room door\r\nwith half a dozen bottles of home-made ginger ale in his arms. Dumping\r\nthem down on the floor, he produced a couple of tots from his\r\nshirt-pockets, saying, "Who votes for a draw of beer? Everyone must feel\r\ninclined for a swig. Harry, you want some; you don\'t look as though the\r\nheat was good for your temper. Hullo, Archie! Got up this far. Take a\r\ndraw out of one of these bottles. If there had been a dozen pubs on the\r\nroad, I\'d have drunk every one of em dry today. I never felt such a\r\ndaddy of a thirst on me before."\r\n\r\n"Good gracious, Julius!" exclaimed grannie, as he offered the governess\r\na pot full of beer, "Miss Craddock can\'t drink out of that pint."\r\n\r\n"Those who don\'t approve of my pints, let \'em bring their own," said\r\nthat mischievous uncle Jay-Jay, who was a great hand at acting the clown\r\nwhen he felt that way inclined.\r\n\r\nI was dispatched for glasses, and after emptying the bottles uncle\r\nproposed a game of tennis first, while the light lasted, and tea\r\nafterwards. This proposition being carried with acclamation, we\r\nproceeded to the tennis court. Harold came too--he had apparently\r\naltered his intention of going home immediately.\r\n\r\nThere were strawberries to be had in the orchard, also some late\r\ncherries, so uncle ordered me to go and get some. I procured a basket,\r\nand willingly agreed to obey him. Mr Goodchum offered to accompany me,\r\nbut Harold stepped forward saying he would go, in such a resolute tragic\r\nmanner that Goodchum winked audaciously, saying waggishly, "Behold, the\r\nhero descends into the burning mine!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER TWENTY-THREE\r\n\r\n    "Ah, For One Hour of Burning Love,\r\n    \'tis Worth an Age of Cold Respect!"\r\n\r\n\r\nWe walked in perfect silence, Harold not offering to carry my little\r\nbasket. I did not dare lift my eyes, as something told me the face of\r\nthe big man would not be pleasant to look upon just then. I twirled the\r\nring he had given me round and round my finger. I occasionally put it\r\non, wearing the stones on the palm-side of my finger, so that it would\r\nnot be taken for other than one of two or three aunt Helen had lent me,\r\nsaying I was at liberty to use them while at Caddagat, if it gave me any\r\npleasure.\r\n\r\nThe Caddagat orchard contained six acres, and being a narrow enclosure,\r\nand the cherries growing at the extreme end from the house, it took us\r\nsome time to reach them. I led the way to our destination--a secluded\r\nnook where grape-vines clambered up fig-trees, and where the top of\r\ngooseberry bushes met the lower limbs of cherry-trees. Blue and yellow\r\nlupins stood knee-high, and strawberries grew wild among them. We had\r\nnot uttered a sound, and I had not glanced at my companion. I stopped;\r\nhe wheeled abruptly and grasped my wrist in a manner which sent the\r\nbasket whirling from my hand. I looked up at his face, which was blazing\r\nwith passion, and dark with a darker tinge than Nature and the sun had\r\ngiven it, from the shapely swelling neck, in its soft well-turned-down\r\ncollar, to where the stiff black hair, wet with perspiration, hung on\r\nthe wide forehead.\r\n\r\n"Unhand me, sir!" I said shortly, attempting to wrench myself free, but\r\nI might as well have tried to pull away from a lion.\r\n\r\n"Unhand me!" I repeated.\r\n\r\nFor answer he took a firmer hold, in one hand seizing my arm above the\r\nelbow, and gripping my shoulder with the other so tightly that, through\r\nmy flimsy covering, his strong fingers bruised me so severely that in a\r\ncalmer moment I would have squirmed and cried out with pain.\r\n\r\n"How dare you touch me!" He drew me so closely to him that, through his\r\nthin shirt--the only garment on the upper part of his figure--I could\r\nfeel the heat of his body, and his big heart beating wildly.\r\n\r\nAt last! at last! I had waked this calm silent giant into life. After\r\nmany an ineffectual struggle I had got at a little real love or passion,\r\nor call it by any name--something wild and warm and splendidly alive\r\nthat one could feel, the most thrilling, electric, and exquisite\r\nsensation known.\r\n\r\nI thoroughly enjoyed the situation, but did not let this appear. A\r\nminute or two passed and he did not speak.\r\n\r\n"Mr Beecham, I\'ll trouble you to explain yourself. How dare you lay your\r\nhands upon me?"\r\n\r\n"Explain!" he breathed rather than spoke, in a tone of concentrated\r\nfury. "I\'ll make _you_ explain, and I\'ll do what I like with you. I\'ll\r\ntouch you as much as I think fit. I\'ll throw you over the fence if _you_\r\ndon\'t explain to _my_ satisfaction."\r\n\r\n"What is there that I can explain?"\r\n\r\n"Explain your conduct with other men. How dare you receive their\r\nattentions and be so friendly with them!"\r\n\r\n"How dare you speak to me like that! I reserve the right of behaving as\r\nI please without your permission."\r\n\r\n"I won\'t have a girl with my engagement ring on her finger going on as\r\nyou do. I think I have a right to complain, for I could get any amount\r\nof splendid women in every way to wear it for me, and behave themselves\r\nproperly too," he said fiercely.\r\n\r\nI tossed my head defiantly, saying, "Loose your hold of me, and I\'ll\r\nquickly explain matters to my own satisfaction and yours, Harold\r\nBeecham."\r\n\r\nHe let me go, and I stepped a pace or two away from him, drew the costly\r\nring from my finger, and, with indifference and contempt, tossed it to\r\nhis feet, where the juice of crushed strawberries was staining the\r\nground, and facing him, said mockingly:\r\n\r\n"Now, speak to the girl who wears your engagement ring, for I\'ll degrade\r\nmyself by wearing it no more. If you think I think you as great a catch\r\nas you think yourself, just because you have a little money, you are a\r\ntrifle mistaken, Mr Beecham, that is all. Ha ha ha! So you thought you\r\nhad a right to lecture me as your future slave! Just fancy! I never had\r\nthe slightest intention of marrying you. You were so disgustingly\r\nconceited that I have been attempting to rub a little of it out of you.\r\nMarry you! Ha ha! Because the social laws are so arranged that a woman\'s\r\nonly sphere is marriage, and because they endeavour to secure a man who\r\ncan give them a little more ease, you must not run away with the idea\r\nthat it is yourself they are angling for, when you are only the\r\nbothersome appendage with which they would have to put up, for the sake\r\nof your property. And you must not think that because some women will\r\nmarry for a home they all will. I trust I have explained to your\r\nsatisfaction, Mr Beecham. Ha ha ha!"\r\n\r\nThe jealous rage had died out of his face and was succeeded by trembling\r\nand a pallor so ghastly, that I began to have a little faith in\r\ndescriptions of love which I had hitherto ridiculed.\r\n\r\n"Are you in earnest?" he asked in a deadly calm voice.\r\n\r\n"Most emphatically I am."\r\n\r\n"Then all I can say is that I haven\'t much respect for you, Miss Melvyn.\r\nI always considered that there were three classes of women--one, that\r\nwould marry a blackfellow if he had money; another, that were shameless\r\nflirts, and who amuse themselves by flirting and disgracing the name of\r\nwoman; and a third class that were pure and true, on whom a man could\r\nstake his life and whom he could worship. I thought you belonged to this\r\nclass, but I have been mistaken. I know you always try to appear\r\nheartless and worthless, but I fancied it was only your youth and\r\nmischief, and imagined you were good underneath; but I have been\r\nmistaken," he repeated with quiet contempt.\r\n\r\nHis face had regained its natural colour, and the well-cut pleasant\r\nmouth, clearly seen beneath the soft drooping moustache, had hardened\r\ninto a sullen line which told me he would never be first to seek\r\nreconciliation--not even to save his life.\r\n\r\n"Bah!"
