I asked;\r\nbut this would cause too much waste, she considered.\r\n\r\nNext I suggested that the piano should be tuned, but they were united in\r\ntheir disapproval of such a fearful extravagance. "The peeany makes a\r\ngood nise. What ails it?"\r\n\r\nThen I suggested that the children should be kept tidier, for which I\r\nwas insulted by their father. I wanted them to be dressed up like\r\nswells, and if he did that he would soon be a pauper like my father.\r\nThis I found was the sentiment of the whole family regarding me. I was\r\nonly the daughter of old hard-up Melvyn, consequently I had little\r\nweight with the children, which made things very hard for me as a\r\nteacher.\r\n\r\nOne day at lunch I asked my mistress if she would like the children to\r\nbe instructed in table-manners. "Certainly," her husband replied, so I\r\ncommenced.\r\n\r\n"Jimmy, you must never put your knife in your mouth."\r\n\r\n"Pa does at any rate," replied Jimmy.\r\n\r\n"Yes," said pa; "and I\'m a richer man today than them as didn\'t do it."\r\n\r\n"Liza, do not put a whole slice of bread to your mouth like that, and\r\ncram so. Cut it into small pieces."\r\n\r\n"Ma doesn\'t," returned Liza.\r\n\r\n"Ye\'ll have yer work cut out with \'em," laughed Mrs M\'Swat, who did not\r\nknow how to correct her family herself, and was too ignorant to uphold\r\nmy authority.\r\n\r\nThat was my only attempt at teaching manners there. In the face of such\r\nodds it was a bootless task, and as there were not enough knives and\r\nforks to go round, I could not inculcate the correct method of handling\r\nthose implements.\r\n\r\nMrs M\'Swat had but one boiler in which to do all her cooking, and one\r\nsmall tub for the washing, and there was seldom anything to eat but\r\nbread and beef; and this was not because they were poor, but because\r\nthey did not know, or want to know, any better.\r\n\r\nTheir idea of religion, pleasure, manners, breeding, respectability,\r\nlove, and everything of that ilk, was the possession of money, and their\r\none idea of accumulating wealth was by hard sordid dragging and\r\ngrinding.\r\n\r\nA man who rises from indigence to opulence by business capabilities must\r\nhave brains worthy of admiration, but the man who makes a fortune as\r\nM\'Swat of Barney\'s Gap was making his must be dirt mean, grasping,\r\nnarrow-minded, and soulless--to me the most uncongenial of my fellows.\r\n\r\nI wrote once more to my mother, to receive the same reply. One hope\r\nremained. I would write to aunt Helen. She understood me somewhat, and\r\nwould know how I felt.\r\n\r\nActing on this inspiration, I requested her to plead for me. Her answer\r\ncame as a slap in the face, as I had always imagined her above the\r\ncommon cant of ordinary religionists. She stated that life was full of\r\ntrials. I must try and bear this little cross patiently, and at the end\r\nof a year they might have me back at Caddagat. A year! A year at\r\nBarney\'s Gap! The possibility of such a thing made me frantic. I picked\r\nup my pen and bitterly reproached my aunt in a letter to which she did\r\nnot deign to reply; and from that day to this she has rigidly ignored\r\nme--never so much as sending me the most commonplace message, or\r\ncasually using my name in her letters to my mother.\r\n\r\nAunt Helen, is there such a thing as firm friendship when even\r\nyours--best of women--quibbled and went under at the hysterical wail\r\nfrom the overburdened heart of a child?\r\n\r\nMy predecessor, previous to her debut at Barney\'s Gap, had spent some\r\ntime in a lunatic asylum, and being a curious character, allowed the\r\nchildren to do as they pleased, consequently they knew not what it meant\r\nto be ruled, and were very hold. They attempted no insubordination while\r\ntheir father was about the house, but when he was absent they gave me a\r\ndog\'s life, their mother sometimes smiling on their pranks, often lazily\r\nheedless of them, but never administering any form of correction.\r\n\r\nIf I walked away from the house to get rid of them, they would follow\r\nand hoot at me; and when I reproved them they informed me they were not\r\ngoing "to knuckle under to old Melvyn\'s darter, the damnedest fool in\r\nthe world, who\'s lost all his prawperty, and has to borry money off of\r\npa."\r\n\r\nDid I shut myself in my room, they shoved sticks in the cracks and made\r\ngrimaces at me. I knew the fallacy of appealing to their father, as they\r\nand their mother would tell falsehoods, and my word would not be taken\r\nin contradiction of theirs. I had experience of this, as the\r\npostmistress had complained of Jimmy, to be insulted by his father, who\r\ncould see no imperfection in his children.\r\n\r\nM\'Swat was much away from home at that time. The drought necessitated\r\nthe removal of some of his sheep, for which he had rented a place eighty\r\nmiles coastwards. There he left them under the charge of a man, but he\r\nrepaired thither frequently to inspect them. Sometimes he was away from\r\nhome a fortnight at a stretch. Peter would be away at work all day, and\r\nthe children took advantage of my defenceless position. Jimmy was the\r\nringleader. I could easily have managed the others had he been removed.\r\nI would have thrashed him well at the start but for the letters I\r\nconstantly received from home warning me against offence to the parents,\r\nand knew that to set my foot on the children\'s larrikinism would require\r\nmeasures that would gain their mother\'s ill-will at once. But when\r\nM\'Swat left home for three weeks Jim got so bold that I resolved to take\r\ndecisive steps towards subjugating him. I procured a switch--a very\r\nsmall one, as his mother had a great objection to corporal\r\npunishment--and when, as usual, he commenced to cheek me during lessons,\r\nI hit him on the coat-sleeve. The blow would not have brought tears from\r\nthe eyes of a toddler, but this great calf emitted a wild yope, and\r\nopening his mouth let his saliva pour on to his slate. The others set up\r\nsuch blood-curdling yells in concert that I was a little disconcerted,\r\nbut I determined not to give in. I delivered another tap, whereupon he\r\nsquealed and roared so that he brought his mother to his rescue like a\r\nton of bricks on stilts, a great fuss in her eyes which generally beamed\r\nwith a cowful calm.\r\n\r\nSeizing my arm she shook me like a rat, broke my harmless little stick\r\nin pieces, threw it in my face, and patting Jimmy on the shoulder, said:\r\n\r\n"Poor man! She sharn\'t touch me Jimmy while I know. Sure you\'ve got no\r\nsense. You\'d had him dead if I hadn\'t come in."\r\n\r\nI walked straight to my room and shut myself in, and did not teach any\r\nmore that afternoon. The children rattled on my door-handle and jeered:\r\n\r\n"She thought she\'d hit me, but ma settled her. Old poor Melvyn\'s darter\r\nwon\'t try no more of her airs on us."\r\n\r\nI pretended not to hear. What was I to do? There was no one to whom I\r\ncould turn for help. M\'Swat would believe the story of his family, and\r\nmy mother would blame me. She would think I had been in fault because I\r\nhated the place.\r\n\r\nMrs M\'Swat called me to tea, but I said I would not have any. I lay\r\nawake all night and got desperate. On the morrow I made up my mind to\r\nconquer or leave. I would stand no more. If in all the wide world and\r\nthe whole of life this was the only use for me, then I would die--take\r\nmy own life if necessary.\r\n\r\nThings progressed as usual next morning. I attended to my duties and\r\nmarched my scholars into the schoolroom at the accustomed hour. There\r\nwas no decided insubordination during the morning, but I felt Jimmy was\r\nwaiting for an opportunity to defy me. It was a fearful day, possessed\r\nby a blasting wind laden with red dust from Riverina, which filled the\r\nair like a fog. The crockery ware became so hot in the kitchen that when\r\ntaking it into the dining-room we had to handle it with cloths. During\r\nthe dinner-hour I slipped away unnoticed to where some quince-trees were\r\ngrowing and procured a sharp rod, which I secreted among the flour-bags\r\nin the schoolroom. At half-past one I brought my scholars in and ordered\r\nthem to their work with a confident air. Things went without a ripple\r\nuntil three o\'clock, when the writing lesson began. Jimmy struck his pen\r\non the bottom of the bottle every time he replenished it with ink.\r\n\r\n"Jimmy," I gently remonstrated, "don\'t jab your pen like that--it will\r\nspoil it. There is no necessity to shove it right to the bottom."\r\n\r\nJab, jab, went Jimmy\'s pen.\r\n\r\n"Jimmy, did you hear me speak to you?"\r\n\r\nJab went the pen.\r\n\r\n"James, I am speaking to you!"\r\n\r\nJab went the pen again.\r\n\r\n"James," I said sternly, "I give you one more chance."\r\n\r\nHe deliberately defied me by stabbing into the ink-bottle with increased\r\nvigour. Liza giggled triumphantly, and the little ones strove to emulate\r\nher. I calmly produced my switch and brought it smartly over the\r\nshoulders of my refractory pupil in a way that sent the dust in a cloud\r\nfrom his dirty coat, knocked the pen from his fingers, and upset the\r\nink.\r\n\r\nHe acted as before--yelled ear-drum-breakingly, letting the saliva from\r\nhis distended mouth run on his copy-book. His brothers and sisters also\r\nstarted to roar, but bringing the rod down on the table, I threatened to\r\nthrash every one of them if they so much as whimpered; and they were so\r\ndumbfounded that they sat silent in terrified surprise. Jimmy continued\r\nto bawl. I hit him again.\r\n\r\n"Cease instantly, sir."\r\n\r\nThrough the cracks Mrs M\'Swat could be seen approaching. Seeing her,\r\nJimmy hollered anew. I expected her to attack me. She stood five feet\r\nnine inches, and weighed about sixteen stones; I measured five feet one\r\ninch, and turned the scale at eight stones--scarcely a fair match; but\r\nmy spirit was aroused, and instead of feeling afraid, I rejoiced at the\r\nencounter which was imminent, and had difficulty to refrain from\r\nshouting "Come on! I\'m ready, physically and mentally, for you and a\r\ndozen others such."\r\n\r\nMy curious ideas regarding human equality gave me confidence. My theory\r\nis that the cripple is equal to the giant, and the idiot to the genius.\r\nAs, if on account of his want of strength the cripple is subservient to\r\nthe giant, the latter, on account of that strength, is compelled to give\r\nin to the cripple. So with the dolt and the man of brain, so with Mrs\r\nM\'Swat and me.\r\n\r\nThe fact of not only my own but my family\'s dependence on M\'Swat--sank\r\ninto oblivion. I merely recognized that she was one human being and I\r\nanother. Should I have been deferential to her by reason of her age and\r\nmaternity, then from the vantage which this gave her, she should have\r\nbeen lenient to me on account of my chit-ship and inexperience. Thus we\r\nwere equal.\r\n\r\nJimmy hollered with renewed energy to attract his mother, and I\r\ncontinued to rain blows across his shoulders. Mrs M\'Swat approached to\r\nwithin a foot of the door, and then, as though changing her mind,\r\nretraced her steps and entered the hot low-roofed kitchen. I knew I had\r\nwon, and felt disappointed that the conquest had been so easy. Jimmy,\r\nseeing he was worsted, ceased his uproar, cleaned his copy-book on his\r\nsleeve, and sheepishly went on with his writing.\r\n\r\nWhether Mrs M\'Swat saw she had been in fault the day before I know not;\r\ncertain it is that the children ever after that obeyed me, and I heard\r\nno more of the matter; neither, as far as I could ascertain, did the\r\n"ruction" reach the ears of M\'Swat.\r\n\r\n"How long, how long!" was my cry, as I walked out ankle-deep in the dust\r\nto see the sun, like a ball of blood, sink behind the hills on that\r\nFebruary evening.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY\r\n\r\nWhere Ignorance is Bliss, \'Tis Folly to be Wise\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen by myself, I fretted so constantly that the traces it left upon me\r\nbecame evident even to the dull comprehension of Mrs M\'Swat.\r\n\r\n"I don\'t hold with too much pleasure and disherpation, but you ain\'t had\r\novermuch of it lately. You\'ve stuck at home pretty constant, and ye and\r\nLizer can have a little fly round. It\'ll do yous good," she said.\r\n\r\nThe dissipation, pleasure, and flying round allotted to "Lizer" and me\r\nwere to visit some of the neighbours. Those, like the M\'Swats, were\r\nsheep-farming selectors. They were very friendly and kind to me, and I\r\nfound them superior to my employers, in that their houses were\r\nbeautifully clean; but they lived the same slow life, and their soul\'s\r\nexistence fed on the same small ideas. I was keenly disappointed that\r\nnone of them had a piano, as my hunger for music could be understood\r\nonly by one with a passion for that art.\r\n\r\nI borrowed something to read, but all that I could get in the way of\r\nbooks were a few _Young Ladies\' Journals_, which I devoured ravenously,\r\nso to speak.\r\n\r\nWhen Lizer\'s back would be turned, the girls would ask me how I managed\r\nto live at Barney\'s Gap, and expressed themselves of the opinion that it\r\nwas the most horrible hole in the world, and Mrs M\'Swat the dirtiest\r\ncreature living, and that they would not go there for 50 pounds a week.\r\nI made a point of never saying anything against Mrs M\'Swat; but I fumed\r\ninwardly that this life was forced upon me, when girls with no longings\r\nor aspirations beyond being the wife of a Peter M\'Swat recoiled from the\r\nthought of it.\r\n\r\nMy mother insisted upon my writing to her regularly, so once a week I\r\nheaded a letter "Black\'s Camp", and condemned the place, while mother as\r\nunfailingly replied that these bad times I should be thankful to God\r\nthat I was fed and clothed. I knew this as well as any one, and was\r\naware there were plenty of girls willing to jump at my place; but they\r\nwere of different temperament to me, and when one is seventeen, that\r\nkind of reasoning does not weigh very heavily.\r\n\r\nMy eldest brother, Horace, twin brother of my sister Gertie, took it\r\nupon himself to honour me with the following letter:\r\n\r\n\r\nWhy the deuce don\'t you give up writing those letters to mother? We get\r\ntongue-pie on account of them, and it\'s not as if they did you any good.\r\nIt only makes mother more determined to leave you where you are. She\r\nsays you are that conceited you think you ought to have something\r\nbetter, and you\'re not fit for the place you have, and she\'s glad it is\r\nsuch a place, and it will do you the world of good and take the nonsense\r\nout of you--that it\'s time you got a bit of sense. Sullivan\'s Ginger.\r\nAfter she gets your letters she does jaw, and wishes she never had a\r\nchild, and what a good mother she is, and what bad devils we are to her.\r\nYou are a fool not to stay where you are. I wish I could get away to\r\nM\'Swat or Mack Pot, and I would jump at the chance like a good un. The\r\nboss still sprees and loafs about town till some one has to go and haul\r\nhim home. I\'m about full of him, and I\'m going to leave home before next\r\nChristmas, or my name ain\'t what it is. Mother says the kiddies would\r\nstarve if I leave; but Stanley is coming on like a haystack, I tell him,\r\nand he does kick up, and he ought to be able to plough next time. I\r\nploughed when I was younger than him. I put in fourteen acres of wheat\r\nand oats this year, and I don\'t think I\'ll cut a wheelbarrow-load of it.\r\nI\'m full of the place. I never have a single penny to my name, and it\r\nain\'t father\'s drinking that\'s all to blame; if he didn\'t booze it\r\nwouldn\'t be much better. It\'s the slowest hole in the world, and I\'ll\r\nchuck it and go shearing or droving. I hate this dairying, it\'s too slow\r\nfor a funeral: there would be more life in trapping \'possums out on\r\nTimlinbilly. Mother always says to have patience, and when the drought\r\nbreaks and good seasons come round again things will be better, but it\'s\r\nno good of trying to stuff me like that. I remember when the seasons\r\nwere wet. It was no good growing anything, because every one grew so\r\nmuch that there was no market, and the sheep died of foot-rot and you\r\ncouldn\'t give your butter away, and it is not much worse to have nothing\r\nto sell than not be able to sell a thing when you have it. And the long\r\nand short of it is that I hate dairying like blue murder. It\'s as tame\r\nas a clucking hen. Fancy a cove sitting down every morning and evening\r\npulling at a cow\'s tits fit to bust himself, and then turning an old\r\nseparator, and washing it up in a dish of water like a blooming girl\'s\r\nwork. And if you go to a picnic, just when the fun commences you have to\r\nnick off home and milk, and when you tog yourself on Sunday evening you\r\nhave to undress again and lay into the milking, and then you have to\r\nchange everything on you and have a bath, or your best girl would scent\r\nthe cow-yard on you, and not have you within cooee of her. We won\'t know\r\nwhat rain is when we see it; but I suppose it will come in floods and\r\nfinish the little left by the drought. The grasshoppers have eaten all\r\nthe fruit and even the bark off the trees, and the caterpillars made a\r\ncroker of the few tomatoes we kept alive with the suds. All the cockeys\r\nround here and dad are applying to the Government to have their rents\r\nsuspended for a time. We have not heard yet whether it will be granted,\r\nbut if Gov. doesn\'t like it, they\'ll have to lump it, for none of us\r\nhave a penny to bless ourselves with, let alone dub up for taxes. I\'ve\r\nwritten you a long letter, and if you growl about the spelling and\r\ngrammar I won\'t write to you any more, so there, and you take my tip and\r\ndon\'t write to mother on that flute any more, for she won\'t take a bit\r\nof notice.\r\n\r\nYr loving brother,\r\n\r\nHorace.\r\n\r\n\r\nSo! Mother had no pity for me, and the more I pleaded with her the more\r\ndetermined she grew upon leaving me to suffer on, so I wrote to her no\r\nmore. However, I continued to correspond with grannie, and in one of her\r\nletters she told me that Harry Beecham (that was in February) was still\r\nin Sydney settling his affairs; but when that was concluded he was going\r\nto Queensland. He had put his case in the hands of squatters he had\r\nknown in his palmy days, and the first thing that turned up in managing\r\nor overseeing he was to have; but for the present he had been offered\r\nthe charge of 1600 head of bullocks from a station up near the Gulf of\r\nCarpentaria overland to Victoria. Uncle Jay-Jay was not home yet: he had\r\nextended his tour to Hong Kong, and grannie was afraid he was spending\r\ntoo much money, as in the face of the drought she had difficulty in\r\nmaking both ends meet, and feared she would be compelled to go on the\r\nbanks. She grieved that I was not becoming more reconciled to my place.\r\nIt was dull, no doubt, but it would do my reputation no harm, whereas,\r\nwere I in a lively situation, there might be numerous temptations hard\r\nto resist. Why did I not try to look at it in that way?\r\n\r\nShe sent a copy of the _Australasian_, which was a great treat to me,\r\nalso to the children, as they were quite ignorant of the commonest\r\nthings in life, and the advent of this illustrated paper was an event to\r\nbe recorded in the diary in capital letters. They clustered round me\r\neagerly to see the pictures. In this edition there chanced to be a page\r\ndevoted to the portraits of eleven Australian singers, and our eyes fell\r\non Madame Melba, who was in the middle. As what character she was\r\ndressed I do not remember, but she looked magnificent. There was a crown\r\nupon her beautiful head, the plentiful hair was worn flowing, and the\r\nshapely bosom and arms exposed.\r\n\r\n"Who\'s that?" they inquired.\r\n\r\n"Madame Melba; did you ever hear her name?"\r\n\r\n"Who\'s Madame Melba? What\'s she do? Is she a queen?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, a queen, and a great queen of song;" and being inspired with great\r\nadmiration for our own Australian cantatrice, who was great among the\r\ngreatest prima-donnas of the world, I began to tell them a little of her\r\nfame, and that she had been recently offered 40,000 pounds to sing for\r\nthree months in America.\r\n\r\nThey were incredulous. Forty thousand pounds! Ten times as much as "pa"\r\nhad given for a paid-up selection he had lately bought. They told me it\r\nwas no use of me trying to tell them fibs. No one would give a woman\r\nanything to sing, not even one pound. Why, Susie Duffy was the best\r\nsinger on the Murrumbidgee, and she would sing for any one who asked\r\nher, and free of charge.\r\n\r\nAt this juncture Jimmy, who had been absent, came to see the show. After\r\ngazing for a few seconds he remarked what the others had failed to\r\nobserve, "Why, the woman\'s naked!"\r\n\r\nI attempted to explain that among rich people in high society it was\r\ncustomary to dress like that in the evening, and that it looked very\r\npretty.\r\n\r\nMrs M\'Swat admonished me for showing the children low pictures.\r\n\r\n"She must be a very bold woman," said Jimmy; and Lizer pronounced her\r\nmad because, as she put it, "It\'s a wonder she\'d be half-undressed in\r\nher photo; you\'d think she oughter dress herself up complete then."\r\n\r\nLizer certainly acted upon this principle, as a photo of her, which had\r\nbeen taken by a travelling artist, bore evidence that for the occasion\r\nshe had arrayed herself in two pairs of ill-fitting cuffs, Peter\'s watch\r\nand chain, strings, jackets, flowers, and other gewgaws galore.\r\n\r\n"There ain\'t no such person as Madame Melber; it\'s only a fairy-tale,"\r\nsaid Mrs M\'Swat.\r\n\r\n"Did you ever hear of Gladstone?" I inquired.\r\n\r\n"No; where is that place?"\r\n\r\n"Did you ever hear of Jesus Christ?"\r\n\r\n"Sure, yes; he\'s got something to do with God, ain\'t he?"\r\n\r\nAfter that I never attempted to enlighten them regarding our\r\ncelebrities.\r\n\r\nOh, how I envied them their ignorant contentment! They were as ducks on\r\na duck-pond; but I was as a duck forced for ever to live in a desert,\r\never wildly longing for water, but never reaching it outside of dreams.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-ONE\r\n\r\nMr M\'Swat and I Have a Bust-up\r\n\r\n\r\nMen only, and they merely on business, came to Barney\'s Gap--women\r\ntabooed the place. Some of them told me they would come to see me, but\r\nnot Mrs M\'Swat, as she always allowed the children to be as rude to them\r\nas they pleased. With the few individuals who chanced to come M\'Swat\r\nwould sit down, light his pipe, and vulgarly and profusely expectorate\r\non the floor, while they yarned and yarned for hours and hours about the\r\nprice of wool, the probable breeding capacity of the male stock they\r\nkept, and of the want of grass--never a word about their country\'s\r\npolitics or the events of the day; even the news of the "Mountain\r\nMurders" by Butler had not penetrated here. I wondered if they were\r\nacquainted with the names of their Governor and Prime Minister.\r\n\r\nIt was not the poor food and the filthy way of preparing it that worried\r\nme, or that Mr M\'Swat used "damn" on an average twice in five minutes\r\nwhen conversing, or that the children for ever nagged about my father\'s\r\npoverty and tormented me in a thousand other ways--it was the dead\r\nmonotony that was killing me.\r\n\r\nI longed feveredly for something to happen. Agony is a tame word\r\nwherewith to express what that life meant to me. Solitary confinement to\r\na gipsy would be something on a par.\r\n\r\nEvery night unfailingly when at home M\'Swat sat in the bosom of his\r\nfamily and speculated as to how much richer he was than his neighbours,\r\nwhat old Recce lived on, and who had the best breed of sheep and who was\r\nthe smartest at counting these animals, until the sordidness of it\r\nturned me dizzy, and I would steal out under the stars to try and cool\r\nmy heated spirit. This became a practice with me, and every night I\r\nwould slip away out of hearing of the household to sing the songs I had\r\nheard at Caddagat, and in imagination to relive every day and hour\r\nthere, till the thing became too much for me, and I was scarcely\r\nresponsible for my actions. Often I knelt on the parched ground beneath\r\nthe balmy summer sky to pray--wild passionate prayers that were never\r\nanswered.\r\n\r\nI was under the impression that my nightly ramble was not specially\r\nnoticed by any one, but I was mistaken. Mr M\'Swat, it appears, suspected\r\nme of having a lover, but was never able to catch me red-handed.\r\n\r\nThe possibility of a girl going out at night to gaze at the stars and\r\ndream was as improbable a thought for him as flying is to me, and having\r\nno soul above mud, had I attempted an explanation he would have\r\nconsidered me mad, and dangerous to have about the place.\r\n\r\nPeter, junior, had a sweetheart, one Susie Duffy, who lived some miles\r\non the other side of the Murrumbidgee. He was in the habit of courting\r\nher every Sunday and two or three nights during the week, and I often\r\nheard the clang of his stirrup-irons and the clink of hobble-chain when\r\nhe returned late; but on one occasion I stayed out later than usual, and\r\nhe passed me going home. I stood still and he did not see me, but his\r\nhorse shied violently. I thought he would imagine I was a ghost, so\r\ncalled out:\r\n\r\n"It is I."\r\n\r\n"Well, I\'ll be hanged! What are ye doin\' at this time ev night. Ain\'t\r\nyuz afraid of ghosts?"\r\n\r\n"Oh dear no. I had a bad headache and couldn\'t sleep, so came out to try\r\nif a walk would cure it," I explained.\r\n\r\nWe were a quarter of a mile or so from the house, so Peter slackened his\r\nspeed that I might keep pace with him. His knowledge of \'etiquette did\r\nnot extend as far as dismounting. There is a great difference between\r\nrudeness and ignorance. Peter was not rude; he was merely ignorant. For\r\nthe same reason he let his mother feed the pigs, clean his boots, and\r\nchop wood, while he sat down and smoked and spat. It was not that he was\r\nunmanly, as that this was the only manliness he had known.\r\n\r\n\r\nI was alone in the schoolroom next afternoon when Mr M\'Swat sidled in,\r\nand after stuttering and hawing a little, delivered himself of:\r\n\r\n"I want to tell ye that I don\'t hold with a gu-r-r-r-l going out of\r\nnights for to meet young men: if ye want to do any coortin\' yuz can do\r\nit inside, if it\'s a decent young man. I have no objections to yer\r\nhangin\' yer cap up to our Peter, only that ye have no prawperty--in\r\nyerself I like ye well enough, but we have other views for Peter. He\'s\r\nalmost as good as made it sure with Susie Duffy, an\' as ole Duffy will\r\nhave a bit ev prawperty I want him to git her, an\' wouldn\'t like ye to\r\nspoil the fun."\r\n\r\nPeter was "tall and freckled and sandy, face of a country lout", and,\r\nlike Middleton\'s rouse-about, "hadn\'t any opinions, hadn\'t any ideas",\r\nbut possessed sufficient instinct and common bushcraft with which, by\r\nhard slogging, to amass money. He was developing a moustache, and had a\r\n"gu-r-r-r-l"; he wore tight trousers and long spurs; he walked with a\r\nsidling swagger that was a cross between shyness and flashness, and took\r\nas much pride in his necktie as any man; he had a kind heart, honest\r\nprinciples, and would not hurt a fly; he worked away from morning till\r\nnight, and contentedly did his duty like a bullock in the sphere in\r\nwhich God had placed him; he never had a bath while I knew him, and was\r\na man according to his lights. He knew there was such a thing as the\r\noutside world, as I know there is such a thing as algebra; but it\r\ntroubled him no more than algebra troubles me.\r\n\r\nThis was my estimation of Peter M\'Swat, junior. I respected him right\r\nenough in his place, as I trust he respected me in mine, but though fate\r\nthought fit for the present to place us in the one groove, yet our lives\r\nwere unmixable commodities as oil and water, which lay apart and would\r\nnever meet until taken in hand by the omnipotent leveller--death.\r\n\r\nMarriage with Peter M\'Swat!\r\n\r\nConsternation and disgust held me speechless, and yet I was half\r\ninclined to laugh at the preposterousness of the thing, when Peter\'s\r\nfather continued:\r\n\r\n"I\'m sorry if you\'ve got smitten on Peter, but I know you\'ll be\r\nsensible. Ye see I have a lot of children, and when the place is divided\r\namong \'em it won\'t be much. I tell ye wot, old Duffy has a good bit of\r\nmoney and only two children, Susie and Mick. I could get you to meet\r\nMick--he mayn\'t be so personable as our Peter," he reflected, with\r\nevident pride in his weedy firstborn, and he got no farther, for I had\r\nbeen as a yeast-bottle bubbling up, and now went off bang!\r\n\r\n"Silence, you ignorant old creature! How dare you have the incomparable\r\nimpertinence to mention my name in conjunction with that of your boor of\r\na son. Though he were a millionaire I would think his touch\r\ncontamination. You have fallen through for once if you imagine I go out\r\nat night to meet any one--I merely go away to be free for a few minutes\r\nfrom the suffocating atmosphere of your odious home. You must not think\r\nthat because you have grasped and slaved and got a little money that it\r\nmakes a gentleman of you; and never you _dare_ to again mention my name\r\nin regard to matrimony with any one about here;" and with my head high\r\nand shoulders thrown back I marched to my room, where I wept till I was\r\nweak and ill.\r\n\r\nThis monotonous sordid life was unhinging me, and there was no\r\nlegitimate way of escape from it. I formed wild plans of running away,\r\nto do what I did not care so long as it brought a little action,\r\nanything but this torturing maddening monotony; but my love for my\r\nlittle brothers and sisters held me back. I could not do anything that\r\nwould put me for ever beyond the pale of their society.\r\n\r\nI was so reduced in spirit that had Harold Beecham appeared then with a\r\nmatrimonial scheme to be fulfilled at once, I would have quickly erased\r\nthe fine lines I had drawn and accepted his proposal; but he did not\r\ncome, and I was unacquainted with his whereabouts or welfare. As I\r\nremembered him, how lovable and superior he seemed in comparison with\r\nthe men I met nowadays: not that he was any better than these men in\r\ntheir place and according to their lights, but his lights--at least not\r\nhis lights, for Harold Beecham was nothing of a philosopher, but the\r\nfurniture of the drawing-room which they illuminated--was more artistic.\r\nWhat a prince of gentlemanliness and winning gallantries he was in his\r\nquiet way!\r\n\r\nThis information concerning him was in a letter I received from my\r\ngrandmother at Easter:\r\n\r\n\r\nWho should surprise us with a visit the other day but Harold Beecham. He\r\nwas as thin as a whipping-post, and very sunburnt [I smiled, imagining\r\nit impossible for Harold to be any browner than of yore]. He has been\r\nnear death\'s door with the measles--caught them in Queensland while\r\ndroving, and got wet. He was so ill that he had to give up charge of\r\nthat 1600 head of cattle he was bringing. He came to say good-bye to us,\r\nas he is off to Western Australia next week to see if he can mend his\r\nfortunes there. I was afraid he was going to be like young Charters, and\r\nswear he would never come back unless he made a pile, but he says he\r\nwill be back next Christmas three years for certain, if he is alive and\r\nkicking, as he says himself.\r\n\r\nWhy he intends returning at that stipulated time I don\'t know, as he\r\nnever was very communicative, and is more unsociable than ever now. He\r\nis a man who never shows his feelings, but he must feel the loss of his\r\nold position deeply. He seemed surprised not to find you here, and says\r\nit was a pity to set you teaching, as it will take all the life and fun\r\nout of you, and that is the first time I ever heard him express an\r\nopinion in any one\'s business but his own. Frank Hawden sends kind\r\nregards, &c.\r\n\r\n\r\nTeaching certainly had the effect upon me anticipated by Harold Beecham,\r\nbut it was not the teaching but the place in which I taught which was\r\ndoing the mischief--good, my mother termed it.\r\n\r\nI was often sleepless for more than forty-eight hours at a stretch, and\r\ncried through the nights until my eyes had black rings round them, which\r\nwashing failed to remove. The neighbours described me as "a sorrowful\r\nlookin\' delicate creetur\', that couldn\'t larf to save her life"--quite a\r\ndifferent character to the girl who at Caddagat was continually chid for\r\nbeing a romp, a hoyden, a boisterous tomboy, a whirlwind, and for\r\nexcessive laughter at anything and everything. I got into such a state\r\nof nervousness that I would jump at the opening of a door or an\r\nunexpected footfall.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen cooling down, after having so vigorously delivered Mr M\'Swat a\r\npiece of my mind, I felt that I owed him an apology. According to his\r\nlights (and that is the only fair way of judging our fellows) he had\r\nacted in a kind of fatherly way. I was a young girl under his charge,\r\nand he would have in a measure been responsible had I come to harm\r\nthrough going out in the night. He had been good-natured, too, in\r\noffering to help things along by providing an eligible, and allowing us\r\nto "spoon" under his surveillance. That I was of temperament and\r\naspirations that made his plans loathsome to me was no fault of\r\nhis--only a heavy misfortune to myself. Yes; I had been in the wrong\r\nentirely.\r\n\r\nWith this idea in my head, sinking ankle-deep in the dust, and threading\r\nmy way through the pigs and fowls which hung around the back door, I\r\nwent in search of my master. Mrs M\'Swat was teaching Jimmy how to kill a\r\nsheep and dress it for use; while Lizer, who was nurse to the baby and\r\nspectator of the performance, was volubly and ungrammatically giving\r\ninstructions in the art. Peter and some of the younger children were\r\naway felling stringybark-trees for the sustenance of the sheep. The fall\r\nof their axes and the murmur of the Murrumbidgee echoed faintly from the\r\nsunset. They would be home presently and at tea; I reflected it would be\r\n"The old yeos looks terrible skinny, but the hoggets is fat yet. By\r\ncrikey! They did go into the bushes. They chawed up stems and all--some\r\nas thick as a pencil."\r\n\r\nThis information in that parlance had been given yesterday, the day\r\nbefore, would be given today, tomorrow, and the next day. It was the\r\nboss item on the conversational programme until further orders.\r\n\r\nI had a pretty good idea where to find Mr M\'Swat, as he had lately\r\npurchased a pair of stud rams, and was in the habit of admiring them for\r\na couple of hours every evening. I went to where they usually grazed,\r\nand there, as I expected, found Mr M\'Swat, pipe in mouth, with\r\nglistening eyes, surveying his darlings.\r\n\r\n"Mr M\'Swat, I have come to beg your pardon."\r\n\r\n"That\'s all right, me gu-r-r-r-l. I didn\'t take no notice to anything ye\r\nmight spit out in a rage."\r\n\r\n"But I was not in a rage. I meant every word I said, but I want to\r\napologize for the rude way in which I said it, as I had no right to\r\nspeak so to my elders. And I want to tell you that you need not fear me\r\nrunning away with Peter, even supposing he should honour me with his\r\naffections, as I am engaged to another man."\r\n\r\n"By dad, I\'ll be hanged!" he exclaimed, with nothing but curiosity on\r\nhis wrinkled dried tobacco-leaf-looking face. He expressed no resentment\r\non account of my behaviour to him.\r\n\r\n"Are ye to be married soon? Has he got any prawperty? Who is he? I\r\nsuppose he\'s respectable. Ye\'re very young."\r\n\r\n"Yes; he is renowned for respectability, but I am not going to marry him\r\ntill I am twenty-one. He is poor, but has good prospects. You must\r\npromise me not to tell anyone, as I wish it kept a secret, and only\r\nmention it to you so that you need not be disturbed about Peter."\r\n\r\nHe assured me that he would keep the secret, and I knew I could rely on\r\nhis word. He was greatly perturbed that my intended was poor.\r\n\r\n"Never ye marry a man widout a bit er prawperty, me gu-r-r-r-l. Take my\r\nadvice--the divil\'s in a poor match, no matter how good the man may be.\r\nDon\'t ye be in a hurry; ye\'re personable enough in yer way, and there\'s\r\nas good fish in the seas as ever come out of \'em. Yer very small; I\r\nadmire a good lump of a woman meself--but don\'t ye lose heart. I\'ve\r\nheerd some men say they like little girls, but, as I said, I like a good\r\nlump of a woman meself."\r\n\r\n"And you\'ve got a good lump of a squaw," I thought to myself.\r\n\r\nDo not mistake me. I do not for an instant fancy myself above the\r\nM\'Swats. Quite the reverse; they are much superior to me. Mr M\'Swat was\r\nupright and clean in his morals, and in his little sphere was as\r\nsensible and kind a man as one could wish for. Mrs M\'Swat was faithful\r\nto him, contented and good-natured, and bore uncomplainingly, year after\r\nyear, that most cruelly agonising of human duties--childbirth, and did\r\nmore for her nation and her Maker than I will ever be noble enough to\r\ndo.\r\n\r\nBut I could not help it that their life was warping my very soul. Nature\r\nfashions us all; we have no voice in the matter, and I could not change\r\nmy organisation to one which would find sufficient sustenance in the\r\nmental atmosphere of Barney\'s Gap.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-TWO\r\n\r\nTa-Ta to Barney\'s Gap\r\n\r\n\r\nIt chanced at last, as June gave place to July and July to August, that\r\nI could bear it no longer. I would go away even if I had to walk, and\r\nwhat I would do I did not know or care, my one idea being to leave\r\nBarney\'s Gap far and far behind. One evening I got a lot of letters from\r\nmy little brothers and sisters at home. I fretted over them a good deal,\r\nand put them under my pillow; and as I had not slept for nights, and was\r\nfeeling weak and queer, I laid my head upon them to rest a little before\r\ngoing out to get the tea ready. The next thing I knew was that Mrs\r\nM\'Swat was shaking me vigorously with one hand, holding a flaring candle\r\nin the other, and saying:\r\n\r\n"Lizer, shut the winder quick. She\'s been lyin\' here in the draught till\r\nshe\'s froze, and must have the nightmare, the way she\'s been singin\' out\r\nthat queer, an\' I can\'t git her woke up. What ails ye, child? Are ye\r\nsick?"\r\n\r\nI did not know what ailed me, but learnt subsequently that I laughed and\r\ncried very much, and pleaded hard with grannie and some Harold to save\r\nme, and kept reiterating, "I cannot bear it, I cannot bear it," and\r\naltogether behaved so strangely that Mr M\'Swat became so alarmed that he\r\nsent seventeen miles for the nearest doctor. He came next morning, felt\r\nmy pulse, asked a few questions, and stated that I was suffering from\r\nnervous prostration.\r\n\r\n"Why, the child is completely run down, and in a fair way to contract\r\nbrain fever!" he exclaimed. "What has she been doing? It seems as though\r\nshe had been under some great mental strain. She must have complete rest\r\nand change, plenty of diversion and nourishing food, or her mind will\r\nbecome impaired."\r\n\r\nHe left me a bottle of tonic and Mr and Mrs M\'Swat many fears. Poor\r\nkind-hearted souls, they got in a great state, and understood about as\r\nmuch of the cause of my breakdown as I do of the inside of the moon.\r\nThey ascribed it to the paltry amount of teaching and work I had done.\r\n\r\nMrs M\'Swat killed a fowl and stewed it for my delectation. There was\r\npart of the inside with many feathers to flavour the dish, and having no\r\nappetite, I did not enjoy it, but made a feint of so doing to please the\r\ngood-natured cook.\r\n\r\nThey intended writing at once to give my parents notice when I would be\r\nput on the train. I was pronounced too ill to act as scribe; Lizer was\r\nsuggested, and then Jimmy, but M\'Swat settled the matter thus:\r\n\r\n"Sure, damn it! I\'m the proper one to write on an important business\r\nmatther like this here."\r\n\r\nSo pens, ink, and paper were laid on the dining-room table, and the\r\ngreat proclamation went forth among the youngsters, "Pa is goin\' to\r\nwrite a whole letter all by hisself."\r\n\r\nMy door opened with the dining-room, and from my bed I could see the\r\nproceeding. Mr M\'Swat hitched his trousers well through the saddle-strap\r\nwhich he always wore as a belt, took off his coat and folded it on the\r\nback of a chair, rolled his shirt-sleeves up to his elbows, pulled his\r\nhat well over his eyes, and "shaped up" to the writing material, none of\r\nwhich met with his approval. The ink was "warter", the pens had not\r\nenough "pint", and the paper was "trash"; but on being assured it was\r\nthe good stuff he had purchased especially for himself, he buckled to\r\nthe fray, producing in three hours a half-sheet epistle, which in\r\ngrammar, composition, and spelling quite eclipsed the entries in his\r\ndiary. However, it served its purpose, and my parents wrote back that,\r\ndid I reach Goulburn on a certain day, a neighbour who would be in town\r\nthen would bring me home.\r\n\r\nNow that it was settled that I had no more to teach the dirty children,\r\nout of dirty books, lessons for which they had great disinclination, and\r\nno more to direct Lizer\'s greasy fingers over the yellow keys of that\r\ndemented piano in a vain endeavour to teach her "choones", of which her\r\nmother expected her to learn on an average two daily, it seemed as\r\nthough I had a mountain lifted off me, and I revived magically, got out\r\nof bed and packed my things.\r\n\r\nI was delighted at the prospect of throwing off the leaden shackles of\r\nBarney\'s Gap, but there was a little regret mingled with my relief. The\r\nlittle boys had not been always bold. Did I express a wish for a\r\nparrot-wing or water-worn stone, or such like, after a time I would be\r\ncertain, on issuing from my bedroom, to find that it had been\r\nsurreptitiously laid there, and the little soft-eyed fellows would\r\nsquabble for the privilege of bringing me my post, simply to give me\r\npleasure. Poor little Lizer, and Rose Jane too, copied me in style of\r\ndress and manners in a way that was somewhat ludicrous but more\r\npathetic.\r\n\r\nThey clustered round to say good-bye. I would be sure to write. Oh yes,\r\nof course, and they would write in return and tell me if the bay mare\r\ngot well, and where they would find the yellow turkey-hen\'s nest. When I\r\ngot well I must come back, and I wouldn\'t have as much work to do, but\r\ngo for more rides to keep well, and so on. Mrs M\'Swat very anxiously\r\nimpressed it upon me that I was to explain to my mother that it was not\r\nher (Mrs M\'Swat\'s) fault that I "ailed" from overwork, as I had never\r\ncomplained and always seemed well.\r\n\r\nWith a kindly light on his homely sunburnt face, M\'Swat said, as he put\r\nme on the train:\r\n\r\n"Sure, tell yer father he needn\'t worry over the money. I\'ll never be\r\nhard on him, an\' if ever I could help ye, I\'d be glad."\r\n\r\n"Thank you; you are very good, and have done too much already."\r\n\r\n"Too much! Sure, damn it, wot\'s the good er bein\' alive if we can\'t help\r\neach other sometimes. I don\'t mind how much I help a person if they have\r\na little gratitood, but, damn it, I can\'t abear ingratitood."\r\n\r\n"Good-bye, Mr M\'Swat, and thank you."\r\n\r\n"Good-bye, me gu-r-r-r-l, and never marry that bloke of yours if he\r\ndon\'t git a bit er prawperty, for the divil\'s in a poor match."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-THREE\r\n\r\nBack at Possum Gully\r\n\r\n\r\nThey were expecting me on the frosty evening in September, and the\r\nchildren came bounding and shouting to meet me, when myself and luggage\r\nwere deposited at Possum Gully by a neighbour, as he passed in a great\r\nhurry to reach his own home ere it got too dark. They bustled me to a\r\nglowing fire in no time.\r\n\r\nMy father sat reading, and, greeting me in a very quiet fashion,\r\ncontinued the perusal of his paper. My mother shut her lips tightly,\r\nsaying exultingly, "It seems it was possible for you to find a worse\r\nplace than home"; and that little speech was the thorn on the rose of my\r\nwelcome home. But there was no sting in Gertie\'s greeting, and how\r\nbeautiful she was growing, and so tall! It touched me to see she had\r\nmade an especial dainty for my tea, and had put things on the table\r\nwhich were only used for visitors. The boys and little Aurora chattered\r\nand danced around me all the while. One brought for my inspection some\r\nsoup-plates which had been procured during my absence; another came with\r\na picture-book; and nothing would do them but that I must, despite the\r\ndarkness, straightaway go out and admire a new fowl-house which "Horace\r\nand Stanley built all by theirselves, and no one helped them one single\r\nbit."\r\n\r\nAfter Mrs M\'Swat it was a rest, a relief, a treat, to hear my mother\'s\r\ncultivated voice, and observe her lady-like and refined figure as she\r\nmoved about; and, what a palace the place seemed in comparison to\r\nBarney\'s Gap! simply because it was clean, orderly, and bore traces of\r\nrefinement; for the stamp of indigent circumstances was legibly\r\nimprinted upon it, and many things which had been considered "done for"\r\nwhen thirteen months before I had left home, were still in use.\r\n\r\nI carefully studied my brothers and sisters. They had grown during my\r\nabsence, and were all big for their age, and though some of them not\r\nexactly handsome, yet all pleasant to look upon--I was the only wanting\r\nin physical charms--also they were often discontented, and wished, as\r\nchildren will, for things they could not have; but they were natural,\r\nunderstandable children, not like myself, cursed with a fevered ambition\r\nfor the utterly unattainable.\r\n\r\n    "Oh, were I seated high as my ambition,\r\n     I\'d place this loot on naked necks of monarchs!"\r\n\r\nAt the time of my departure for Caddagat my father had been negotiating\r\nwith beer regarding the sale of his manhood; on returning I found that\r\nhe had completed the bargain, and held a stamped receipt in his\r\nmiserable appearance and demeanour. In the broken-down man, regardless\r\nof manners, one would have failed to recognize Dick Melvyn, "Smart Dick\r\nMelvyn", "Jolly-good-fellow Melvyn" "Thorough Gentleman" and "Manly\r\nMelvyn" of the handsome face and ingratiating manners, one-time holder\r\nof Bruggabrong, Bin Bin East, and Bin Bin West. He never corrected his\r\nfamily nowadays, and his example was most deleterious to them.\r\n\r\nMother gave me a list of her worries in private after tea that night.\r\nShe wished she had never married: not only was her husband a failure,\r\nbut to all appearances her children would be the same. I wasn\'t worth my\r\nsalt or I would have remained at Barney\'s Gap; and there was\r\nHorace--heaven only knew where he would end. God would surely punish him\r\nfor his disrespect to his father. It was impossible to keep things\r\ntogether much longer, etc., etc.\r\n\r\nWhen we went to bed that night Gertie poured all her troubles into my\r\near in a jumbled string. It was terrible to have such a father. She was\r\nashamed of him. He was always going into town, and stayed there till\r\nmother had to go after him, or some of the neighbours were so good as to\r\nbring him home. It took all the money to pay the publican\'s bills, and\r\nGertie was ashamed to be seen abroad in the nice clothes which grannie\r\nsent, as the neighbours said the Melvyns ought to pay up the old man\'s\r\nbills instead of dressing like swells; and she couldn\'t help it, and she\r\nwas sick and tired of trying to keep up respectability in the teeth of\r\nsuch odds.\r\n\r\nI comforted her with the assurance that the only thing was to feel right\r\nwithin ourselves, and let people say whatsoever entertained their poor\r\nlittle minds. And I fell asleep thinking that parents have a duty to\r\nchildren greater than children to parents, and they who do not fulfil\r\ntheir responsibility in this respect are as bad in their morals as a\r\ndebauchee, corrupt the community as much as a thief, and are among the\r\nablest underminers of their nation.\r\n\r\nOn the morrow, the first time we were alone, Horace seized the\r\nopportunity of holding forth on _his_ woes. It was no use, he was choke\r\nfull of Possum Gully: he would stick to it for another year, and then he\r\nwould chuck it, even if he had to go on the wallaby. He wasn\'t going to\r\nbe slaving for ever for the boss to swallow the proceeds, and there was\r\nnothing to be made out of dairying. When it wasn\'t drought it was floods\r\nand caterpillars and grasshoppers.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nAmong my brothers and sisters I quickly revived to a certain extent, and\r\nmother asserted her opinion that I had not been ill at all, but had made\r\nup my mind to torment her; had not taken sufficient exercise, and might\r\nhave had a little derangement of the system but nothing more. It was\r\nproposed that I should return to Barney\'s Gap. I demurred, and was\r\nanathematized as ungrateful and altogether corrupt, that I would not go\r\nback to M\'Swat, who was so good as to lend my father money out of pure\r\nfriendship; but for once in my life I could not be made submit by either\r\ncoercion or persuasion. Grannie offered to take one of us to Caddagat;\r\nmother preferred that Gertie should go. So we sent the pretty girl to\r\ndwell among her kindred in a land of comfort and pleasure.\r\n\r\nI remained at Possum Gully to tread the same old life in its tame narrow\r\npath, with its never-ending dawn-till-daylight round of tasks; with, as\r\nits entertainments, an occasional picnic or funeral or a day in town,\r\nwhen, should it happen to be Sunday, I never fail to patronize one of\r\nthe cathedrals. I love the organ music, and the hush which pervades the\r\nbuilding; and there is much entertainment in various ways if one goes\r\nearly and watches the well-dressed congregation filing in. The costumes\r\nand the women are pretty, and, in his own particular line, the ability\r\nof the verger is something at which to marvel. Regular attendants, of\r\ncourse, pay for and have reserved their seats, but it is in classing the\r\nvisitors that the verger displays his talent. He can cull the commoners\r\nfrom the parvenu aristocrats, and put them in their respective places as\r\nskilfully as an expert horse-dealer can draft his stock at a sale. Then,\r\nwhen the audience is complete, in the middle and front of the edifice\r\nare to be found they of the white hands and fine jewels; and in the\r\ntopmost seat of the synagogue, praying audibly, is one who has made all\r\nhis wealth by devouring widows\' houses; while pushed away to the corners\r\nand wings are they who earn their bread by the sweat of their brow; and\r\nthose who cannot afford good linen are too proud to be seen here at all.\r\n\r\n"The choir sings and the organ rings," the uninteresting prayers are\r\nrattled off ("O come, let us worship, and fall down: and kneel before\r\nthe Lord, our Maker"); a sermon, mostly of the debts of the concern, of\r\nthe customs of the ancients, or of the rites and ceremonies of\r\nup-to-date churchism, is delivered, and the play is done, and as I leave\r\nthe building a great hunger for a little Christianity fills my heart.\r\n\r\nOh that a preacher might arise and expound from the Book of books a\r\nreligion with a God, a religion with a heart in it--a Christian\r\nreligion, which would abolish the cold legend whose centre is\r\nrespectability, and which rears great buildings in which the rich\r\nrecline on silken hassocks while the poor perish in the shadow thereof.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nThrough the hot dry summer, then the heartless winter and the scorching\r\nsummer again which have spent themselves since Gertie\'s departure, I\r\nhave struggled hard to do my duty in that state of life unto which it\r\nhad pleased God to call me, and sometimes I have partially succeeded. I\r\nhave had no books or papers, nothing but peasant surroundings and\r\npeasant tasks, and have encouraged peasant ignorance--ignorance being\r\nthe mainspring of contentment, and contentment the bed-rock of\r\nhappiness; but it is all to no purpose. A note from the other world will\r\nstrike upon the chord of my being, and the spirit which has been dozing\r\nwithin me awakens and fiercely beats at its bars, demanding some nobler\r\nthought, some higher aspiration, some wider action, a more saturnalian\r\npleasure, something more than the peasant life can ever yield. Then I\r\nhold my spirit tight till wild passionate longing sinks down, down to\r\nsickening dumb despair, and had I the privilege extended to job of\r\nold--to curse God and die--I would leap at it eagerly.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-FOUR\r\n\r\nBut Absent Friends are Soon Forgot\r\n\r\n\r\nWe received a great many letters from Gertie for a little while after\r\nshe went up the country, but they grew shorter and farther between as\r\ntime went on.\r\n\r\nIn one of grannie\'s letters there was concerning my sister: "I find\r\nGertie is a much younger girl for her age than Sybylla was, and not\r\nnearly so wild and hard to manage. She is a great comfort to me. Every\r\none remarks upon her good looks."\r\n\r\nFrom one of Gertie\'s letters:\r\n\r\n\r\nUncle Julius came home from Hong Kong and America last week, and brought\r\nsuch a lot of funny presents for every one. He had a lot for you, but he\r\nhas given them to me instead as you are not here. He calls me his pretty\r\nlittle sunbeam, and says I must always live with him.\r\n\r\n\r\nI sighed to myself as I read this. Uncle Jay-Jay had said much the same\r\nto me, and where was I now? My thoughts were ever turning to the people\r\nand old place I love so well, but Gertie\'s letters showed me that I was\r\nutterly forgotten and unmissed.\r\n\r\nGertie left us in October 1897, and it was somewhere about January 1898\r\nthat all the letters from Caddagat were full to overflowing with the\r\nwonderful news of Harold Beecham\'s reinstatement at Five-Bob Downs,\r\nunder the same conditions as he had held sway there in my day.\r\n\r\nFrom grannie\'s letters I learnt that some old sweetheart of Harold\'s\r\nfather had bequeathed untold wealth to this her lost love\'s son. The\r\nwealth was in bonds and stocks principally, and though it would be some\r\ntime ere Harold was actually in possession of it, yet he had no\r\ndifficulty in getting advancements to any amount, and had immediately\r\nrepurchased Five-Bob.\r\n\r\nI had never dreamed of such a possibility. True, I had often said were\r\nHarold a character in fiction instead of real life, some relative would\r\ndie opportunely and set him up in his former position, but, here, this\r\nutterly unanticipated contingency had arisen in a manner which would\r\naffect my own life, and what were my feelings regarding the matter?\r\n\r\nI think I was not fully aware of the extent of my lack of wifely love\r\nfor Harold Beecham, until experiencing the sense of relief which stole\r\nover me on holding in my hand the announcement of his return to the\r\nsmile of fortune.\r\n\r\nHe was rich; he would not need me now; my obligation to him ceased to\r\nexist; I was free. He would no longer wish to be hampered with me. He\r\ncould take his choice of beauty and worth; he might even purchase a\r\nprincess did his ambition point that way.\r\n\r\nOne of Gertie\'s letters ran:\r\n\r\n\r\nThat Mr Beecham you used to tell me so much about has come back to live\r\nat Five-Bob. He has brought his aunts back. Every one went to welcome\r\nthem, and there was a great fuss. Aunt Helen says he (Mr B.) is very\r\nconservative; he has everything just as it used to be. I believe he is\r\nricher than ever. Every one is laughing about his luck. He was here\r\ntwice last week, and has just left this evening. He is very quiet. I\r\ndon\'t know how you thought him so wonderful. I think he is too slow, I\r\nhave great work to talk to him, but he is very kind, and I like him. He\r\nseems to remember you well, and often says you were a game youngster,\r\nand could ride like old Nick himself.\r\n\r\n\r\nI wrote to the owner of Five-Bob desiring to know if what I heard\r\nconcerning his good fortune was correct, and he replied by return post:\r\n\r\n\r\nMy dear little Syb,\r\n\r\nYes, thank goodness it is all true. The old lady left me nearly a\r\nmillion. It seems like a fairy yarn, and I will know how to value it\r\nmore now. I would have written sooner, only you remember our bargain,\r\nand I was just waiting to get things fixed up a little, when I\'m off at\r\ngreat tracks to claim you in the flesh, as there is no need for us to\r\nwait above a month or two now if you are agreeable. I am just run to\r\ndeath. It takes a bit of jigging to get things straight again, but it\'s\r\nsimply too good to believe to be back in the same old beat. I\'ve seen\r\nGertie a good many times, and find your descriptions of her were not at\r\nall overdrawn. I won\'t send any love in this, or there would be a "bust\r\nup" in the post-office, because I\'d be sure to overdo the thing, and I\'d\r\nhave all the officials on to me for damages. Gather up your goods and\r\nchattels, because I\'ll be along in a week or two to take possession of\r\nyou.\r\n\r\n--Yr devoted\r\n\r\nHal.\r\n\r\n\r\nI screwed the letter in two and dropped it into the kitchen-fire.\r\n\r\nI knew Harold meant what he had said. He was a strong-natured man of\r\nfirm determinations, and having made up his mind to marry me would never\r\nfor an instant think of anything else; but I could see what he could not\r\nsee himself--that he had probably tired of me, and was becoming\r\nenamoured of Gertie\'s beauty.\r\n\r\nThe discordance of life smote hard upon me, and the letter I wrote was\r\nnot pleasant. It ran:\r\n\r\n    To H. A. BEECHAM, Esq.,\r\n      Five-Bob Downs Station,\r\n        Gool-Gool, N.S.W.\r\n\r\nSir,\r\n\r\nYour favour duly to hand. I heartily rejoice at your good fortune, and\r\ntrust you may live long and have health to enjoy it. Do not for an\r\ninstant consider yourself under any obligations to me, for you are\r\nperfectly free. Choose some one who will reflect more credit on your\r\ntaste and sense.\r\n\r\n    With all good wishes,\r\n      Faithfully yrs,\r\n        S. PENELOPE MELVYN.\r\n\r\nAs I closed and directed this how far away Harold Beecham seemed! Less\r\nthan two years ago I had been familiar with every curve and expression\r\nof his face, every outline of his great figure, every intonation of his\r\nstrong cultivated voice; but now he seemed as the shadow of a former\r\nage.\r\n\r\nHe wrote in reply: What did I mean? Was it a joke--just a little of my\r\nold tormenting spirit? Would I explain immediately? He couldn\'t get down\r\nto see me for a fortnight at the least. .\r\n\r\nI explained, and very tersely, that I had meant what I said, and in\r\nreturn received a letter as short as my own:\r\n\r\n\r\nDEAR MISS MELVYN,\r\n\r\nI regret your decision, but trust I have sufficient manhood to prevent\r\nme from thrusting myself upon any lady, much less you.\r\n\r\n    Your sincere friend,\r\n      HAROLD AUGUSTUS BEECHAM.\r\n\r\nHe did not demand a reason for my decision, but accepted it\r\nunquestionably. As I read his words he grew near to me, as in the days\r\ngone by.\r\n\r\nI closed my eyes, and before my mental vision there arose an overgrown\r\nold orchard, skirting one of the great stock-routes from Riverina to\r\nMonaro. A glorious day was languidly smiling good night on abundance of\r\nripe and ripening fruit and flowers. The scent of stock and the merry\r\ncry of the tennis-players filled the air. I could feel Harold\'s wild\r\njolting heart-beats, his burning breath on my brow, and his voice husky\r\nwith rage in my ear. As he wrote that letter I could fancy the well-cut\r\nmouth settling into a sullen line, as it had done on my birthday when,\r\nby caressing, I had won it back to its habitual pleasant expression; but\r\non this occasion I would not be there. He would be angry just a little\r\nwhile--a man of his strength and importance could not long hold ill-will\r\ntowards a woman, a girl, a child! as weak and insignificant as I. Then\r\nwhen I should meet him in the years to come, when he would be the\r\nfaithful and loving husband of another woman, he would be a little\r\nembarrassed perhaps; but I would set him at his case, and we would laugh\r\ntogether re what he would term our foolish young days, and he would like\r\nme in a brotherly way. Yes, that was how it would be. The tiny note\r\nblackened in the flames.\r\n\r\nSo much for my romance of love! It had ended in a bottle of smoke, as\r\nall my other dreams of life bid fair to do.\r\n\r\nI think I was not fully aware how near I had been to loving Harold\r\nBeecham until experiencing the sense of loss which stole over me on\r\nholding in my hand the acceptance of his dismissal. It was a something\r\ngone out of my life, which contained so few somethings, that I\r\ncrushingly felt the loss of any one.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nOur greatest heart-treasure is a knowledge that there is in creation an\r\nindividual to whom our existence is necessary--some one who is part of\r\nour life as we are part of theirs, some one in whose life we feel\r\nassured our death would leave a gap for a day or two. And who can be\r\nthis but a husband or wife? Our parents have other children and\r\nthemselves, our brothers and sisters marry and have lives apart, so with\r\nour friends; but one\'s husband would be different. And I had thrown\r\nbehind me this chance; but in the days that followed I knew that I had\r\nacted wisely.\r\n\r\nGertie\'s letters would contain: "Harold Beecham, he makes me call him\r\nHarry, took me to Five-Bob last week, and it was lovely fun."\r\n\r\nAgain it would be: "Harry says I am the prettiest little girl ever was,\r\nCaddagat or anywhere else, and he gave me such a lovely bracelet. I wish\r\nyou could see it."\r\n\r\nOr this:\r\n\r\n\r\nWe all went to church yesterday. Harry rode with me. There is to be a\r\nvery swell ball at Wyambeet next month, and Harry says I am to keep\r\nnearly all my dances for him. Frank Hawden sailed for England last week.\r\nWe have a new jackeroo. He is better-looking than Frank, but I don\'t\r\nlike him as well.\r\n\r\n\r\nGrannie\'s and aunt Helen\'s letters to my mother corroborated these\r\nadmissions. Grannie wrote:\r\n\r\n\r\nHarry Beecham seems to be very much struck with Gertie. I think it would\r\nbe a good thing, as he is immensely rich, and a very steady young fellow\r\ninto the bargain. They say no woman could live with him on account of\r\nhis temper; but he has always been a favourite of mine, and we cannot\r\nexpect a man without some faults.\r\n\r\n\r\nAunt Helen remarked:\r\n\r\n\r\nDon\'t be surprised if you have young Beecham down there presently on an\r\n"asking papa" excursion. He spends a great deal of time here, and has\r\nbeen inquiring the best route to Possum Gully. Do you remember him? I\r\ndon\'t think he was here in your day. He is an estimable and likeable\r\nyoung fellow, and I think will make a good husband apart from his\r\nwealth. He and Gertie present a marked contrast.\r\n\r\n\r\nSometimes on reading this kind of thing I would wax rather bitter. Love,\r\nI said, was not a lasting thing; but knowledge told me that it was for\r\nthose of beauty and winsome ways, and not for me. I was ever to be a\r\nlonely-hearted waif from end to end of the world of love--an alien among\r\nmy own kin.\r\n\r\nBut there were other things to worry me. Horace had left the family\r\nroof. He averred he was "full up of life under the old man\'s rule. It\r\nwas too slow and messed up." His uncle, George Melvyn, his father\'s\r\neldest brother, who had so often and so kindly set us up with cows, had\r\noffered to take him, and his father had consented to let him go. George\r\nMelvyn had a large station outback, a large sheep-shearing machine, and\r\nother improvements. Thence, strong in the hope of sixteen years, Horace\r\nset out on horseback one springless spring morning ere the sun had\r\nrisen, with all his earthly possessions strapped before him. Bravely the\r\nhorse stepped out for its week\'s journey, and bravely its rider sat,\r\nleaving me and the shadeless, wooden sun-baked house on the side of the\r\nhill, with the regretlessness of teens--especially masculine teens. I\r\nwatched him depart until the clacking of his horse\'s hoofs grew faint on\r\nthe stony hillside and his form disappeared amid the she-oak scrub which\r\ncrowned the ridge to the westward. He was gone. Such is life. I sat down\r\nand buried my face in my apron, too miserable even for tears. Here was\r\nanother article I ill could spare wrenched from my poorly and sparsely\r\nfurnished existence.\r\n\r\nTrue, our intercourse had not always been carpeted with rose-leaves. His\r\npitiless scorn of my want of size and beauty had often given me a\r\nsleepless night; but I felt no bitterness against him for this, but\r\nmerely cursed the Potter who had fashioned the clay that was thus\r\ndescribed.\r\n\r\nOn the other hand, he was the only one who had ever stood up and said a\r\nword of extenuation for me in the teeth of a family squall. Father did\r\nnot count; my mother thought me bad from end to end; Gertie, in addition\r\nto the gifts of beauty and lovableness, possessed that of holding with\r\nthe hare and running with the hound; but Horace once had put in a word\r\nfor me that I would never forget. I missed his presence in the house,\r\nhis pounding of the old piano with four dumb notes in the middle, as he\r\nbawled thereto rollicking sea and comic songs; I missed his energetic\r\ndissertations on spurs, whips, and blood-horses, and his spirited\r\nrendering of snatches of Paterson and Gordon, as he came in and out,\r\nbanging doors and gates, teasing the cats and dogs and tormenting the\r\nchildren.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-FIVE\r\n\r\nThe 3rd of December 1898\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was a very hot day. So extreme was the heat that to save the lives of\r\nsome young swallows my father had to put wet bags over the iron roof\r\nabove their nest. A galvanized-iron awning connected our kitchen and\r\nhouse: in this some swallows had built, placing their nest so near the\r\niron that the young ones were baking with the heat until rescued by the\r\nwet bagging. I had a heavy day\'s work before me, and, from my exertions\r\nof the day before, was tired at the beginning. Bush-fires had been\r\nraging in the vicinity during the week, and yesterday had come so close\r\nthat I had been called out to carry buckets of water all the afternoon\r\nin the blazing sun. The fire had been allayed, after making a gap in one\r\nof our boundary fences. Father and the boys had been forced to leave the\r\nharvesting of the miserable pinched wheat while they went to mend it, as\r\nthe small allowance of grass the drought gave us was precious, and had\r\nto be carefully preserved from neighbours\' stock.\r\n\r\nI had baked and cooked, scrubbed floors and whitewashed hearths, scoured\r\ntinware and cutlery, cleaned windows, swept yards, and discharged\r\nnumerous miscellaneous jobs, and half-past two in the afternoon found me\r\nvery dirty and very tired, and with very much more yet to do.\r\n\r\nOne of my half-starved poddy calves was very ill, and I went out to\r\ndoctor it previous to bathing and tidying myself for my finishing\r\nhousehold duties.\r\n\r\nMy mother was busy upon piles and piles of wearying mending, which was\r\none of the most hopeless of the many slaveries of her life. This was\r\nhard work, and my father was slaving away in the sun, and mine was\r\narduous labour, and it was a very hot day, and a drought-smitten and a\r\nlong day, and poddy calves ever have a tendency to make me moralize and\r\nsnarl. This was life, my life and my parents\' life, and the life of\r\nthose around us, and if I was a good girl and honoured my parents I\r\nwould be rewarded with a long stretch of it. Yah!\r\n\r\nThese pagan meditations were interrupted by a footfall slowly\r\napproaching. I did not turn to ascertain who it might be, but trusted it\r\nwas no one of importance, as the poddy and I presented rather a\r\ngrotesque appearance. It was one of the most miserable and sickly of its\r\nmiserable kind, and I was in the working uniform of the Australian\r\npeasantry. My tattered skirt and my odd and bursted boots, laced with\r\ntwine, were spattered with whitewash, for coolness my soiled cotton\r\nblouse hung loose, an exceedingly dilapidated sun-bonnet surmounted my\r\nhead, and a bottle of castor-oil was in my hand.\r\n\r\nI supposed it was one of the neighbours or a tea-agent, and I would send\r\nthem to mother.\r\n\r\nThe footsteps had come to a halt beside me.\r\n\r\n"Could you tell me if--"\r\n\r\nI glanced upwards. Horrors! There stood Harold Beecham, as tall and\r\nbroad as of yore, even more sunburnt than ever, and looking very stylish\r\nin a suit of grey and a soft fashionable dinted-in hat; and it was the\r\nfirst time I had ever seen him in a white shirt and high collar.\r\n\r\nI wished he would explode, or I might sink into the ground, or the calf\r\nwould disappear, or that something might happen.\r\n\r\nOn recognizing me his silence grew profound, but an unmistakable\r\nexpression of pity filled his eyes and stung me to the quick.\r\n\r\nI have a faculty of self-pity, but my pride promptly refuses the\r\nslightest offer of sympathy from another.\r\n\r\nI could feel my heart grow as bitterly cold as my demeanour was icily\r\nstiff, when I stood up and said curtly:\r\n\r\n"This is a great surprise, Mr Beecham."\r\n\r\n"Not an unpleasant one, I hope," he said pleasantly.\r\n\r\n"We will not discuss the matter. Come inside out of the heat."\r\n\r\n"I\'m in no hurry, Syb, and couldn\'t I help you with that poor little\r\ndevil?"\r\n\r\n"I\'m only trying to give it another chance of life."\r\n\r\n"What will you do with it if it lives?"\r\n\r\n"Sell it for half a crown when it\'s a yearling."\r\n\r\n"It would pay better to shoot the poor little beggar now."\r\n\r\n"No doubt it would the owner of Five-Bob, but we have to be more\r\ncareful," I said tartly.\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t mean to offend you."\r\n\r\n"I\'m not offended," I returned, leading the way to the house, imagining\r\nwith a keen pain that Harold Beecham must be wondering how for an\r\ninstant he could have been foolish enough to fancy such an object two\r\nyears ago.\r\n\r\nThank goodness I have never felt any humiliation on account of my\r\nmother, and felt none then, as she rose to greet Harold upon my\r\nintroduction. She was a lady, and looked it, in spite of the piles of\r\ncoarse mending, and the pair of trousers, almost bullet-proof with\r\npatches, out of which she drew her hand, roughened and reddened with\r\nhard labour, in spite of her patched and faded cotton gown, and the\r\ncommonest and most poverty-stricken of peasant surroundings, which\r\nfailed to hide that she had not been always thus.\r\n\r\nLeaving them together, I expeditiously proceeded to relieve the\r\nlivery-stable horse, on which Harold had come, of the valise, saddle,\r\nand bridle with which it was encumbered, and then let it loose in one of\r\nthe grassless paddocks near at hand.\r\n\r\nThen I threw myself on a stool in the kitchen, and felt, to the bone,\r\nthe sting of having ideas above one\'s position.\r\n\r\nIn a few minutes mother came hurrying out.\r\n\r\n"Good gracious, what\'s the matter? I suppose you didn\'t like being\r\ncaught in such a pickle, but don\'t get in the dumps about it. I\'ll get\r\nhim some tea while you clean yourself, and then you\'ll be able to help\r\nme by and by."\r\n\r\nI found my little sister Aurora, and we climbed through the window into\r\nmy bedroom to get tidy. I put a pair of white socks and shoes and a\r\nclean pinafore on the little girl, and combed her golden curls. She was\r\nall mine--slept with me, obeyed me, championed me; while I--well, I\r\nworshipped her.\r\n\r\nThere was a hole in the wall, and through it I could see without being\r\nseen.\r\n\r\nMother was dispensing afternoon tea and talking to Harold. It was\r\npleasant to see that manly figure once again. My spirits rose\r\nconsiderably. After all, if the place was poor, it was very clean, as I\r\nhad scrubbed it all that morning, and when I came to consider the\r\nmatter, I remembered that men weren\'t such terrible creatures, and never\r\nmade one feel the sting of one\'s poverty half as much as women do.\r\n\r\n"Aurora," I said, I want you to go out and tell Mr Beecham something.\r\n\r\nThe little girl assented. I carefully instructed her in what she was to\r\nsay, and dispatched her. She placed herself in front of Harold--a\r\nwide-eyed mite of four, that scarcely reached above his knee--and\r\nclasping her chubby hands behind her, gazed at him fearlessly and\r\nunwinkingly.\r\n\r\n"Aurora, you mustn\'t stand staring like that," said mother.\r\n\r\n"Yes, I must," she replied confidently.\r\n\r\n"Well, and what\'s your name?" said Harold laughingly.\r\n\r\n"Aurora and Roy. I belong to Sybyller, and got to tell you somesing."\r\n\r\n"Have you? Let\'s hear it."\r\n\r\n"Sybyller says you\'s Mr Beecher; when you\'re done tea, you\'d like me if\r\nI would to \'scort you to farver and the boys, and \'duce you."\r\n\r\nMother laughed. "That\'s some of Sybylla\'s nonsense. She considers Rory\r\nher especial property, and delights to make the child attempt long\r\nwords. Perhaps you would care to take a stroll to where they are at\r\nwork, by and by."\r\n\r\nHarold said he would go at once, and accepting Rory\'s escort, and with a\r\nfew directions from mother, they presently set out--she importantly\r\ntrudging beneath a big white sun-bonnet, and he looking down at her in\r\namusement. Presently he tossed her high above his head, and depositing\r\nher upon his shoulder, held one sturdy brown leg in his browner hand,\r\nwhile she held on by his hair.\r\n\r\n"My first impressions are very much in his favour," said mother, when\r\nthey had got out of hearing. "But fancy Gertie the wife of that great\r\nman!"\r\n\r\n"She is four inches taller than I am," I snapped. "And if he was as big\r\nas a gum-tree, he would be a man all the same, and just as soft on a\r\npretty face as all the rest of them."\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI bathed, dressed, arranged my hair, got something ready for tea, and\r\nprepared a room for our visitor. For this I collected from all parts of\r\nthe house--a mat from one room, a toilet-set from another, and so\r\non--till I had quite an elaborately furnished chamber ready for my\r\none-time lover.\r\n\r\nThey returned at dusk, Rory again seated on Harold\'s shoulder, and two\r\nof the little boys clinging around him.\r\n\r\nAs I conducted him to his room I was in a different humour from that of\r\nthe sweep-like object who had met him during the afternoon. I laughed to\r\nmyself, for, as on a former occasion during our acquaintance, I felt I\r\nwas master of the situation.\r\n\r\n"I say, Syb, don\'t treat a fellow as though he was altogether a\r\nstranger," he said diffidently, leaning against the door-post.\r\n\r\nOur hands met in a cordial grasp as I said, "I\'m awfully glad to see\r\nyou, Hal; but, but----"\r\n\r\n"But what?"\r\n\r\n"I didn\'t feel over delighted to be caught in such a stew this\r\nafternoon."\r\n\r\n"Nonsense! It only reminded me of the first time we met," he said with a\r\ntwinkle in his eye. "That\'s always the way with you girls. You can\'t be\r\ncivil to a man unless you\'re dressed up fit to stun him, as though you\r\ncouldn\'t make fool enough of him without the aid of clothes at all."\r\n\r\n"You\'d better shut up," I said over my shoulder as I departed, "or you\r\nwill be saying something better left unsaid, like at our first meeting.\r\nDo you remember?"\r\n\r\n"Do I not? Great Scot, it\'s just like old times to have you giving me\r\nimpudence over your shoulder like that!" he replied merrily.\r\n\r\n"Like, yet unlike," I retorted with a sigh.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-SIX\r\n\r\nOnce Upon a Time, when the days were long and hot\r\n\r\n\r\nNext day was Sunday--a blazing one it was too. I proposed that in the\r\nafternoon some of us should go to church. Father sat upon the idea as a\r\nmad one. Walk two miles in such heat for nothing! as walk we would be\r\ncompelled to do, horseflesh being too precious in such a drought to\r\nfritter it away in idle jaunts. Surprising to say, however, Harold, who\r\nnever walked anywhere when he could get any sort of a horse, uttered a\r\nwish to go. Accordingly, when the midday dinner was over, he, Stanley,\r\nand I set out. Going to church was quite the event of the week to the\r\nresidents around Possum Gully. It was a small Dissenting chapel, where a\r\nlayman ungrammatically held forth at 3 p.m. every Sunday; but the\r\ncongregation was composed of all denominations, who attended more for\r\nthe sitting about on logs outside, and yarning about the price of\r\nbutter, the continuance of the drought, and the latest gossip, before\r\nand after the service, than for the service itself.\r\n\r\nI knew the appearance of Harold Beecham, would make quite a miniature\r\nsensation, and form food for no end of conjecture and chatter. In any\r\ncompany he was a distinguished-looking man, and particularly so among\r\nthese hard-worked farmer-selectors, on whose careworn features the cruel\r\neffects of the drought were leaving additional lines of worry. I felt\r\nproud of my quondam sweetheart. There was an unconscious air of physical\r\nlordliness about him, and he looked such a swell--not the black-clothed,\r\nclean-shaved, great display of white collar-and-cuffs swell appertaining\r\nto the office and city street, but of the easy sunburnt squatter type of\r\nswelldom, redolent of the sun, the saddle, the wide open country--a man\r\nwho is a man, utterly free from the least suspicion of effeminacy, and\r\ncapable of earning his bread by the sweat of his brow--with an arm ready\r\nand willing to save in an accident.\r\n\r\nAll eyes were turned on us as we approached, and I knew that the\r\nattentions he paid me out of simple courtesy--tying my shoe, carrying my\r\nbook, holding my parasol--would be put down as those of a lover.\r\n\r\nI introduced him to a group of men who were sitting on a log, under the\r\nshade of a stringybark, and leaving him to converse with them, made my\r\nway to where the women sat beneath a gum-tree. The children made a third\r\ngroup at some distance. We always divided ourselves thus.
