A young fellow\r\nhad to be very far gone ere he was willing to run the gauntlet of all\r\nthe chaff levelled at him had he the courage to single out a girl and\r\ntalk to her.\r\n\r\nI greeted all the girls and women, beginning at the great-grandmother of\r\nthe community, who illustrated to perfection the grim sarcasm of the\r\nfifth commandment. She had worked hard from morning till night, until\r\ntoo old to do so longer, and now hung around with aching weariness\r\nwaiting for the grave. She generally poured into my ears a wail about\r\nher "rheumatisms", and "How long it do be waiting for the Lord"; but\r\ntoday she was too curious about Harold to think of herself.\r\n\r\n"Sure, Sybyller, who\'s that? Is he yer sweetheart? Sure he\'s as fine a\r\nman as iver I clapped me eyes on."\r\n\r\nI proceeded to give his pedigree, but was interrupted by the arrival of\r\nthe preacher, and we all went into the weatherboard iron-roofed house of\r\nprayer.\r\n\r\nAfter service, one of the girls came up to me and whispered, "that is\r\nyour sweetheart, isn\'t it, Sybyller? He was looking at you all the time\r\nin church."\r\n\r\n"Oh dear, no! I\'ll introduce him to you."\r\n\r\nI did so, and watched him as they made remarks about the heat and\r\ndrought. There was nothing of the cad or snob about him, and his short\r\nseason of adversity had rubbed all the little crudities off his\r\ncharacter, leaving him a man that the majority of both sexes would\r\nadmire: women for his bigness, his gentleness, his fine brown\r\nmoustache--and for his wealth; men, because he was a manly fellow.\r\n\r\nI know he had walked to church on purpose to get a chance of speaking to\r\nme about Gertie, before approaching her parents on the matter; but\r\nStanley accompanied us, and, boy-like, never relaxed in vigilance for an\r\ninstant, so there was no opportunity for anything but matter-of-fact\r\nremarks. The heat was intense. We wiped the perspiration and flies from\r\nour face frequently, and disturbed millions of grasshoppers as we\r\nwalked. They had devoured all the fruit in the orchards about, and had\r\neven destroyed many of the trees by eating the bark, and now they were\r\nstripping the briers of foliage. In one orchard we passed, the apricot,\r\nplum, and peach-stones hung naked on their leafless trees as evidence of\r\ntheir ravages. It was too hot to indulge in any but the most desultory\r\nconversation. We dawdled along. A tiger-snake crossed our path. Harold\r\nprocured a stick and killed it, and Stanley hung it on the top wire of a\r\nfence which was near at hand. After this we discussed snakes for a few\r\nyards.\r\n\r\nA blue sea-breeze, redolent of the bush-fires which were raging at\r\nTocumwal and Bombala, came rushing and roaring over the ranges from the\r\ncast, and enshrouded the scene in its heavy fog-like folds. The sun was\r\nobscured, and the temperature suddenly took such a great drop that I\r\nfelt chilled in my flimsy clothing, and I noticed Harold draw his coat\r\ntogether.\r\n\r\nStanley had to go after the cows, which were little better than walking\r\nhides, yet were yarded morning and evening to yield a dribble of milk.\r\nHe left us among some sallie-trees, in a secluded nook, walled in by\r\nbriers, and went across the paddock to roundup the cows. Harold and I\r\ncame to a halt by tacit consent.\r\n\r\n"Syb, I want to speak to you," he said earnestly, and then came to a\r\ndead stop.\r\n\r\n"Very well; \'tear into it,\' as Horace would say; but if it is anything\r\nfrightful, break it gently," I said flippantly.\r\n\r\n"Surely, Syb, you can guess what it is I have to say."\r\n\r\nYes, I could guess, I knew what he was going to say, and the knowledge\r\nleft a dull bitterness at my heart. I knew he was going to tell me that\r\nI had been right and he wrong--that he had found some one he loved\r\nbetter than me, and that some one being my sister, he felt I needed some\r\nexplanation before he could go in and win; and though I had refused him\r\nfor want of love, yet it gave me pain when the moment arrived that the\r\nonly man who had ever pretended to love me was going to say he had been\r\nmistaken, and preferred my sister.\r\n\r\nThere was silence save for the whirr of the countless grasshoppers in\r\nthe brier bushes. I knew he was expecting me to help him out, but I felt\r\ndoggedly savage and wouldn\'t. I looked up at him. He was a tall grand\r\nman, and honest and true and rich. He loved my sister; she would marry\r\nhim, and they would be happy. I thought bitterly that God was good to\r\none and cruel to another--not that I wanted this man, but why was I so\r\ndifferent from other girls?\r\n\r\nBut then I thought of Gertie, so pretty, so girlish, so understandable,\r\nso full of innocent winning coquetry. I softened. Could any one help\r\npreferring her to me, who was strange, weird, and perverse--too\r\noutspoken to be engaging, devoid of beauty and endearing little ways? It\r\nwas my own misfortune and nobody\'s fault that my singular individuality\r\nexcluded me from the ordinary run of youthful joyous-heartednesses, and\r\nwhy should I be nasty to these young people?\r\n\r\nI was no heroine, only a common little bush-girl, so had to make the\r\nbest of the situation without any fooling. I raised my eyes from the\r\nscanty baked wisps of grass at my feet, placed my hand on Hal\'s arm, and\r\ntiptoeing so as to bring my five-foot stature more on a level with his,\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n"Yes, Hal, I know what you want to say. Say it all. I won\'t be nasty."\r\n\r\n"Well, you see you are so jolly touchy, and have snubbed me so often,\r\nthat I don\'t know how to begin; and if you know what I\'m going to say,\r\nwon\'t you give me an answer without hearing it?"\r\n\r\n"Yes, Hal; but you\'d better say it, as I don\'t know what conditions--"\r\n\r\n"Conditions!"--catching me up eagerly at the word. "If it is only\r\nconditions that are stopping you, you can make your own conditions if\r\nyou will marry me."\r\n\r\n"Marry you, Harold! What do you mean? Do you know what you are saying?"\r\nI exclaimed.\r\n\r\n"There!" he replied: "I knew you would take it as an insult. I believe\r\nyou are the proudest girl in the world. I know you are too clever for\r\nme; but I love you, and could give you everything you fancied."\r\n\r\n"Hal, dear, let me explain. I\'m not insulted, only surprised. I thought\r\nyou were going to tell me that you loved Gertie, and would ask me not to\r\nmake things unpleasant by telling her of the foolish little bit of\r\nflirtation there had been between us."\r\n\r\n"Marry Gertie! Why, she\'s only a child! A mere baby, in fact. Marry\r\nGertie! I never thought of her in that light; and did you think I was\r\nthat sort of a fellow, Syb?" he asked reproachfully.\r\n\r\n"No, Hal," I promptly made answer. "I did not think you were that sort\r\nof fellow; but I thought that was the only sort of fellow there was."\r\n\r\n"Good heavens, Syb! Did you really mean those queer little letters you\r\nwrote me last February? I never for an instant looked upon them as\r\nanything but a little bit of playful contrariness. And have you\r\nforgotten me? Did you not mean your promise of two years ago, that you\r\nspeak of what passed between us as a paltry bit of flirtation? Is that\r\nall you thought it?"\r\n\r\n"No, I did not consider it flirtation; but that is what I thought you\r\nwould term it when announcing your affection for Gertie."\r\n\r\n"Gertie! Pretty little Gertie! I never looked upon the child as anything\r\nbut your sister, consequently mine also. She\'s a child."\r\n\r\n"Child! She is eighteen. More than a year older than I was when you\r\nfirst introduced the subject of matrimony to me, and she is very\r\nbeautiful, and twenty times as good and lovable as I could ever be even\r\nin my best moments."\r\n\r\n"Yes, I know you are young in years, but there is nothing of the child\r\nin you. As for beauty, it is nothing. If beauty was all a man required,\r\nhe could, if rich, have a harem full of it any day. I want some one to\r\nbe true."\r\n\r\n    "The world is filled with folly and sin,\r\n       And love must cling where it can, I say;\r\n     For beauty is easy enough to win,\r\n       But one isn\'t loved every day,"\r\n\r\nI quoted from Owen Meredith.\r\n\r\n"Yes," he said, "that is why I want you. Just think a moment; don\'t say\r\nno. You are not vexed with me--are you, Syb?"\r\n\r\n"Vexed, Hal! I am scarcely inhuman enough to be angry on account of\r\nbeing loved."\r\n\r\nAh, why did I not love him as I have it in me to love! Why did he look\r\nso exasperatingly humble? I was weak, oh, so pitifully weak! I wanted a\r\nman who would be masterful and strong, who would help me over the rough\r\nspots of life--one who had done hard grinding in the mill of fate--one\r\nwho had suffered, who had understood. No; I could never marry Harold\r\nBeecham.\r\n\r\n"Well, Syb, little chum, what do you say?"\r\n\r\n"Say!"--and the words fell from me bitterly--"I say, leave me; go and\r\nmarry the sort of woman you ought to marry. The sort that all men like.\r\nA good conventional woman, who will do the things she should at the\r\nproper time. Leave me alone."\r\n\r\nHe was painfully agitated. A look of pain crossed his face.\r\n\r\n"Don\'t say that, Syb, because I was a beastly cad once: I\'ve had all\r\nthat knocked out of me."\r\n\r\n"I am the cad," I replied. "What I said was nasty and unwomanly, and I\r\nwish I had left it unsaid. I am not good enough to be your wife, Hal, or\r\nthat of any man. Oh, Hal, I have never deceived you! There are scores of\r\ngood noble women in the world who would wed you for the asking--marry\r\none of them."\r\n\r\n"But, Syb, I want you. You are the best and truest girl in the world."\r\n\r\n"Och! Sure, the blarney-stone is getting a good rub now," I said\r\nplayfully.\r\n\r\nAnnoyance and amusement struggled for mastery in his expression as he\r\nreplied:\r\n\r\n"You\'re the queerest girl in the world. One minute you snub a person,\r\nthe next you are the jolliest girl going, and then you get as grave and\r\nearnest as a fellow\'s mother would be."\r\n\r\n"Yes, I am queer. If you had any sense, you\'d have nothing to do with\r\nme. I\'m more queer, too. I am given to something which a man never\r\npardons in a woman. You will draw away as though I were a snake when you\r\nhear."\r\n\r\n"What is it?"\r\n\r\n"I am given to writing stories, and literary people predict I will yet\r\nbe an authoress."\r\n\r\nHe laughed--his soft, rich laugh.\r\n\r\n"That\'s just into my hand. I\'d rather work all day than write the\r\nshortest letter; so if you will give me a hand occasionally, you can\r\nwrite as many yarns as you like. I\'ll give you a study, and send for a\r\ntruck-load of writing-gear at once, if you like. Is that the only horror\r\nyou had to tell me?"\r\n\r\nI bowed my head.\r\n\r\n"Well, I can have you now," he said gently, folding me softly in his\r\narms with such tender reverence that I cried out in pain, "Oh, Hal,\r\ndon\'t, don\'t!" and struggled free. I was ashamed, knowing I was not\r\nworthy of this.\r\n\r\nHe flushed a dusky red.\r\n\r\n"Am I so hateful to you that you cannot bear my touch?" he asked half\r\nwistfully, half angrily.\r\n\r\n"Oh no; it isn\'t that. I\'m really very fond of you, if you\'d only\r\nunderstand," I said half to myself.\r\n\r\n"Understand! If you care for me, that is all I want to understand. I\r\nlove you, and have plenty of money. There is nothing to keep us apart.\r\nNow that I know you care for me, I _will_ have you, in spite of the\r\ndevil."\r\n\r\n"There will be a great tussle between you," I said mischievously,\r\nlaughing at him. "Old Nick has a great hold on me, and I\'m sure he will\r\ndispute your right."\r\n\r\nAt any time Harold\'s sense of humour was not at all in accordance with\r\nhis size, and he failed to see how my remark applied now.\r\n\r\nHe gripped my hands in a passion of pleading, as two years previously he\r\nhad seized me in jealous rage. He drew me to him. His eyes were dark and\r\nfull of entreaty; his voice was husky.\r\n\r\n"Syb, poor little Syb, I will be good to you! You can have what you\r\nlike. You don\'t know what you mean when you say no."\r\n\r\nNo; I would not yield. He offered me everything--but control. He was a\r\nman who meant all he said. His were no idle promises on the spur of the\r\nmoment. But no, no, no, no, he was not for me. My love must know, must\r\nhave suffered, must understand.\r\n\r\n"Syb, you do not answer. May I call you mine? You must, you must, you\r\nmust!"\r\n\r\nHis hot breath was upon my cheek. The pleasant, open, manly countenance\r\nwas very near-perilously near. The intoxication of his love was\r\noverpowering me. I had no hesitation about trusting him. He was not\r\ndistasteful to me in any way. What was the good of waiting for that\r\nother--the man who had suffered, who knew, who understood? I might never\r\nfind him; and, if I did, ninety-nine chances to one he would not care\r\nfor me.\r\n\r\n"Syb, Syb, can\'t you love me just a little?"\r\n\r\nThere was a winning charm in his manner. Nature had endowed him\r\nliberally with virile fascination. My hard uncongenial life had rendered\r\nme weak. He was drawing me to him; he was irresistible. Yes; I would be\r\nhis wife. I grew dizzy, and turned my head sharply backwards and took a\r\nlong gasping breath, another and another, of that fresh cool air\r\nsuggestive of the grand old sea and creak of cordage and bustle and\r\nstrife of life. My old spirit revived, and my momentary weakness fled.\r\nThere was another to think of than myself, and that was Harold. Under a\r\nmaster-hand I would be harmless; but to this man I would be as a\r\ntwo-edged sword in the hand of a novice--gashing his fingers at every\r\nturn, and eventually stabbing his honest heart.\r\n\r\nIt was impossible to make him see my refusal was for his good. He was as\r\na favourite child pleading for a dangerous toy. I desired to gratify\r\nhim, but the awful responsibility of the after-effects loomed up and\r\ndeterred me.\r\n\r\n"Hal, it can never be."\r\n\r\nHe dropped my hands and drew himself up.\r\n\r\n"I will not take your No till the morning. Why do you refuse me? Is it\r\nmy temper? You need not be afraid of that. I don\'t think I\'d hurt you;\r\nand I don\'t drink, or smoke, or swear very much; and I\'ve never\r\ndestroyed a woman\'s name. I would not stoop to press you against your\r\nwill if you were like the ordinary run of women; but you are such a\r\nqueer little party, that I\'m afraid you might be boggling at some funny\r\nlittle point that could easily be wiped out."\r\n\r\n"Yes; it is only a little point. But if you wipe it out you will knock\r\nthe end out of the whole thing--for the point is myself. I would not\r\nsuit you. It would not be wise for you to marry me."\r\n\r\n"But I\'m the only person concerned. If you are not afraid for yourself,\r\nI am quite satisfied."\r\n\r\nWe faced about and walked homewards in unbroken silence--too perturbed\r\nto fall into our usual custom of chewing bush-leaves as we went.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI thought much that night when all the house was abed. It was tempting.\r\nHarold would be good to me, and would lift me from this life of poverty\r\nwhich I hated, to one of ease. Should I elect to remain where I was,\r\ntill the grave there was nothing before me but the life I was leading\r\nnow: my only chance of getting above it was by marriage, and Harold\r\nBeecham\'s offer was the one chance of a lifetime. Perhaps he could\r\nmanage me well enough. Yes; I had better marry him.\r\n\r\nAnd I believe in marriage--that is, I think it the most sensible and\r\nrespectable arrangement for the replenishing of a nation which has yet\r\nbeen suggested. But marriage is a solemn issue of life. I was as suited\r\nfor matrimony as any of the sex, but only with an exceptional\r\nhelpmeet--and Harold was not he. My latent womanliness arose and pointed\r\nthis out so plainly that I seized my pen and wrote:\r\n\r\n\r\nDEAR HAROLD,\r\n\r\nI will not get a chance of speaking to you in the morning, so write.\r\nNever mention marriage to me again. I have firmly made up my mind--it\r\nmust be No. It will always be a comfort to me in the years to come to\r\nknow that I was loved once, if only for a few hours. It is not that I do\r\nnot care for you, as I like you better than any man I have ever seen;\r\nbut I do not mean ever to marry. When you lost your fortune I was\r\nwilling to accede to your request, as I thought you wanted me; but now\r\nthat you are rich again you will not need me. I am not good enough to be\r\nyour wife, for you are a good man; and better, because you do not know\r\nyou are good. You may feel uncomfortable or lonely for a little while,\r\nbecause, when you make up your mind, you are not easily thwarted; but\r\nyou will find that your fancy for me will soon pass. It is only a fancy,\r\nHal. Take a look in the glass, and you will see reflected there the\r\nfigure of a stalwart man who is purely virile, possessing not the\r\nslightest attribute of the weaker sex, therefore your love is merely a\r\npassing flame. I do not impute fickleness to you, but merely point out a\r\nmasculine characteristic, and that you are a man, and only a man, pure\r\nand unadulterated. Look around, and from the numbers of good women to be\r\nfound on every side choose one who will make you a fitter helpmeet, a\r\nmore conventional comrade, than I could ever do. I thank you for the\r\ninestimable honour you have conferred upon me; but keep it till you find\r\nsome one worthy of it, and by and by you will be glad that I have set\r\nyou free.\r\n\r\nGood-bye, Hal!\r\n\r\n    Your sincere and affec. friend,\r\n      SYBYLLA PENELOPE MELVYN.\r\n\r\nThen I crept into bed beside my little sister, and though the air inside\r\nhad not cooled, and the room was warm, I shivered so that I clasped the\r\nchubby, golden-haired little sleeper in my arms that I might feel\r\nsomething living and real and warm.\r\n\r\n"Oh, Rory, Rory!" I whispered, raining upon her lonely-hearted tears.\r\n"In all the world is there never a comrade strong and true to teach me\r\nthe meaning of this hollow, grim little tragedy--life? Will it always be\r\nthis ghastly aloneness? Why am I not good and pretty and simple like\r\nother girls? Oh, Rory, Rory, why was I ever born? I am of no use or\r\npleasure to any one in all the world!"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-SEVEN\r\n\r\nHe that despiseth little things, shall fall little by little\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe morning came, breakfast, next Harold\'s departure. I shook my head\r\nand slipped the note into his hand as we parted. He rode slowly down the\r\nroad. I sat on the step of the garden gate, buried my face in my hands,\r\nand reviewed the situation. I could see my life, stretching out ahead of\r\nme, barren and monotonous as the thirsty track along which Harold was\r\ndisappearing. Today it was washing, ironing tomorrow, next day baking,\r\nafter that scrubbing--thus on and on. We would occasionally see a\r\nneighbour or a tea-agent, a tramp or an Assyrian hawker. By hard\r\nslogging against flood, fire, drought, pests, stock diseases, and the\r\nsweating occasioned by importation, we could manage to keep bread in our\r\nmouths. By training and education I was fitted for nought but what I\r\nwas, or a general slavey, which was many degrees worse. I could take my\r\nchoice. Life was too much for me. What was the end of it, what its\r\nmeaning, aim, hope, or use?\r\n\r\nIn comparison to millions I knew that I had received more than a fair\r\nshare of the goods of life; but knowing another has leprosy makes our\r\ncancer none the easier to bear.\r\n\r\nMy mother\'s voice, sharp and cross, roused me. "Sybylla, you lazy\r\nunprincipled girl, to sit scheming there while your poor old mother is\r\nat the wash-tub. You sit idling there, and then by and by you\'ll be\r\ngroaning about this terrible life in which there\'s time for nothing but\r\nwork."\r\n\r\nHow she fussed and bothered over the clothes was a marvel to me. My\r\nframe of mind was such that it seemed it would not signify if all our\r\nclothes went to the dogs, and the clothes of our neighbours, and the\r\nclothes of the whole world, and the world itself for the matter of that.\r\n\r\n"Sybylla, you are a dirty careless washer. You\'ve put Stanley\'s trousers\r\nin the boil and the colour is coming out of them, and your father\'s best\r\nwhite handkerchief should have been with the first lot, and here it is\r\nnow."\r\n\r\nPoor mother got crosser as she grew weary with the fierce heat and\r\narduous toil, and as I in my abstraction continued to make mistakes, but\r\nthe last straw was the breaking of an old cup which I accidentally\r\npushed off the table.\r\n\r\nI got it hot. Had I committed an act of premeditated villainy I could\r\nnot have received more lecturing. I deserved it--I was careless, cups\r\nwere scarce with us, and we could not afford more; but what I rail\r\nagainst is the grindingly uneventful narrowness of the life in which the\r\nunintentional breaking of a common cup is good for a long scolding.\r\n\r\nAh, my mother! In my life of nineteen years I can look back and see a\r\ntime when she was all gentleness and refinement, but the polish has been\r\nworn off it by years and years of scrubbing and scratching, and washing\r\nand patching, and poverty and husbandly neglect, and the bearing of\r\nburdens too heavy for delicate shoulders. Would that we were more\r\ncompanionable, it would make many an oasis in the desert of our lives.\r\nOh that I could take an all-absorbing interest in patterns and recipes,\r\nbargains and orthodoxy! Oh that you could understand my desire to feel\r\nthe rolling billows of the ocean beneath, to hear the pealing of a great\r\norgan through dimly lit arches, or the sob and wail of a violin in a\r\nbrilliant crowded hall, to be swept on by the human stream.\r\n\r\nAh, thou cruel fiend--Ambition! Desire!\r\n\r\n    "Soul of the leaping flame,\r\n       Heart of the scarlet fire,\r\n     Spirit that hath for name\r\n       Only the name--Desire!"\r\n\r\nTo hot young hearts beating passionately in strong breasts, the sweetest\r\nthing is motion.\r\n\r\nNo, that part of me went beyond my mother\'s understanding. On the other\r\nhand, there was a part of my mother--her brave cheerfulness, her trust\r\nin God, her heroic struggle to keep the home together--which went\r\nsoaring on beyond my understanding, leaving me a coward weakling,\r\ngrovelling in the dust.\r\n\r\nWould that hot dreary day never close? What advantage when it did? The\r\nnext and the next and many weeks of others just the same were following\r\nhard after.\r\n\r\nIf the souls of lives were voiced in music, there are some that none but\r\na great organ could express, others the clash of a full orchestra, a few\r\nto which nought but the refined and exquisite sadness of a violin could\r\ndo justice. Many might be likened unto common pianos, jangling and out\r\nof tune, and some to the feeble piping of a penny whistle, and mine\r\ncould be told with a couple of nails in a rusty tin-pot.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nWhy do I write? For what does any one write? Shall I get a hearing? If\r\nso--what then?\r\n\r\nI have voiced the things around me, the small-minded thoughts, the\r\nsodden round of grinding tasks--a monotonous, purposeless, needless\r\nexistence. But patience, O heart, surely I can make a purpose! For the\r\npresent, of my family I am the most suited to wait about common\r\npublic-houses to look after my father when he is inebriated. It breaks\r\nmy mother\'s heart to do it; it is dangerous for my brothers; imagine\r\nGertie in such a position! But me it does not injure, I have the faculty\r\nfor doing that sort of thing without coming to harm, and if it makes me\r\nmore bitter and godless, well, what matter?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next letter I received from Gertie contained:\r\n\r\n\r\nI suppose you were glad to see Harry. He did not tell me he was going,\r\nor I would have sent some things by him. I thought he would be able to\r\ntell me lots about you that I was dying to hear, but he never said a\r\nword, only that you were all well. He went travelling some weeks ago. I\r\nmissed him at first because he used to be so kind to me; but now I\r\ndon\'t, because Mr Creyton, whom Harry left to manage Five-Bob, comes\r\njust as often as Harry used to, and is lots funnier. He brings me\r\nsomething nice every time. Uncle Jay-Jay teases me about him.\r\n\r\n\r\nHappy butterfly-natured Gertie! I envied her. With Gertie\'s letter came\r\nalso one from grannie, with further mention of Harold Beecham.\r\n\r\n\r\nWe don\'t know what to make of Harold Beecham. He was always such a\r\nsteady fellow, and hated to go away from home even for a short time, but\r\nnow he has taken an idea to rush away to America, and is not coming home\r\ntill he has gone over the world. He is not going to see anything,\r\nbecause by cablegrams his aunts got he is one place today and hundreds\r\nof miles away tomorrow. It is some craze he has suddenly taken. I was\r\nasking Augusta if there was ever any lunacy in the family, and she says\r\nnot that she knows of. It was a very unwise act to leave full management\r\nto Creyton and Benson in the face of such a drought. One warning and\r\nmarvellous escape such as he has had ought to be enough for a man with\r\nany sense. I told him he\'d be poor again if he didn\'t take care, but he\r\nsaid he didn\'t mind if all his property was blown into atoms, as it had\r\ndone him more harm than good, whatever he means by talking that way.\r\nInsanity is the only reason I can see for his conduct. I thought he had\r\nhis eye on Gertie, but I questioned her, and it appears he has never\r\nsaid anything to her. I wonder what was his motive for going to Possum\r\nGully that time?\r\n\r\n\r\nTravel was indeed an unexpected development on the part of Harold\r\nBeecham. He had such a marked aversion to anything of that sort, and\r\nnever went even to Sydney or Melbourne for more than a few days at a\r\nstretch, and that on business or at a time of stock shows.\r\n\r\nThere were many conjectures re the motive of his visit to Possum Gully,\r\nbut I held my peace.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THIRTY-EIGHT\r\n\r\nA Tale that is told and a Day that is done\r\n\r\n    "There are others toiling and straining\r\n       \'Neath burdens graver than mine;\r\n     They are weary, yet uncomplaining,--\r\n       I know it, yet I repine:\r\n     I know it, how time will ravage,\r\n       How time will level, and yet\r\n     I long with a longing savage,\r\n       I regret with a fierce regret."\r\n\r\n    A. L. GORDON.\r\n    \'Possum Gully, 25th March, 1899\r\n\r\nChristmas, only distinguished from the fifty-two slow Sundays of the\r\nyear by plum-pudding, roast turkey, and a few bottles of home-made beer,\r\nhas been once more; New Year, ushered in with sweet-scented midsummer\r\nwattle and bloom of gum- and box-tree has gone; February has followed,\r\nMarch is doing likewise, and my life is still the same.\r\n\r\nWhat the future holds I know not, and am tonight so Weary that I do not\r\ncare.\r\n\r\n      "Time rules us all. And life, indeed, is not\r\n    The thing we planned it out, ere hope was dead;\r\n      And then, we women cannot choose our lot."\r\n\r\nTime is thorough in his work, and as that arch-cheat, Hope, gradually\r\nbecomes a phantom of the past, the neck will grow inured to its yoke.\r\n\r\nTonight is one of the times when the littleness--the abject\r\nlittleness--of all things in life comes home to me.\r\n\r\nAfter all, what is there in vain ambition? King or slave, we all must\r\ndie, and when death knocks at our door, will it matter whether our life\r\nhas been great or small, fast or slow, so long as it has been true--true\r\nwith the truth that will bring rest to the soul?\r\n\r\n    "But the toughest lives are brittle,\r\n       And the bravest and the best\r\n     Lightly fall--it matters little;\r\n       Now I only long for rest."\r\n\r\nTo weary hearts throbbing slowly in hopeless breasts the sweetest thing\r\nis rest.\r\n\r\nAnd my heart is weary. Oh, how it aches tonight--not with the ache of a\r\nyoung heart passionately crying out for battle, but with the slow dead\r\nache of an old heart returning vanquished and defeated!\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\nEnough of pessimistic snarling and grumbling! Enough! Enough! Now for a\r\nlilt of another theme:\r\n\r\nI am proud that I am an Australian, a daughter of the Southern Cross, a\r\nchild of the mighty bush. I am thankful I am a peasant, a part of the\r\nbone and muscle of my nation, and earn my bread by the sweat of my brow,\r\nas man was meant to do. I rejoice I was not born a parasite, one of the\r\nblood-suckers who loll on velvet and satin, crushed from the proceeds of\r\nhuman sweat and blood and souls.\r\n\r\nAh, my sunburnt brothers!--sons of toil and of Australia! I love and\r\nrespect you well, for you are brave and good and true. I have seen not\r\nonly those of you with youth and hope strong in your veins, but those\r\nwith pathetic streaks of grey in your hair, large families to support,\r\nand with half a century sitting upon your work-laden shoulders. I have\r\nseen you struggle uncomplainingly against flood, fire, disease in stock,\r\npests, drought, trade depression, and sickness, and yet have time to\r\nextend your hands and hearts in true sympathy to a brother in\r\nmisfortune, and spirits to laugh and joke and be cheerful.\r\n\r\nAnd for my sisters a great love and pity fills my heart. Daughters of\r\ntoil, who scrub and wash and mend and cook, who are dressmakers,\r\npaperhangers, milkmaids, gardeners, and candle-makers all in one, and\r\nyet have time to be cheerful and tasty in your homes, and make the best\r\nof the few oases to be found along the narrow dusty track of your\r\nexistence. Would that I were more worthy to be one of you--more a\r\ntypical Australian peasant--cheerful, honest, brave!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI love you, I love you. Bravely you jog along with the rope of class\r\ndistinction drawing closer, closer, tighter, tighter around you: a few\r\nmore generations and you will be as enslaved as were ever the moujiks of\r\nRussia. I see it and know it, but I cannot help you. My ineffective life\r\nwill be trod out in the same round of toil--I am only one of yourselves,\r\nI am only an unnecessary, little, bush commoner, I am only a--woman!\r\n\r\n       *       *       *       *       *\r\n\r\nThe great sun is sinking in the west, grinning and winking knowingly as\r\nhe goes, upon the starving stock and drought-smitten wastes of land.\r\nNearer he draws to the gum-tree scrubby horizon, turns the clouds to\r\norange, scarlet, silver flame, gold! Down, down he goes. The gorgeous,\r\ngarish splendour of sunset pageantry flames out; the long shadows\r\neagerly cover all; the kookaburras laugh their merry mocking good-night;\r\nthe clouds fade to turquoise, green, and grey; the stars peep shyly out;\r\nthe soft call of the mopoke arises in the gullies! With much love and\r\ngood wishes to all--Good night! Good-bye!\r\n\r\nAMEN\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n***END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK MY BRILLIANT CAREER***\r\n\r\n\r\n******* This file should be named 11620.txt or 11620.zip *******\r\n\r\n\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\nhttps://www.gutenberg.org/1/1/6/2/11620\r\n\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions\r\nwill be renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from public domain print editions means that no\r\none owns a United States copyright in these works, so the Foundation\r\n(and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United States without\r\npermission and without paying copyright royalties.
